6 min
The Necessity of Accidents and Refuting Deniers
Al-Nasafi proves accidents and refutes deniers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi considers denying accidents as rejecting self-evident truths and how this leads to the origination of the world.

Core Idea
Denying accidents is like denying certainty itself.

Al-Nasafi begins by addressing a crucial challenge: some thinkers reject the reality of accidents. He counters this by elevating their denial to the level of rejecting necessary knowledge. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for proving the world's origination.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Find where al-Nasafi states that denying accidents is like denying necessities. This is his core argument.
The passage directly equates jahd al-a'rad with jahd al-daruriyyat.
2
Understand the refutation of al-Asamm
Al-Nasafi specifically refutes al-Asamm, a known denier of accidents. Consider why this refutation is extensive.
3
Connect to the world's division
Notice that al-Nasafi concludes that every part of the world falls under accidents or entities, and both are originated.
This step ties the proof to the broader argument for the world's temporal origin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد هذا ينتقل إلى إثبات الأعراض مناقشا المنكرين، وعلى رأسهم الأصم ويكثر في رده، ويقول في النهاية: إن جحد الأعراض من قبيل جحد الضروريات – ثم يقول: لا يوجد للعالم قسم إلا وهو داخل تحت ما ذكر وهذا يعني حدوث الأعراض والأعيان. ويبدأ في بيان حد القديم فيقول : هو ما لا أول لوجوده، ويقرر أنه تعريف الأشعري، ويعرف المحدث بأنه ما لوجوده ابتداء.
Then after this he moves to proving accidents, discussing the deniers, foremost among them al-Asamm, and he is extensive in his refutation, and he says in the end: 'Denying accidents is like denying necessities.' Then he says: 'There is no division of the world except that it is included under what was mentioned, and this entails the origination of accidents and entities.' And he begins to explain the definition of the eternal, saying: 'It is that which has no beginning of its existence,' and he affirms that it is al-Ash'ari's definition, and he defines the originated as that which has a beginning of its existence.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage first emphasizes the necessity of accepting accidents. Al-Nasafi refutes the deniers, especially al-Asamm, and declares that denying accidents is tantamount to denying self-evident truths. He then concludes that everything in the world falls under the categories mentioned (accidents and entities), and therefore both accidents and entities are originated (haditha). Next, he defines two key terms: 'qadim' (eternal) as that which has no beginning of existence, attributing this definition to al-Ash'ari, and 'muhdath' (originated) as that which has a beginning. These definitions are foundational for proofs of God's existence and the world's origination.

Key Terms
الأعراض
الأصم
الضروريات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some thinkers, like al-Asamm, deny the reality of accidents because they seem impermanent or non-substantial.
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that denying accidents is as absurd as denying self-evident truths; accidents are undeniable features of the world.

Checkpoint
What does al-Nasafi equate the denial of accidents with?
A) Denying God's existence
B) Denying self-evident truths
C) Denying the afterlife
D) Denying prophethood

Key Takeaway
Accidents are so evident that rejecting them is like denying what is self-evident, and their origination entails the world's temporality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself rationalizing away a clear truth about your own existence—like your need for God—are you not behaving like al-Asamm, denying what is self-evident? What necessity are you currently resisting? Will you submit to it today?
?
If the very fabric of the world—accidents and entities—is originated and dependent, what does that demand from you now? Not tomorrow, but this moment. What would it mean to live as if everything around you is a sign of its Creator?
?
Al-Nasafi calls the denial of accidents a denial of necessities. Is there any area of your belief where you have allowed intellectual comfort to override what is plainly true? Will you repent from that pride today and embrace the truth, however uncomfortable?

Defining the Eternal and the Originated
Al-Nasafi defines 'qadim' and 'muhdath' and attributes the definition to al-Ash'ari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of 'eternal' (qadim) and 'originated' (muhdath) as presented by al-Nasafi and their importance for theology.

Core Idea
Eternal means no beginning; originated means a beginning.

After establishing the world's origination, al-Nasafi clarifies two foundational terms: the eternal and the originated. These definitions are essential for discussing God's attributes and the world's origin. This lesson explains these definitions and their attribution to al-Ash'ari.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of 'qadim'
Al-Nasafi defines 'qadim' as that which has no beginning of existence. Note the precision: it is about the beginning, not the end.
He attributes this definition to al-Ash'ari.
2
Read the definition of 'muhdath'
Then he defines 'muhdath' as that which has a beginning of existence. These two definitions are opposites.
Together they cover everything: either eternal or originated.
3
Reflect on why these definitions matter
Consider how these definitions are used in proofs for God's existence and the world's creation. They provide a clear logical framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد هذا ينتقل إلى إثبات الأعراض مناقشا المنكرين، وعلى رأسهم الأصم ويكثر في رده، ويقول في النهاية: إن جحد الأعراض من قبيل جحد الضروريات – ثم يقول: لا يوجد للعالم قسم إلا وهو داخل تحت ما ذكر وهذا يعني حدوث الأعراض والأعيان. ويبدأ في بيان حد القديم فيقول : هو ما لا أول لوجوده، ويقرر أنه تعريف الأشعري، ويعرف المحدث بأنه ما لوجوده ابتداء.
Then after this he moves to proving accidents, discussing the deniers, foremost among them al-Asamm, and he is extensive in his refutation, and he says in the end: 'Denying accidents is like denying necessities.' Then he says: 'There is no division of the world except that it is included under what was mentioned, and this entails the origination of accidents and entities.' And he begins to explain the definition of the eternal, saying: 'It is that which has no beginning of its existence,' and he affirms that it is al-Ash'ari's definition, and he defines the originated as that which has a beginning of its existence.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage first emphasizes the necessity of accepting accidents. Al-Nasafi refutes the deniers, especially al-Asamm, and declares that denying accidents is tantamount to denying self-evident truths. He then concludes that everything in the world falls under the categories mentioned (accidents and entities), and therefore both accidents and entities are originated (haditha). Next, he defines two key terms: 'qadim' (eternal) as that which has no beginning of existence, attributing this definition to al-Ash'ari, and 'muhdath' (originated) as that which has a beginning. These definitions are foundational for proofs of God's existence and the world's origination.

Key Terms
القديم
المحدث
الأشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'eternal' means 'without end' or 'existing forever in the future'.
Correction: In this context, 'qadim' specifically means 'without beginning', not 'without end'.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, how is the originated (muhdath) defined?
A) It has no beginning
B) It has no end
C) It has a beginning of existence
D) It is composed of accidents

Key Takeaway
The eternal has no beginning; the originated has a beginning – these definitions are the foundation for proving God's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that only the Eternal has no beginning. Examine your life: what do you rely on that has a beginning—your wealth, your youth, your health? Will they last forever? What would change if you truly anchored your heart in the Eternal alone?
?
Al-Ash'ari defined qadim as 'no beginning'. Do you sometimes behave as if something other than God has no beginning—like a habit, an ideology, or a worldview you treat as self-evident? What would it mean to honestly recognize that everything besides God is muhdath?
?
This passage sharply distinguishes between eternal and originated. Are your daily efforts invested more in what will perish (muhdath) or in the One who is truly qadim? Be specific: what is one thing you will change today to prioritize the Eternal?

Three Views on the Origin of the World
Al-Nasafi categorizes three groups on the world's origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three positions on the world's origin (fully originated, fully eternal, or mixed) and understand why Islamic theology affirms full origination.

Core Idea
Ahl al-Haqq affirm the world's full origination; all other views compromise divine sovereignty.

Imam al-Nasafi opens his discussion by mapping out the landscape of beliefs about the world's origin. This taxonomy is essential because every theological argument about God's existence and attributes depends on whether the world is created or eternal. The passage introduces three camps and signals the refutation of errors to follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the three groups
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Focus on the three categories: Ahl al-Haqq (people of truth), the Azaliyya (eternalists), and the third group (philosophers and hylists).
Notice how al-Nasafi uses concise terms like 'tina' (matter) and 'sina'a' (craftsmanship/form).
2
Identify the key difference between the groups
The groups differ on whether 'tina' (matter) and 'sina'a' (form) are eternal or originated. Ahl al-Haqq say both are originated; Azaliyya say both are eternal; the third group says matter is eternal but form is originated.
3
Examine the elaboration on the second and third views
The passage then spends time on the views that claim some kind of eternity—either full eternity or eternal matter. Note the mention of philosophers and hyle (prime matter).
The cause-effect argument from some philosophers is also mentioned: the world as effect co-eternal with its cause.
4
Understand the refutation strategy
Al-Nasafi states he will prove the origination of the entire world, including accidents, which is the standard kalam argument. This sets up the refutation of all forms of eternalism.
The refutation method is hinted: proving origination of accidents leads to the temporality of substances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقسم الناس بعد ذلك إلى أقسام ثلاثة: أ- أهل الحق ويقولون : إن العالم بجواهره وأعراضه حديث الطينة، والصنعة. ب لم يزلية ويقولون: بقدم الطينة والصنعة وهو لم يزل. وقسم أخير يقول : العالم قديم الطينة حديث الصنعة، ومنهم بعض الفلاسفة وأصحاب الهيولي. ويعرض بإسهاب لرأي من يقول بقدم العالم مادة، وصنعة، ولرأي من يقول: إن العالم مصنوع إلا أنه قديم بقدم صانعه لأن صانعه علة صنعه، والمعلول لا يفارق العلة، ولا يتأخر عنها. وبعد ذلك يعرض رأي من يقول : إن العالم حادث إلا أنه من أصل قديم له مثل الهيولى أو الاسطقسات أو الطبائع أو غير ذلك. ثم بعد ذلك يقول : إن هناك فروعا كثيرة لا داعي لذكرها، لأنه كان شرط على نفسه الإيجاز. ويناقش بعد ذلك قضية حدوث العالم بجميع أجزائه، لينتهي إلى إثبات الحدوث، ويبطل مذاهب من قالوا بالقدم من خلال مناقشة طويلة. وهو كأي متكلم يتحدث عن حدوث الأعراض، ويناقش المخالفين ليبطل مزاعمهم، وينتهي إلى إثبات حدوث الأعراض.
Then he divides people after that into three sections: A) The people of truth, and they say: The world, with its substances and accidents, is originated in both matter and craftsmanship. B) The Azaliyya (eternalists), and they say: Matter and craftsmanship are eternal, and it is unceasing. C) And a final section says: The world is eternal in matter, originated in craftsmanship; among them are some philosophers and the proponents of hyle. And he extensively presents the opinion of those who say the world is eternal in matter and craftsmanship, and the opinion of those who say: The world is made, but it is eternal due to the eternity of its maker, because its maker is the cause of its making, and the effect does not separate from the cause, nor does it lag behind it. And after that he presents the opinion of those who say: The world is originated, but from an eternal origin, like hyle, elements, natures, or other things. Then after that he says: There are many branches that need not be mentioned, because he placed upon himself the condition of brevity. And after that he discusses the issue of the origination of the world in all its parts, concluding to proving origination, and he invalidates the doctrines of those who said eternity through a lengthy discussion. And he, like any mutakallim, speaks about the origination of accidents, and discusses the dissenters to invalidate their claims, and he ends with proving the origination of accidents.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents a taxonomy of views on the origin of the world. First, Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth) affirm that the world is entirely originated—both its matter (tina) and its craftsmanship (sina'a). Second, the Azaliyya claim that both matter and craftsmanship are eternal. Third, a group including some philosophers and hylists claims that matter is eternal but craftsmanship (form) is originated. Al-Nasafi then elaborates on the second view, including the argument that the world is co-eternal with its cause (God). He also refutes the view that the world is originated from an eternal substratum (hyle, elements). He concludes by asserting that he will prove the origination of the entire world through a discussion of accidents, which is the standard kalam proof for the world's temporality.

Key Terms
أهل الحق
لم يزلية
الطينة
الصنعة
الفلاسفة
الهيولي
الحدوث
القدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'eternal' (qadim) can apply to the world in any sense—whether in matter or form—without contradicting Islamic theology.
Correction: Ahl al-Haqq affirm that both matter and form are originated. Any claim of eternity (even partial) for the world diminishes Allah's sole pre-existence and creative power.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the Azaliyya believe about the world?
A) The world is originated in both matter and form
B) The world is eternal in matter and originated in form
C) Both matter and craftsmanship are eternal
D) The world is created from nothing

Key Takeaway
The world, in both its matter and form, is originated (hadith) according to Ahl al-Haqq, and this is the foundation of tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before studying this lesson, which of the three views might you have unconsciously held? Does your daily life reflect a deep conviction that everything—including your very existence—is brought into being by Allah at every moment?
?
The philosophers claimed the world is a necessary effect of God, like light from the sun. Are there blessings in your life (health, wealth, relationships) that you treat as automatic effects rather than as fresh gifts from Allah's free will? How can you cultivate a constant awareness of their origination?
?
If the world had always existed, would you feel less dependent on Allah? How does affirming the world's total origination (matter and form) transform your sense of gratitude and reliance on Him right now?

The Logical Necessity of Capacity Accompanying the Act
Nasafi refutes Mu'tazila on capacity precedence using cause-effect logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that capacity must be simultaneous with the act, based on the principle that a cause cannot exist without its effect.

Core Idea
A cause cannot precede its effect — capacity must accompany action.

This lesson introduces the logical foundation of the Maturidi position on human capacity. Nasafi begins by stating the views of Abu Hashim and the Mu'tazila, then rejects them on the basis of the necessary relationship between cause and effect. The key principle is that if capacity is a cause of action, it cannot exist without that action, just as fire cannot be fire without heat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views refuted
Read the passage and note the two positions on capacity being devoid of action: Abu Hashim says forever, the Mu'tazila say for one moment. Understand these as the targets of refutation.
These views are considered false by Nasafi.
2
Understand the cause-effect principle
The passage states that effects must be concomitant with their causes. Reflect on why a cause existing without its effect is logically impossible.
This principle is central to Islamic theology and philosophy.
3
Apply the principle to capacity
If capacity is a cause of action, it cannot precede the action. Conclude that the Mu'tazila view violates this principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأبو هاشم يقول بخلوها عنه أبد الدهر ، أما أهل نحلته من المعتزلة فيقولون بخلوها عنه زمانا واحدا، وكل ذلك باطل، ويقول النسفي: إن المحققين من أئمته أبوا تقدم الاستطاعة على الأفعال، لما فيه من القول بخلو العلة عن المعلول زمانا، وكذا قالوا باقتران المعلولات بعللها ، ولم يجوزوا تقدم العلل عليها. ذلك إلى وقد اجتمع أهل القبلة على سؤال الله المعونة والتوفيق في كل لحظة، ولو كانت قدرة الطاعة معطاة للإنسان قبل ذلك لم يكن للطلب معنى، وأدى بقاء العرض. ويبين أدلته بالتفصيل على وجوب مقارنة الاستطاعة للفعل، وليس قبله، ويقول : إن القول بتقدم القدرة على الفعل يوجب استغناء العبد عن الله وقت الفعل، والقول به كفر، وقد تعرض الشيخ أبو منصور لهذا في حديث طويل ملخصه أن إعطاء القدرة قبل الفعل خروج عن الحكمة، ولا يليق ذلك بحكمة الله. وقد تعلق المعتزلة بآيات تثبت سبق القدرة للفعل، ويقول النسفي في ذاك: إن المقصود بهذه الآيات الاستطاعة الأولى التي هي سلامة الأسباب والآلات وهو متفق معهم في ذلك، أما ما عدا ذلك فلا.
And Abu Hashim says that it (capacity) is devoid of it (action) forever; as for the people of his sect from the Mu'tazila, they say it is devoid of it for one moment, and all that is false. And al-Nasafi says: Indeed the verifiers from his (Abu Hashim's) imams refused the precedence of capacity over actions, because it involves saying that the cause is devoid of the effect for a moment; and likewise they said that effects are concomitant with their causes, and they did not permit causes to precede them. Moreover, the people of the qibla have agreed upon asking God for assistance and success (tawfiq) at every moment; if the power of obedience were given to man before that, the request would have no meaning, and it would entail the subsistence of the accident. And he explains his evidences in detail for the necessity of the concomitance of capacity with the act, and not before it, and he says: Indeed the saying that capacity precedes the act necessitates the servant's independence from God at the time of the act, and that saying is disbelief (kufr). And Shaykh Abu Mansur (al-Maturidi) addressed this in a lengthy speech, the summary of which is that giving capacity before the act is a departure from wisdom, and that does not befit the wisdom of God. And the Mu'tazila clung to verses that prove the precedence of capacity over the act, and al-Nasafi says concerning that: What is intended by these verses is the first capacity, which is the soundness of causes and instruments, and he agrees with them on that; as for other than that, no.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents a Sunni Maturidi refutation of the Mu'tazilite view that human capacity (istita'ah) can precede the act. Nasafi begins by reporting Abu Hashim's extreme view that capacity is devoid of action forever, and the more moderate Mu'tazilite view that it is devoid for one moment, then declares both false. He then states the position of the verifiers among the imams: they reject the precedence of capacity because it would imply a cause existing without its effect, which is invalid. They affirm that effects are concomitant with their causes. Nasafi strengthens this with a practical argument: all Muslims ask God for help and success (tawfiq) at every moment; if the power to obey were already given, such prayer would be meaningless. He further argues that pre-existence of capacity makes the servant independent of God at the time of action, which is kufr. He cites Abu Mansur al-Maturidi's summary that giving capacity before the act is against divine wisdom. Finally, he addresses Mu'tazilite proof texts from the Qur'an by distinguishing the 'first capacity' (soundness of causes and instruments) from the capacity for the act itself; the verses refer to the former, not the latter, so there is agreement on that point.

Key Terms
الاستطاعة
التقدم
المقارنة
العلة والمعلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What principle do the verifiers use to reject the precedence of capacity over action?
A) Capacity is an accident and cannot subsist.
B) A cause cannot be devoid of its effect.
C) Action is created by God directly.
D) Capacity is given only at the moment of action.

Key Takeaway
The logical principle that cause and effect must coexist refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that capacity can precede action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you told yourself you have the ability to do a good deed later, but then never did it? How does this lesson's truth that capacity must accompany action expose that excuse as hollow?
?
If your capacity to act is always tied to the moment of action, what does that mean for the times you claim you 'could have' but didn't? Are you now accountable for not acting when the capacity was there?
?
This lesson demolishes the idea that you can have a potential that lies idle. In what area of your faith are you waiting for a future time to act, thinking your capacity exists now but the action doesn't? What will you do about it today?

The Practical Evidence: Prayer for Divine Assistance
Nasafi uses the practice of prayer to argue capacity must be simultaneous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that the universal practice of asking God for help at every moment proves that capacity cannot precede the act.

Core Idea
If we already had capacity, why would we ask God for it?

In this lesson, Nasafi presents a simple yet powerful argument from religious practice. All Muslims, at every moment, ask Allah for tawfiq (success) and assistance. If the capacity to obey were already given before the act, such prayer would be pointless. This communal agreement reinforces the logical argument from cause and effect.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the consensus of Muslims
The passage mentions that all people of the qibla (Muslims) ask God for assistance and tawfiq at every moment. This is a practical consensus (ijma') that reflects the need for divine help.
This is not a formal legal consensus but a universal practice.
2
Understand the implication
If capacity were already present before the act, the request for help would be meaningless. Nasafi uses this to show that capacity must be concurrent with the act.
The argument also implies that without capacity, the act cannot occur.
3
Connect to the subsistence of accident
The passage adds that pre-existing capacity would entail the subsistence of the accident (capacity as an accident persisting without action). This is an additional technical point.
Accidents in kalam do not subsist for two moments; this would be a violation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأبو هاشم يقول بخلوها عنه أبد الدهر ، أما أهل نحلته من المعتزلة فيقولون بخلوها عنه زمانا واحدا، وكل ذلك باطل، ويقول النسفي: إن المحققين من أئمته أبوا تقدم الاستطاعة على الأفعال، لما فيه من القول بخلو العلة عن المعلول زمانا، وكذا قالوا باقتران المعلولات بعللها ، ولم يجوزوا تقدم العلل عليها. ذلك إلى وقد اجتمع أهل القبلة على سؤال الله المعونة والتوفيق في كل لحظة، ولو كانت قدرة الطاعة معطاة للإنسان قبل ذلك لم يكن للطلب معنى، وأدى بقاء العرض. ويبين أدلته بالتفصيل على وجوب مقارنة الاستطاعة للفعل، وليس قبله، ويقول : إن القول بتقدم القدرة على الفعل يوجب استغناء العبد عن الله وقت الفعل، والقول به كفر، وقد تعرض الشيخ أبو منصور لهذا في حديث طويل ملخصه أن إعطاء القدرة قبل الفعل خروج عن الحكمة، ولا يليق ذلك بحكمة الله. وقد تعلق المعتزلة بآيات تثبت سبق القدرة للفعل، ويقول النسفي في ذاك: إن المقصود بهذه الآيات الاستطاعة الأولى التي هي سلامة الأسباب والآلات وهو متفق معهم في ذلك، أما ما عدا ذلك فلا.
And Abu Hashim says that it (capacity) is devoid of it (action) forever; as for the people of his sect from the Mu'tazila, they say it is devoid of it for one moment, and all that is false. And al-Nasafi says: Indeed the verifiers from his (Abu Hashim's) imams refused the precedence of capacity over actions, because it involves saying that the cause is devoid of the effect for a moment; and likewise they said that effects are concomitant with their causes, and they did not permit causes to precede them. Moreover, the people of the qibla have agreed upon asking God for assistance and success (tawfiq) at every moment; if the power of obedience were given to man before that, the request would have no meaning, and it would entail the subsistence of the accident. And he explains his evidences in detail for the necessity of the concomitance of capacity with the act, and not before it, and he says: Indeed the saying that capacity precedes the act necessitates the servant's independence from God at the time of the act, and that saying is disbelief (kufr). And Shaykh Abu Mansur (al-Maturidi) addressed this in a lengthy speech, the summary of which is that giving capacity before the act is a departure from wisdom, and that does not befit the wisdom of God. And the Mu'tazila clung to verses that prove the precedence of capacity over the act, and al-Nasafi says concerning that: What is intended by these verses is the first capacity, which is the soundness of causes and instruments, and he agrees with them on that; as for other than that, no.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents a Sunni Maturidi refutation of the Mu'tazilite view that human capacity (istita'ah) can precede the act. Nasafi begins by reporting Abu Hashim's extreme view that capacity is devoid of action forever, and the more moderate Mu'tazilite view that it is devoid for one moment, then declares both false. He then states the position of the verifiers among the imams: they reject the precedence of capacity because it would imply a cause existing without its effect, which is invalid. They affirm that effects are concomitant with their causes. Nasafi strengthens this with a practical argument: all Muslims ask God for help and success (tawfiq) at every moment; if the power to obey were already given, such prayer would be meaningless. He further argues that pre-existence of capacity makes the servant independent of God at the time of action, which is kufr. He cites Abu Mansur al-Maturidi's summary that giving capacity before the act is against divine wisdom. Finally, he addresses Mu'tazilite proof texts from the Qur'an by distinguishing the 'first capacity' (soundness of causes and instruments) from the capacity for the act itself; the verses refer to the former, not the latter, so there is agreement on that point.

Key Terms
المعونة
التوفيق
بقاء العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi say would happen if capacity were given before the act?
A) The act would be unnecessary.
B) The request for divine help would be meaningless.
C) The accident would not subsist.
D) The servant would become an associate of God.

Key Takeaway
The universal practice of asking God for help at every moment demonstrates that capacity must be given at the time of the act, not before.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for tawfiq in your daily prayers, do you truly feel that your ability to obey hangs on that moment's divine assistance, or do you treat it as a routine phrase?
?
Think back to a time you felt spiritually strong and self-reliant—did that sense of independence ever lead you to slacken in asking Allah for help?
?
If you genuinely believed that without Allah's assistance at every moment you cannot even say 'Bismillah', how would that change the way you begin each action, especially your acts of worship?

The Theological Danger: Preceding Capacity Implies Independence from God
Nasafi argues pre-existing capacity is kufr and resolves scriptural proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's claim that the pre-existence of capacity leads to the servant's independence from God at the time of action, which amounts to disbelief (kufr), and his resolution of conflicting scriptural evidence.

Core Idea
Capacity before action makes man independent of God — a grave error.

This lesson covers the most serious consequence of the Mu'tazilite view: it implies that at the moment of action, the servant does not need God. Nasafi declares this kufr. He also cites al-Maturidi's argument from divine wisdom and addresses the Mu'tazilite proof texts by distinguishing two meanings of capacity, showing that the Qur'anic verses refer only to the first capacity (soundness of means), not the capacity for the act itself.

Guided Path
1
Understand the kufr argument
Read the statement that pre-existing capacity necessitates the servant's independence from God at the time of action. Reflect on why this is considered kufr: it denies the need for divine causation at the moment of act.
This is a strong theological conclusion.
2
Consider al-Maturidi's wisdom argument
The passage quotes al-Maturidi's summary that giving capacity before the act is contrary to divine wisdom. Think about why it would be unwise for God to grant ability before the act.
It implies that God would give something unnecessary.
3
Examine the scriptural resolution
The Mu'tazila cite verses that seem to show capacity preceding action. Nasafi distinguishes between the 'first capacity' (soundness of causes and instruments) and the capacity for the act itself. The verses refer to the first, which is agreed upon.
This harmonizes the texts with the logical and practical arguments.

Refuting Generated Actions (Tawallud)
Al-Nasafi refutes generated actions using Divine creation principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Nasafi denies that generated actions (like pain after a strike) are acts of the servant, based on the principle that God is the sole creator of all actions.

Core Idea
The pain from a strike is created by Allah, not generated by the striker.

In this passage, Al-Nasafi applies the Maturidi principle of acquisition (kasb) to a specific case raised by the Mu'tazila: the so-called 'generated actions' (tawallud). The Mu'tazila claim that certain effects, like pain after a blow, are produced by the human agent. Al-Nasafi rejects this, reaffirming that only Allah creates all actions and events. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recall the foundational principle
Al-Nasafi begins by restating the core Maturidi belief: Allah alone is the creator of all actions of the servants; the servant is merely an acquirer (muktasib). Understand this principle before proceeding.
This principle is the basis for the entire argument.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
The Mu'tazila claim that when a man strikes an animal, the resulting pain is a 'generated action' produced by the man. Recognize this as the view being refuted.
3
Apply the principle to the example
Al-Nasafi applies the principle: since Allah creates all actions, the pain after the strike cannot be an act of the servant. It must be created directly by Allah.
Note that the text cuts off, but the logical conclusion is clear.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: if humans could generate actions, they would share in divine power. Al-Nasafi upholds God's exclusive creative power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إبطال القول بالتولد : سبق الكلام في أن الله سبحانه هو الخالق لأفعال العباد، والعبد ليس إلا مكتسبا، وعليه فإن ما يوجد من الألم في الحيوان عقيب ضرب الرجل إياه، وهو ما يسمى بالأفعال المتولدة ليس بفعل للعباد كما يقول المعتزلة ، وليست الأفعال المتولدة (م) - تبصرة الأدلة جـ ١ )
The discussion on refuting the statement on tawallud (generation): The discussion has preceded that Allah the Exalted is the Creator of the actions of the servants, and the servant is nothing but an acquirer (muktasib). Accordingly, what exists of pain in the animal following a man's striking it, which is called generated actions (al-afʿāl al-mutawallada), is not an act of the servants as the Mu'tazila claim. And the generated actions are not ... (marker).
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of refuting the Mu'tazila concept of 'generated actions' (tawallud). The author recalls the previous conclusion that God is the creator of all actions of servants, and the servant is only an acquirer (muktasib). He then applies this principle to a specific case: when a man strikes an animal and pain results, the Mu'tazila consider the resulting pain a 'generated action' produced by the man's action. The author denies this, stating that such generated actions are not acts of the servant. The text cuts off, but the argument is that since God creates all actions, the pain is created by God, not by the servant.

Misconception: Generated actions (like pain after a strike) are natural consequences produced by the striker's action.
Correction: Al-Nasafi asserts that such effects are not generated by the human; they are directly created by Allah, as He is the sole creator of all actions.

Key Takeaway

Al-Nasafi insists that the pain from a strike is not a human act but a divine creation. How will you remind yourself of this truth tomorrow?
?
If you accidentally cause harm to someone, do you feel the full weight of responsibility knowing that the harm itself is created by Allah? How does this Maturidi perspective transform your sense of accountability and your reliance on Allah’s mercy?

Refutation of Generated Actions (Tawallud)
Nasafi refutes the doctrine of tawallud by analyzing and rejecting four alternative causal accounts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the doctrine of generated actions (tawallud) is rejected in Maturidi theology, as it ascribes creative power to creatures or leads to denial of the Creator.

Core Idea
Generated actions would give creatures divine power or negate God's role as Creator.

The Mu'tazila argued that some actions, like pain from a strike, are generated naturally from human acts. Nasafi refutes this by showing it either forces God into necessity, creates partners for Him, or denies a Creator altogether. This passage lays out the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Recall the established position
Understand that Nasafi begins by restating the Maturidi view: God creates actions, servants only acquire them. This sets the foundation for rejecting generated actions.
2
Identify the erroneous views on generated actions
Find the four positions: (1) Mu'tazila say the servant generates; (2) al-Nazzam says God's nature necessitates; (3) al-Qalanisi says God's disposition necessitates; (4) Thumama says no agent at all.
Notice how each view assigns causality in a different way.
3
Understand why each view is rejected
Necessitation (ijab) implies compulsion on God (idtirar). The no-agent view leads to ta'til (divesting God of action), which closes the door to proving the Maker.
Ta'til is considered a form of atheism.
4
Grasp Nasafi's own proof
Nasafi argues that affirming generated actions means affirming ikhtira' (invention) to other than God, which is impossible. This would lead to shirk. The passage then promises refutation of Mu'tazili doubts.
The proof is simple: only God creates, so any 'generation' ascribes creation to creatures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إبطال القول بالتولد : سبق الكلام في أن الله سبحانه هو الخالق لأفعال العباد، والعبد ليس إلا مكتسبا، وعليه فإن ما يوجد من الألم في الحيوان عقيب ضرب الرجل إياه، وهو ما يسمى بالأفعال المتولدة ليس بفعل للعباد كما يقول المعتزلة ، وليست الأفعال المتولدة فعل الله تعالى بإيجاب الخلقة كما يقول النظام، أو بإيجاب الطبع كما يقول القلانسي وذلك لأن الإيجاب على الله قول باضطراره، وكذلك قول ثمامة بن الأشرس: إن المتولدات أفعال لا فاعل لها قول باطل، لأنه بذلك يسد على نفسه إثبات طريق الصانع حيث جوز بذلك خروج بعض الأفعال المحكمة المتقنة من العدم إلى الوجود من غير تخصيص، وهذا يعنى التعطيل المحض. ويقيم النسفي أدلته على إبطال القول بالتولد، وذلك لأن من يقول به يثبت الاختراع لغير الله، وهذا مستحيل، ثم يعرض لشبه المعتزلة ويفندها مثبتا أن الأفعال كلها مخلوقة لله، وأن القول بالتولد يؤدي إلى وجود شركاء الله تعالى الله عن ذلك.
Discussion on nullifying the doctrine of tawallud (generated actions): Preceding discussion established that God Almighty is the creator of the actions of servants, and the servant is only an acquirer. Accordingly, the pain that occurs in an animal following a man's striking it, which is called generated actions, is not an action of the servant as the Mu'tazila claim, nor are the generated actions an action of God Almighty by necessitation of nature (ijab al-khilqa) as al-Nazzam says, nor by necessitation of disposition (ijab al-tab') as al-Qalanisi says, because ascribing necessitation to God is a statement of coercion (idtirar). Likewise, the statement of Thumama ibn al-Ashras that generated actions are actions with no agent is false, because by that he closes the door to proving the existence of the Maker, since he thereby allows that some well-ordered, precise actions emerge from non-existence to existence without specification (takhsis), and this is pure divestiture (ta'til). Nasafi then presents his proofs for nullifying the doctrine of tawallud, because whoever holds it affirms invention (ikhtira') to other than God, and this is impossible. Then he presents the doubts of the Mu'tazila and refutes them, affirming that all actions are created by God, and that the doctrine of tawallud leads to the existence of partners to God, exalted is He above that.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazili concept of generated actions (tawallud) — the idea that an action of a person can naturally cause a subsequent effect without direct divine intervention. Nasafi begins by restating the established position: God alone creates actions; the servant only acquires. Therefore, the pain that results from a strike is not an action of the striker (as Mu'tazila claim) nor is it God's action by necessitation (as al-Nazzam's theory of nature or al-Qalanisi's theory of disposition would have it) because necessitation implies compulsion on God. He also rejects Thumama's view that generated actions have no agent at all, as that negates the need for a Creator and amounts to ta'til (divesting God of His attributes of action). Nasafi then gives his own proof: whoever affirms generated actions affirms creation (ikhtira') for other than God, which is impossible. He promises to refute the Mu'tazili doubts and concludes that the doctrine leads to shirk.

Key Terms
التولد
الافعال المتولدة
الاختراع
الايجاب
التعطيل
النظام
القلانسي
ثمامة بن الأشرس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think generated actions are a form of natural causality that does not require direct divine intervention.
Correction: Nasafi shows that any supposed generation either compels God (al-Nazzam, al-Qalanisi) or denies a cause (Thumama), both of which are false. Only God creates actions directly.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi reject the view that generated actions have no agent?
A) It implies fatalism.
B) It negates the existence of God by allowing well-ordered actions without a cause.
C) It denies free will.
D) It contradicts scripture.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of generated actions is rejected because it either compels God, denies His creation, or leads to atheism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience pain or hardship, do you instinctively attribute it to natural causes or to people? Stop and ask yourself: Do I truly believe that Allah alone created this pain and every detail of its occurrence? Commit to saying 'Allah created this' the next time difficulty strikes.
?
You often say 'this medicine cured me' or 'this hard work earned me success.' In your heart, do you ever feel that these means generate their own results? This lesson teaches that such thinking is a subtle shirk. Will you train your tongue to say 'Allah healed me' and 'Allah granted success' even when means are present?
?
Do you ever feel that your good deeds automatically produce reward or that your sins automatically produce punishment, as if by their own nature? The doctrine of tawallud is refuted precisely because it removes Allah's direct creation. Ask yourself: Is your reliance truly on Allah's creation of reward, or do you feel entitled? Repent and renew your tawhid.

Causality and Created Things
beginner
4 min
Refutation of the Theory of Generated Acts
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite theory of generated acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite theory of tawallud (generated acts) is rejected by Imam al-Nasafi and how it leads to atheism and polytheism.

Core Idea
The theory of tawallud either compels God or denies Him as Creator.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi confronts a dangerous Mu'tazilite idea—that some acts arise by 'generation' without a direct agent. He exposes the logical consequences: either God is forced to act (compulsion) or the universe produces perfect results without a Creator, which is pure atheism. Let's follow his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm, then read the literal translation. Notice the different views al-Nasafi refutes.
2
Identify the three views refuted
Find the views of al-Nazzam (necessitation of creation), al-Qalanisi (necessitation by nature), and Thumama (generated acts have no agent).
These are the targets of al-Nasafi's refutation.
3
Understand why each view is false
Al-Nasafi argues that necessitation implies compulsion on God, and Thumama's view closes the door to proving the Creator by allowing orderly acts without a specifier.
This leads to pure atheism (ta'til).
4
Note al-Nasafi's conclusion
Al-Nasafi affirms that all acts are created by Allah and that tawallud implies partners with Allah, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعل الله تعالى بإيجاب الخلقة كما يقول النظام، أو بإيجاب الطبع كما يقول القلانسي وذلك لأن الإيجاب على الله قول باضطراره، وكذلك قول ثمامة بن الأشرس: إن المتولدات أفعال لا فاعل لها قول باطل، لأنه بذلك يسد على نفسه إثبات طريق الصانع حيث جوز بذلك خروج بعض الأفعال المحكمة المتقنة من العدم إلى الوجود من غير تخصيص، وهذا يعنى التعطيل المحض. ويقيم النسفي أدلته على إبطال القول بالتولد، وذلك لأن من يقول به يثبت الاختراع لغير الله، وهذا مستحيل، ثم يعرض لشبه المعتزلة ويفندها مثبتا أن الأفعال كلها مخلوقة لله، وأن القول بالتولد يؤدي إلى وجود شركاء الله تعالى الله عن ذلك.
The act of God Almighty by necessitating creation as al-Nazzam says, or by necessitating nature as al-Qalanisi says, because necessitation upon God is a statement that necessitates Him. Likewise, the statement of Thumama ibn al-Ashras: 'Generated acts are acts with no agent' is a false statement, because by that he closes upon himself the proof of the way of the Creator, since he thereby permits the emergence of some precise and perfected acts from non-existence to existence without specification, and this means pure atheism. Al-Nasafi establishes his proofs for invalidating the statement on tawallud (generation), because whoever says it affirms invention for other than God, and this is impossible. Then he presents the doubts of the Mu'tazila and refutes them, affirming that all acts are created by God, and that the statement on tawallud leads to the existence of partners with God — exalted is God above that.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage refutes the theory of tawallud (generated acts) upheld by some Mu'tazila. It begins by rejecting the views of al-Nazzam, who claimed that God acts by necessitating creation, and al-Qalanisi, who claimed necessitation by nature, because necessitation implies compulsion on God, which is denied. Then it refutes Thumama's claim that generated acts have no agent, arguing that this closes the door to proving the existence of the Creator since it allows the emergence of perfectly ordered acts without a specifier, which amounts to pure atheism (ta'til). Al-Nasafi then states that the theory of tawallud is invalid because it attributes invention (ikhitira') to other than God, which is impossible, and leads to polytheism. The passage also notes that al-Nasafi presents and refutes the doubts of the Mu'tazila, affirming that all acts are created by God.

Misconception: That the theory of tawallud can explain the origin of acts without an agent.
Correction: It leads to atheism by allowing perfectly ordered acts to exist without a Creator, and also implies partners with Allah.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Nasafi consider Thumama's statement that 'generated acts have no agent' as false?
A) Because it implies that God is not the creator of acts.
B) Because it leads to denying the possibility of proving the Creator from orderly acts.
C) Because it contradicts the Quran.
D) Because it supports the view of al-Nazzam.

Key Takeaway
The theory of tawallud is false because it either compels God or denies His role as Creator, leading to atheism and polytheism.

?
The theory of tawallud subtly assigns creative power to other than Allah. Are there people, systems, or even your own efforts that you unconsciously credit as 'generators' of good in your life? Commit to reciting 'La hawla wala quwwata illa billah' daily to affirm that all power belongs to Allah.
?
This passage declares that any act attributed to other than Allah is 'pure atheism.' Do you truly believe in your heart that Allah is the sole Creator of every act, including your own? What would change in your daily duas and reliance if you lived this belief fully?

Why the Magian's Argument Fails: Intellect vs Desire
Nasafi refutes the Magian's flawed argument and defends the validity of rational inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Magian's argument from evil and why flawed reasoning does not invalidate rational inference as a whole.

Core Idea
A single false premise can corrupt an entire argument, but that does not discredit reason itself.

This passage presents a classic refutation of a dualist argument that uses the existence of evil to posit two creators. Nasafi carefully traces the Magian's reasoning to pinpoint the exact error: mistaking God's wisdom in creating evil as foolishness. He then defends the validity of rational inference against the objection that some reasoning is flawed.

Guided Path
1
Trace the Magian's reasoning
The passage lists several correct premises of the Magian and one false one. Follow each step to see where he goes wrong.
Notice that most of his premises are correct; only one is erroneous.
2
Identify the error
The Magian judged that creating evil is foolishness, which is an error. This led him to posit a second creator.
3
Understand the correct method
Nasafi explains that valid reasoning requires all premises to be based on intellect (aql), not desire (hawa). The Magian followed his desire.
4
Examine the defense of reasoning
Nasafi argues that errors in reasoning do not invalidate reason, just as errors in sense perception do not invalidate the senses. He gives examples of visual errors.
This is an important principle for kalam epistemology.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this argument defends the use of reason in theology and how it responds to skepticism about rational inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المجوسي نظر في أقسام العالم، فوجدها محدثة، فاعتقد حدوثها وهو صحيح، ثم وجد في العالم الشرور والقبايح والأقذار والأنتان فاعتقد ثبوتها وهو صحيح، ثم اعتقد حدوثها وهو أيضا صحيح ثم اعتقد أن المحدث لا بد له من المحدث وهو صحيح، ثم اعتقد أن صانع العالم حكيم وهو صحيح، ثم اعتقد أن إيجاد الشرور والقبائح والأقذار والأنتان سفه وهذا خطأ، واعتقد أنها لما كانت محدثة فلابد لها من محدث والصانع حكيم لا يسفه لم يخلق هذه الأشياء، ولابد لضرورة اقتضاء المحدثات محدثا أن يكون سوى الباري جل وعلا، صانع سفيه يتولى تخليق هذه الأشياء فوقع في الباطل لنظره في مقدمة واحدة بهواه دون عقله، ولو تأمل بعقله لعرف أن إيجاد هذه الأشياء حكمة على ما نبين إن شاء الله تعالى. فلم يقع في الباطل، والنظر يكون مقضيا إلى العلم بشريطة حصوله في جميع المقدمات بالعقل دون الهوى وثبت أن لا تناقض في قضايا العقل، ولا خلل في كون النظر مفضيا إلى العلم عند وجوده بشرطه ثم نقول : هذا الكلام نظر ، فيجب أن يكون باطلا، ثم يقال لهم : لو كان فساد ما فسد من النظر والاستدلال يوجب فساد كل نظر مع قيام الدليل على صحته، وثبوت القانون المميز بين صحيحه وفاسده، لكان ينبغي أن يخرج الخبر والحس من أسباب المعارف، لوجود الكذب في الخبر والغلط في الحس عند البعد إذ يرى كبير الجثة صغيرا عنده وكذا عند الآفة، إذ الأحول الشيء شيئين وحيث لم يبطل الخبر والحس لم يبطل العقل والنظر والله الموفق.
The Magian examined the divisions of the world, found them originated, and believed in their origination, which is correct. Then he found in the world evils, ugly things, filth, and stench, and believed in their existence, which is correct. Then he believed in their origination, which is also correct. Then he believed that the originated necessarily has an originator, which is correct. Then he believed that the Maker of the world is wise, which is correct. Then he believed that bringing into existence evils, ugly things, filth, and stench is foolishness, and this is an error. He believed that since they are originated, they must have an originator, and the Maker is wise and does not act foolishly, so He did not create these things. Necessarily, due to the requirement of originated things for an originator, there must be another besides the Blessed and Exalted Creator, a foolish originator who undertakes the creation of these things. So he fell into falsehood because of his consideration of a single premise with his desire (hawa) rather than his intellect ('aql). If he had reflected with his intellect, he would have known that bringing into existence these things is wisdom (hikma), as we will explain, God willing. So he would not have fallen into falsehood. And reasoning (nazar) leads to knowledge on condition that it occurs in all premises with the intellect, not desire. And it is established that there is no contradiction in the judgments of the intellect, nor any flaw in the fact that reasoning leads to knowledge when it exists with its condition. Then we say: This speech is reasoning, so it must be false. Then it is said to them: If the corruption that has corrupted some reasoning and inference necessitates the corruption of all reasoning, despite the existence of proof of its validity and the established criterion distinguishing between its sound and unsound, then it would be necessary to remove reports (khabar) and sense perception (hiss) from the causes of knowledge, because of the existence of lying in reports and error in sense perception at a distance when one sees a large body as small, and likewise due to defect when the one with squint sees one thing as two. And since reports and sense perception are not invalidated, intellect and reasoning are not invalidated. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Magian argument about the existence of evil. The author first traces the Magian's reasoning step by step, showing that each premise is correct except one: the belief that creating evil is foolishness. The author then points out that if the Magian had used his intellect properly, he would have seen wisdom in evil. The author then makes a general point: valid reasoning requires all premises to be based on intellect, not desire. He then defends reasoning itself: if some reasoning is flawed, it does not mean all reasoning is flawed, just as errors in reports or sense perception do not invalidate them entirely. He gives examples: a large body appears small at a distance, and a squinting person sees one thing as two, yet we do not reject sense perception entirely. Similarly, occasional errors in reasoning do not invalidate the faculty of reason. So the passage defends the validity of rational inference as a means of knowledge.

قانون مميز

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that because some rational arguments are flawed, all rational reasoning is unreliable.
Correction: Nasafi shows that just as occasional errors in sense perception do not negate the validity of sensory knowledge, occasional errors in reasoning do not invalidate rational inference when proper criteria are applied.

Checkpoint
Why did the Magian fall into error according to Nasafi?
A) Because he used his intellect correctly
B) Because he based his reasoning on a single false premise
C) Because he rejected all reasoning
D) Because he followed inspiration

Key Takeaway
Reasoning is a valid path to knowledge when based on intellect and proper criteria; occasional errors do not negate its overall reliability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you commit to seeking knowledge through proper reasoning, even when it is challenging?

Proof from Color Change for Accidents
Nasafi's main proof of accidents from color change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observable change of color in a substance demonstrates the existence and subsistence of accidents independent of the substance's essence.

Core Idea
Color change proves accidents exist because the essence stays while the color changes.

This passage presents a foundational proof in Islamic theology for the existence of accidents. By examining a common observable phenomenon—a black object turning white—the author shows that the change cannot be explained by the essence itself, requiring the postulation of transient accidents that inhere in the substance. This proof is a key move in establishing the reality of the empirical world and the framework for discussing divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the argument starts from a black object that becomes white while its essence remains unchanged.
The proof hinges on the persistence of the essence.
2
Identify the key inference
Why does the author conclude that blackness and whiteness must be accidents? Trace the reasoning: if the essence were the cause, it would have always been that color. Since it changed, the colors must be separate entities.
The author rejects both 'by essence' alternatives.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that this is a proof for the subsistence of accidents. Ask yourself how this argument serves as a foundation for later discussions on causality and divine attributes.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بكونه أسود قائم، ولا جائز أيضا أن يصير أبيض وذاته الذي هو علة كونه أسود موجود، وكذا إذا صار أبيض لذاته وإما أن صار أبيض لمعنى غير ذاته، ولا جائز أن صار أبيض لذاته لما أن هذا الذات كان موجودا، ولم يكن متصفا بكونه أبيض، وحيث صار أبيض مع بقاء الذات الذي كان أسود دل أنه ما صار أبيض لذاته ولا كان قبل هذا أسود لذاته بل كان أسود لمعنى انعدم والذات باق، وصار أبيض لمعنى حدث والذات غير حادث في هذه الحالة، فكان هذا دليلا على ثبوت الأعراض. ولا يقال إنه كان أسود لا لذاته ولا لمعنى، لأن هذا نفي، والنفي لا يوجب الاختصاص بأحد الوصفين فيقتضى هذا أن لا يكون كونه أسود حال كونه أسود أولى من كونه أبيض وهذا محال ولا يقال بأنه يكون أسود لعينه ولمعنى، لأنك إذا قلت: لعينه يوجب أن لا يكون لمعنى، وإذا قلت لمعنى يوجب أن لا يكون لعينه، إذ الحكم العقلي لا يثبت إلا بعلة واحدة فيصير في التقدير كأنك قلت: كان أسود لا لعينه ولا لمعنى، وقد بينا فساده، مع أن هذا اعتراف منك بثبوت معنى إلى هذا ندعوك . ولا يقال كان أسود لجعل جاعل لأن جعل الجاعل لا يخلو إما أن يكون لأجل جعله ذاته مقتضيا لكونه أسود أو لأجل جعله معنى مقتضيا لذلك، فآل الأمر إلى ما قلنا من كونه أسود لذاته أو لمعنى ولا يقال كان أسود لكونه على حال لأن تلك الحال إما أن تكون عائدة إلى الذات أو إلى ما ليس بعين الذات ولا واسطة بينهما، فآل الأمر إلى ما بينا فإن قالوا السواد إذا وجد في محل هل كان من الجائز بأن وجد في محل آخر على البدل؟ فإن قلتم: لا فقد تركتم أصلكم، لأن من قولكم جواز ذلك، وإن قلتم: كان ذلك جائزا قلنا: اختصاصه بهذا المحل على جواز اختصاصه بمحل آخر كان لذاته أم لمعنى؟ فإن قلتم: لذاته فقد ناقضتم، لأنكم قلتم بجواز وجود عين هذا الذات في غير هذا المحل. وإن قلتم لمعنى، فقد أثبتم للسواد معنى قائما به ووقعتم في المحال، لأن قيام العرض بالعرض غير جائز عند القائلين بثبوت الأعراض. وإن قلتم: اختص بهذا المحل مع جواز أن لا يكون مختصا به لا لذاته ولا لمعنى، فقد هدمتم دليلكم، وأبطلتم حجتكم. أجبنا عن ذلك: بأن كثيرا من المتكلمين قالوا إن السواد اختص بهذا المحل لعينه فلا يتصور وجوده إلا مختصا بهذا المحل حتى قال هؤلاء: إن الله تعالى أعاد هذا السواد بعد العدم لأعاده في عين هذا المحل لا في غيره، لاقتضاء عين السواد ذلك، فسقط هذا السؤال على هذا الأصل، وظهر الفرق لأن الأسود وجد بعد ذلك أبيض على ما قررنا وهذا يخالفه ومن المتكلمين من قال: إن السواد يقتضي لذاته محلا ما، وكذا كل عرض، فأما العرض لا يقتضي أن محله زيد أم لأنه إضافة وهي لا تعلل إنما الحكم هو الذي يعلل، ألا ترى أنا إذا قلنا: الأسود أسود بسواد لزم أن لا يوجد أسود إلا سواد، ولا يجوز أن يقال: هو عمرو
By its being black and standing, and it is also not possible that it becomes white while its essence which is the cause of its being black exists. Likewise, if it becomes white by its essence, or it becomes white by a meaning other than its essence. And it is not possible that it becomes white by its essence because this essence existed and was not described by whiteness. And since it became white with the persistence of the essence that was black, it indicates that it did not become white by its essence, nor was it before this black by its essence; rather, it was black by a meaning that ceased to exist while the essence remains, and it became white by a meaning that came into existence while the essence is not originated in this state. So this was a proof for the subsistence of accidents. And it is not said that it was black neither by its essence nor by a meaning, because this is a negation, and negation does not necessitate specification by one of the two descriptions; thus this would require that its being black while it is black is not more appropriate than its being white, and this is impossible. And it is not said that it is black by its own essence and by a meaning, because if you say 'by its essence', it necessitates that it is not by a meaning, and if you say 'by a meaning', it necessitates that it is not by its essence, since a rational judgment is only established by one cause; so in estimation it becomes as if you said: 'it was black neither by its essence nor by a meaning', and we have already shown its corruption, and moreover this is an admission from you of the subsistence of a meaning to which we call you. And it is not said that it was black by the making of a maker, because the making of a maker is not free from either being due to his making its essence necessitating its being black, or due to his making a meaning necessitating that; so the matter reduces to what we said of its being black by its essence or by a meaning. And it is not said that it was black by being in a state, because that state is either referable to the essence or to what is not the essence, and there is no intermediate; so the matter reduces to what we have explained. Then if they say: 'Blackness, when it exists in a locus, is it possible that it exists in another locus alternatively?' If you say no, you have abandoned your principle, because your statement implies its possibility. And if you say it was possible, we say: its specification to this locus given the possibility of its specification to another locus — is it by its essence or by a meaning? If you say by its essence, you have contradicted yourselves, because you said the existence of this same essence in another locus is possible. And if you say by a meaning, you have affirmed for blackness a meaning subsisting in it and have fallen into impossibility, because the subsistence of an accident in an accident is not permissible according to those who affirm the subsistence of accidents. And if you say: it is specified to this locus with the possibility that it is not specified to it, neither by its essence nor by a meaning, you have demolished your proof and invalidated your argument. We answered this: that many of the mutakallimun said that blackness is specified to this locus by its own essence, so its existence is not conceivable except specified to this locus, such that these said that if Allah (exalted) were to restore this blackness after non-existence, He would restore it in this very locus and not in another, due to the requirement of the essence of blackness. So this question falls on this principle, and the difference appears because the black object afterwards became white as we have established, and this is contrary to that. And among the mutakallimun are those who said: blackness by its essence requires some locus, and likewise every accident. As for the accident, it does not require that its locus be Zayd or 'Amr, because it is an attribution, and attribution is not caused; rather, the judgment is what is caused. Do you not see that if we say: 'the black thing is black by blackness', it follows that nothing black exists except blackness, and it is not permissible to say: 'it is 'Amr'?
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of accidents (a'rād) based on the observable change of color in a substance. The argument begins by considering an object that is black and then becomes white while its underlying essence (dhāt) remains. The author argues that the change cannot be due to the essence itself, because the essence existed before without whiteness. Therefore, the blackness must have been an accident that ceased to exist, and the whiteness is a new accident that came into existence. This demonstrates that accidents subsist in substances. The text then refutes several objections: (1) That the blackness could be neither by essence nor by a meaning – this is rejected because it would imply that being black is no more appropriate than being white, which is absurd. (2) That it could be both by essence and by meaning – rejected because a single effect cannot have two causes. (3) That it could be by the making of a maker – this reduces to either essence or meaning. (4) That it could be by being in a state – again reduces to essence or non-essence. The objection about blackness being located in a specific locus is then addressed with two answers: First, many mutakallimun hold that blackness is specified to its locus by its own essence, so it cannot exist elsewhere. Second, others hold that blackness requires a locus but not a specific one, and the specification comes from a cause. The conclusion is that the argument for accidents stands.

Key Terms
عرض (arad)
ذات (dhat)
معنى (ma'na)
سواد (sawad)
بياض (bayad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the proof relies on the impossibility of the substance changing its color, but the proof actually relies on the persistence of the substance.
Correction: The substance does change color, but the proof uses the fact that the essence remains unchanged to infer that the color must be an accident.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why can't blackness be essential to the object?
A) Because blackness is not a real property.
B) Because the object existed without whiteness before it turned white.
C) Because the essence is the cause of blackness.
D) Because blackness and whiteness are the same.

Key Takeaway
The persistence of a substance through color change proves that colors are accidents that subsist in the substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Will you let this lesson free you from identifying with fleeting states, and instead anchor your identity in what is unchanging?
?
The passage argues that change in accidents points to a deeper reality. Reflect on your own life: when you undergo a major change—repentance, growth, failure—do you believe your core self can remain the same? What would it mean to hold onto your essence while letting go of what is merely accidental? How can you actively distinguish between what is core to you and what is temporary?

Refuting the 'Neither by Essence Nor Meaning' Objection
Nasafi's refutation of the 'neither by essence nor meaning' objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that an accident is neither by essence nor by meaning leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
Denying both essence and meaning leaves no cause for specificity, which is impossible.

Having established that color change proves accidents, the author anticipates an objection: what if blackness is neither by essence nor by any meaning? In this lesson, we examine how the author refutes this by showing that such a negation would imply that being black is no more appropriate than being white, which is absurd.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its refutation
Read the short Arabic and translation. The objector says blackness is neither by essence nor by meaning. The author responds by pointing out that this negation fails to explain why the object is black rather than white.
The key term is 'negation' (nafy).
2
Understand the logical consequence
The author argues that if there is no cause (essence or meaning), then the object has no reason to be black over white, which is impossible because we observe it is black.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Connect to the proof
Recall that the earlier proof established that color change is due to accidents. This objection would undermine that proof, so refuting it is essential to uphold the existence of accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بكونه أسود قائم، ولا جائز أيضا أن يصير أبيض وذاته الذي هو علة كونه أسود موجود، وكذا إذا صار أبيض لذاته وإما أن صار أبيض لمعنى غير ذاته، ولا جائز أن صار أبيض لذاته لما أن هذا الذات كان موجودا، ولم يكن متصفا بكونه أبيض، وحيث صار أبيض مع بقاء الذات الذي كان أسود دل أنه ما صار أبيض لذاته ولا كان قبل هذا أسود لذاته بل كان أسود لمعنى انعدم والذات باق، وصار أبيض لمعنى حدث والذات غير حادث في هذه الحالة، فكان هذا دليلا على ثبوت الأعراض. ولا يقال إنه كان أسود لا لذاته ولا لمعنى، لأن هذا نفي، والنفي لا يوجب الاختصاص بأحد الوصفين فيقتضى هذا أن لا يكون كونه أسود حال كونه أسود أولى من كونه أبيض وهذا محال ولا يقال بأنه يكون أسود لعينه ولمعنى، لأنك إذا قلت: لعينه يوجب أن لا يكون لمعنى، وإذا قلت لمعنى يوجب أن لا يكون لعينه، إذ الحكم العقلي لا يثبت إلا بعلة واحدة فيصير في التقدير كأنك قلت: كان أسود لا لعينه ولا لمعنى، وقد بينا فساده، مع أن هذا اعتراف منك بثبوت معنى إلى هذا ندعوك . ولا يقال كان أسود لجعل جاعل لأن جعل الجاعل لا يخلو إما أن يكون لأجل جعله ذاته مقتضيا لكونه أسود أو لأجل جعله معنى مقتضيا لذلك، فآل الأمر إلى ما قلنا من كونه أسود لذاته أو لمعنى ولا يقال كان أسود لكونه على حال لأن تلك الحال إما أن تكون عائدة إلى الذات أو إلى ما ليس بعين الذات ولا واسطة بينهما، فآل الأمر إلى ما بينا فإن قالوا السواد إذا وجد في محل هل كان من الجائز بأن وجد في محل آخر على البدل؟ فإن قلتم: لا فقد تركتم أصلكم، لأن من قولكم جواز ذلك، وإن قلتم: كان ذلك جائزا قلنا: اختصاصه بهذا المحل على جواز اختصاصه بمحل آخر كان لذاته أم لمعنى؟ فإن قلتم: لذاته فقد ناقضتم، لأنكم قلتم بجواز وجود عين هذا الذات في غير هذا المحل. وإن قلتم لمعنى، فقد أثبتم للسواد معنى قائما به ووقعتم في المحال، لأن قيام العرض بالعرض غير جائز عند القائلين بثبوت الأعراض. وإن قلتم: اختص بهذا المحل مع جواز أن لا يكون مختصا به لا لذاته ولا لمعنى، فقد هدمتم دليلكم، وأبطلتم حجتكم. أجبنا عن ذلك: بأن كثيرا من المتكلمين قالوا إن السواد اختص بهذا المحل لعينه فلا يتصور وجوده إلا مختصا بهذا المحل حتى قال هؤلاء: إن الله تعالى أعاد هذا السواد بعد العدم لأعاده في عين هذا المحل لا في غيره، لاقتضاء عين السواد ذلك، فسقط هذا السؤال على هذا الأصل، وظهر الفرق لأن الأسود وجد بعد ذلك أبيض على ما قررنا وهذا يخالفه ومن المتكلمين من قال: إن السواد يقتضي لذاته محلا ما، وكذا كل عرض، فأما العرض لا يقتضي أن محله زيد أم لأنه إضافة وهي لا تعلل إنما الحكم هو الذي يعلل، ألا ترى أنا إذا قلنا: الأسود أسود بسواد لزم أن لا يوجد أسود إلا سواد، ولا يجوز أن يقال: هو عمرو
By its being black and standing, and it is also not possible that it becomes white while its essence which is the cause of its being black exists. Likewise, if it becomes white by its essence, or it becomes white by a meaning other than its essence. And it is not possible that it becomes white by its essence because this essence existed and was not described by whiteness. And since it became white with the persistence of the essence that was black, it indicates that it did not become white by its essence, nor was it before this black by its essence; rather, it was black by a meaning that ceased to exist while the essence remains, and it became white by a meaning that came into existence while the essence is not originated in this state. So this was a proof for the subsistence of accidents. And it is not said that it was black neither by its essence nor by a meaning, because this is a negation, and negation does not necessitate specification by one of the two descriptions; thus this would require that its being black while it is black is not more appropriate than its being white, and this is impossible. And it is not said that it is black by its own essence and by a meaning, because if you say 'by its essence', it necessitates that it is not by a meaning, and if you say 'by a meaning', it necessitates that it is not by its essence, since a rational judgment is only established by one cause; so in estimation it becomes as if you said: 'it was black neither by its essence nor by a meaning', and we have already shown its corruption, and moreover this is an admission from you of the subsistence of a meaning to which we call you. And it is not said that it was black by the making of a maker, because the making of a maker is not free from either being due to his making its essence necessitating its being black, or due to his making a meaning necessitating that; so the matter reduces to what we said of its being black by its essence or by a meaning. And it is not said that it was black by being in a state, because that state is either referable to the essence or to what is not the essence, and there is no intermediate; so the matter reduces to what we have explained. Then if they say: 'Blackness, when it exists in a locus, is it possible that it exists in another locus alternatively?' If you say no, you have abandoned your principle, because your statement implies its possibility. And if you say it was possible, we say: its specification to this locus given the possibility of its specification to another locus — is it by its essence or by a meaning? If you say by its essence, you have contradicted yourselves, because you said the existence of this same essence in another locus is possible. And if you say by a meaning, you have affirmed for blackness a meaning subsisting in it and have fallen into impossibility, because the subsistence of an accident in an accident is not permissible according to those who affirm the subsistence of accidents. And if you say: it is specified to this locus with the possibility that it is not specified to it, neither by its essence nor by a meaning, you have demolished your proof and invalidated your argument. We answered this: that many of the mutakallimun said that blackness is specified to this locus by its own essence, so its existence is not conceivable except specified to this locus, such that these said that if Allah (exalted) were to restore this blackness after non-existence, He would restore it in this very locus and not in another, due to the requirement of the essence of blackness. So this question falls on this principle, and the difference appears because the black object afterwards became white as we have established, and this is contrary to that. And among the mutakallimun are those who said: blackness by its essence requires some locus, and likewise every accident. As for the accident, it does not require that its locus be Zayd or 'Amr, because it is an attribution, and attribution is not caused; rather, the judgment is what is caused. Do you not see that if we say: 'the black thing is black by blackness', it follows that nothing black exists except blackness, and it is not permissible to say: 'it is 'Amr'?
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of accidents (a'rād) based on the observable change of color in a substance. The argument begins by considering an object that is black and then becomes white while its underlying essence (dhāt) remains. The author argues that the change cannot be due to the essence itself, because the essence existed before without whiteness. Therefore, the blackness must have been an accident that ceased to exist, and the whiteness is a new accident that came into existence. This demonstrates that accidents subsist in substances. The text then refutes several objections: (1) That the blackness could be neither by essence nor by a meaning – this is rejected because it would imply that being black is no more appropriate than being white, which is absurd. (2) That it could be both by essence and by meaning – rejected because a single effect cannot have two causes. (3) That it could be by the making of a maker – this reduces to either essence or meaning. (4) That it could be by being in a state – again reduces to essence or non-essence. The objection about blackness being located in a specific locus is then addressed with two answers: First, many mutakallimun hold that blackness is specified to its locus by its own essence, so it cannot exist elsewhere. Second, others hold that blackness requires a locus but not a specific one, and the specification comes from a cause. The conclusion is that the argument for accidents stands.

Key Terms
ذات (dhat)
معنى (ma'na)
نفي (nafy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the logical problem with saying blackness is neither by essence nor by meaning?
A) It contradicts the earlier proof.
B) It implies being black is no more appropriate than being white.
C) It implies there is no such thing as blackness.
D) It is self-referential.

Key Takeaway
Negation alone cannot explain why a particular accident applies; a cause is needed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself saying 'I just am this way' about a persistent flaw, are you committing the same error as someone who says a thing is black without cause? What real cause might you be avoiding?
?
Can you identify a time you assumed something about another person's character without a clear basis? How does this lesson's logical argument expose the emptiness of such assumptions?
?
If every specific quality must have a cause, what does that demand of you when you reflect on your own habits and beliefs? Are you willing to trace them back to their real roots?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
Refuting 'Both by Essence and Meaning' and Related Objections
Nasafi's refutation of multiple objections that reduce to essence/meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an accident cannot be caused by both essence and a meaning, and how objections about a maker or a state reduce to the same dichotomy.

Core Idea
One effect cannot have two causes; objections reduce to essence or meaning.

The author now addresses a cluster of objections: that blackness is both by essence and by meaning, that it is by the making of a maker, or that it is by being in a state. In each case, the author shows that these proposals either contradict rational principles or collapse into the original two alternatives of essence or meaning, thereby reinforcing the proof for accidents.

Guided Path
1
Examine the 'both' objection
Read the first part: the objector says blackness is by both essence and meaning. The author argues that this is self-contradictory because a rational judgment can only have one cause.
The principle 'one effect, one cause' is central.
2
Analyze the 'maker' objection
The objector says a maker made the object black. The author reduces this to either the maker made the essence necessitate blackness or made a meaning that causes it – again, essence or meaning.
No third option is possible.
3
Analyze the 'state' objection
The objector says blackness is due to a state. The author points out that a state is either about the essence or not; again, it reduces.
This reinforces the dichotomy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a moral failure in yourself, do you tend to blame it on multiple sources—your nature, your environment, your upbringing—to avoid taking full responsibility? This lesson insists that a single effect has a single cause. What is the single root cause of your recurring sin? Will you admit it tonight to Allah?
?
The makers and states you rely on for your identity—your job, your relationships, your reputation—are they ultimately reducible to your essence or to something distinct? If they were stripped away, who would you be before Allah? Are you ready to answer that question honestly?
?
You claim to believe in one Creator, yet you often act as if created things have independent causal power over your happiness. This lesson shows that every cause resolves into either essence or meaning—neither of which ultimately belongs to creation. What created 'cause' are you clinging to as your source of peace? Will you release it now and attribute all to Allah alone?

Causality and Created Things
