The Locus Objection and Two Answers
Nasafi's answer to the locus objection with two positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection about blackness's specific locus is answered by two different schools of mutakallimun.

Core Idea
Blackness is either essentially tied to its locus or only requires a locus in general; both preserve the proof.

The final objection in this passage concerns the specificity of blackness to a particular locus (e.g., this piece of cloth). If blackness could exist elsewhere, why is it here? The author presents two answers from the mutakallimun: one holds that blackness is essentially specified to its locus, the other that it requires a locus but not a specific one. Both answers defend the existence of accidents.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part: the objector asks whether blackness, if it exists in a locus, could have existed in a different locus. This challenges the contingency of accidents.
The objection aims to undermine the proof by showing an absurdity.
2
Examine the first answer
The first answer (many mutakallimun) says blackness is essentially tied to its specific locus; it cannot exist elsewhere. This is a strong essentialist view.
This answer blocks the objection by denying the premise of alternative loci.
3
Examine the second answer
The second answer says blackness requires a locus in general, but not a specific one; the specification is due to an external cause (the judgment). This preserves the accident's contingency.
This answer accepts the possibility but explains it causally.
4
Connect to the main proof
Both answers show that the objection does not disprove accidents; they only explain why a particular blackness is in a particular place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ولا يقال بأنه يكون أسود لعينه ولمعنى، لأنك إذا قلت: لعينه يوجب أن لا يكون لمعنى، وإذا قلت لمعنى يوجب أن لا يكون لعينه، إذ الحكم العقلي لا يثبت إلا بعلة واحدة فيصير في التقدير كأنك قلت: كان أسود لا لعينه ولا لمعنى، وقد بينا فساده، مع أن هذا اعتراف منك بثبوت معنى إلى هذا ندعوك . ولا يقال كان أسود لجعل جاعل لأن جعل الجاعل لا يخلو إما أن يكون لأجل جعله ذاته مقتضيا لكونه أسود أو لأجل جعله معنى مقتضيا لذلك، فآل الأمر إلى ما قلنا من كونه أسود لذاته أو لمعنى ولا يقال كان أسود لكونه على حال لأن تلك الحال إما أن تكون عائدة إلى الذات أو إلى ما ليس بعين الذات ولا واسطة بينهما، فآل الأمر إلى ما بينا فإن قالوا السواد إذا وجد في محل هل كان من الجائز بأن وجد في محل آخر على البدل؟ فإن قلتم: لا فقد تركتم أصلكم، لأن من قولكم جواز ذلك، وإن قلتم: كان ذلك جائزا قلنا: اختصاصه بهذا المحل على جواز اختصاصه بمحل آخر كان لذاته أم لمعنى؟ فإن قلتم: لذاته فقد ناقضتم، لأنكم قلتم بجواز وجود عين هذا الذات في غير هذا المحل. وإن قلتم لمعنى، فقد أثبتم للسواد معنى قائما به ووقعتم في المحال، لأن قيام العرض بالعرض غير جائز عند القائلين بثبوت الأعراض. وإن قلتم: اختص بهذا المحل مع جواز أن لا يكون مختصا به لا لذاته ولا لمعنى، فقد هدمتم دليلكم، وأبطلتم حجتكم. أجبنا عن ذلك: بأن كثيرا من المتكلمين قالوا إن السواد اختص بهذا المحل لعينه فلا يتصور وجوده إلا مختصا بهذا المحل حتى قال هؤلاء: إن الله تعالى أعاد هذا السواد بعد العدم لأعاده في عين هذا المحل لا في غيره، لاقتضاء عين السواد ذلك، فسقط هذا السؤال على هذا الأصل، وظهر الفرق لأن الأسود وجد بعد ذلك أبيض على ما قررنا وهذا يخالفه ومن المتكلمين من قال: إن السواد يقتضي لذاته محلا ما، وكذا كل عرض، فأما العرض لا يقتضي أن محله زيد أم لأنه إضافة وهي لا تعلل إنما الحكم هو الذي يعلل، ألا ترى أنا إذا قلنا: الأسود أسود بسواد لزم أن لا يوجد أسود إلا سواد، ولا يجوز أن يقال: هو عمرو
By its being black and standing, and it is also not possible that it becomes white while its essence which is the cause of its being black exists. Likewise, if it becomes white by its essence, or it becomes white by a meaning other than its essence. And it is not possible that it becomes white by its essence because this essence existed and was not described by whiteness. And since it became white with the persistence of the essence that was black, it indicates that it did not become white by its essence, nor was it before this black by its essence; rather, it was black by a meaning that ceased to exist while the essence remains, and it became white by a meaning that came into existence while the essence is not originated in this state. So this was a proof for the subsistence of accidents. And it is not said that it was black neither by its essence nor by a meaning, because this is a negation, and negation does not necessitate specification by one of the two descriptions; thus this would require that its being black while it is black is not more appropriate than its being white, and this is impossible. And it is not said that it is black by its own essence and by a meaning, because if you say 'by its essence', it necessitates that it is not by a meaning, and if you say 'by a meaning', it necessitates that it is not by its essence, since a rational judgment is only established by one cause; so in estimation it becomes as if you said: 'it was black neither by its essence nor by a meaning', and we have already shown its corruption, and moreover this is an admission from you of the subsistence of a meaning to which we call you. And it is not said that it was black by the making of a maker, because the making of a maker is not free from either being due to his making its essence necessitating its being black, or due to his making a meaning necessitating that; so the matter reduces to what we said of its being black by its essence or by a meaning. And it is not said that it was black by being in a state, because that state is either referable to the essence or to what is not the essence, and there is no intermediate; so the matter reduces to what we have explained. Then if they say: 'Blackness, when it exists in a locus, is it possible that it exists in another locus alternatively?' If you say no, you have abandoned your principle, because your statement implies its possibility. And if you say it was possible, we say: its specification to this locus given the possibility of its specification to another locus — is it by its essence or by a meaning? If you say by its essence, you have contradicted yourselves, because you said the existence of this same essence in another locus is possible. And if you say by a meaning, you have affirmed for blackness a meaning subsisting in it and have fallen into impossibility, because the subsistence of an accident in an accident is not permissible according to those who affirm the subsistence of accidents. And if you say: it is specified to this locus with the possibility that it is not specified to it, neither by its essence nor by a meaning, you have demolished your proof and invalidated your argument. We answered this: that many of the mutakallimun said that blackness is specified to this locus by its own essence, so its existence is not conceivable except specified to this locus, such that these said that if Allah (exalted) were to restore this blackness after non-existence, He would restore it in this very locus and not in another, due to the requirement of the essence of blackness. So this question falls on this principle, and the difference appears because the black object afterwards became white as we have established, and this is contrary to that. And among the mutakallimun are those who said: blackness by its essence requires some locus, and likewise every accident. As for the accident, it does not require that its locus be Zayd or 'Amr, because it is an attribution, and attribution is not caused; rather, the judgment is what is caused. Do you not see that if we say: 'the black thing is black by blackness', it follows that nothing black exists except blackness, and it is not permissible to say: 'it is 'Amr'?
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of accidents (a'rād) based on the observable change of color in a substance. The argument begins by considering an object that is black and then becomes white while its underlying essence (dhāt) remains. The author argues that the change cannot be due to the essence itself, because the essence existed before without whiteness. Therefore, the blackness must have been an accident that ceased to exist, and the whiteness is a new accident that came into existence. This demonstrates that accidents subsist in substances. The text then refutes several objections: (1) That the blackness could be neither by essence nor by a meaning – this is rejected because it would imply that being black is no more appropriate than being white, which is absurd. (2) That it could be both by essence and by meaning – rejected because a single effect cannot have two causes. (3) That it could be by the making of a maker – this reduces to either essence or meaning. (4) That it could be by being in a state – again reduces to essence or non-essence. The objection about blackness being located in a specific locus is then addressed with two answers: First, many mutakallimun hold that blackness is specified to its locus by its own essence, so it cannot exist elsewhere. Second, others hold that blackness requires a locus but not a specific one, and the specification comes from a cause. The conclusion is that the argument for accidents stands.

Key Terms
محل (mahall)
سواد (sawad)
عرض (arad)
ذات (dhat)
إضافة (idafa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if blackness could have been elsewhere, then its presence here is arbitrary and undermines the proof for accidents.
Correction: The second answer shows that the specificity is due to God's creation, not to a flaw in the proof.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer to the locus objection, why is blackness in this specific locus?
A) Because God chose it arbitrarily.
B) Because blackness's essence requires that specific locus.
C) Because blackness is an accident of a substance.
D) Because the object is black.

Key Takeaway
The locus objection is resolved by either essential specificity or general requirement of a locus, both consistent with the existence of accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the blackness of your own hair or skin, do you recognize that its continuation in its specific locus is not automatic but depends on the ongoing judgment of Allah? How does this change your awareness of His constant sustenance of even the smallest attributes of your existence? What will you do today to remember this dependence?
?
The mutakallimun differed: some said blackness is essentially tied to its locus, others said it only needs any locus. In your own life, do you sometimes assume that your circumstances, abilities, or relationships are 'essentially' fixed and cannot change? How can you adopt the second view—that each specification is by divine judgment and therefore open to change by the same power? Will you pray for a change in your locus?
?
If every accident in your body requires a locus and is upheld by the Creator, how can you ever feel self-sufficient? What feature of your physical existence—your strength, beauty, health—are you most prone to forget is contingent on Allah's will every moment? Will you make a specific daily dhikr to combat this forgetfulness?

Five Arguments for the World's Co-Eternity with God
The author enumerates five rational arguments for the world's co-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand five rational arguments that some theologians and philosophers used to claim that the world is co-eternal with God, each derived from a divine attribute: causality, generosity, knowledge, wisdom, and power.

Core Idea
The world's eternity is argued not from itself, but from God's attributes.

In this passage, the author reports the reasoning of a group who, while affirming that the world has a Maker, argue that it must be eternal because of certain attributes of that Maker. They appeal to causality, generosity, knowledge, wisdom, and power. As you read, note that each argument starts from an attribute of God and concludes that the world cannot be temporally separate from Him. The author presents these views to later refute them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author’s rhythm come through.
Notice the repeated pattern: 'because of attribute X, therefore the world is eternal.'
2
Identify the five arguments
Go through each sentence and identify the attribute used (cause, generosity, knowledge, wisdom, power). Note how each conclusion follows from that attribute.
3
Understand the causal argument
The first argument: the Maker is the cause ('illa) of the world, so the effect (ma'lul) cannot be later or separate. This is a key philosophical claim.
Later kalam scholars reject the idea that cause and effect must be simultaneous.
4
Reflect on the pattern
All five arguments derive the world's eternity from a necessary attribute of God. Ask yourself: Is this a valid inference? The author will later critique it.
5
Anticipate refutations
The author will respond to each argument in subsequent sections. For now, just note the structure of each claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت طائفة أخرى منهم بل العالم مصنوع وله صانع لقيام دلالة كونه بغيره لا بنفسه غير أنه قديم لقدم صانعه لأن صانعه علة لكونه، والمعلول لا يفارق العلة ولا يتأخر عنه، وبعضهم قالوا سبب وجود العالم وجود البارئ، ووجوده أزلى لاستحالة البخل عليه في الأزل فيقتضي قدم جوده قدم ما يتعلق وجوده بجوده، وهؤلاء يقولون في تسبيحهم الله تعالى یا من جوده سبب وجود كل موجود. وبعضهم يقولون : كان الباري جل وعلا في الأزل عالما بالعالم موجودا لأنه علمه قديم لاستحالة الجهل عليه، وقدم علمه يقتضي قدم معلومه ومنهم من قال: القديم كان لم يزل حكيما، فاقتضى ذلك وجود حكمته وهو العالم في الأزل. وقال بعضهم: إن الله تعالى كان في الأزل قادرا على الفعل، ولا مانع له عن الفعل فلا معنى لتأخر فعله هذا هو مقالة القائلين بكون العالم بجميع أجزائه قديما.
And another group among them said: Rather the world is made and has a Maker, due to the evidence that it exists through another not through itself, except that it is eternal due to the eternity of its Maker, because its Maker is the cause of its existence, and the effect does not separate from the cause nor is it later than it. And some of them said: The cause of the existence of the world is the existence of the Creator, and His existence is eternal because stinginess is impossible for Him in pre-eternity, so the eternity of His generosity necessitates the eternity of that whose existence depends on His generosity. And these say in their glorification of God Almighty: 'O He whose generosity is the cause of the existence of every existent.' And some of them say: The Creator, exalted and majestic, was in pre-eternity knowing the world as existent, because His knowledge is eternal due to the impossibility of ignorance for Him, and the eternity of His knowledge necessitates the eternity of its known object. And among them is one who said: The Eternal has never ceased to be wise, so this required the existence of His wisdom, which is the world in pre-eternity. And some of them said: God Almighty was in pre-eternity capable of action, and there was no impediment preventing Him from action, so there is no meaning in the delay of His action. This is the statement of those who claim that the world with all its parts is eternal.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage enumerates five arguments used by a group (likely philosophers or certain rationalist theologians) to argue that the world, though dependent on a Maker, is eternal. The first argument is causal: since the Maker (God) is the cause ('illa) of the world's existence, and the effect (ma'lul) cannot be separate from or later than its cause, the world must be co-eternal with God. The second argument is based on God's generosity (jūd): because stinginess is impossible for God, His generosity is eternal, and it necessitates the eternal existence of whatever depends on that generosity—i.e., the world. They even have a liturgical phrase praising God's generosity as the cause of all existence. The third argument invokes God's eternal knowledge: since God has always known the world as existent (because ignorance is impossible), His eternal knowledge requires an eternal object of knowledge, so the world must be eternal. The fourth argument is from wisdom (ḥikma): God is eternally wise, and wisdom entails the existence of what is known as wise activity, which they identify with the world in pre-eternity. The fifth argument is from power (qudra): God has always been able to act, and there was no obstacle preventing action, so His action (i.e., creation of the world) could not have been delayed; hence the world is eternal. The passage concludes that these are the statements of those who claim the entire world is eternal.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
جود
حكمة
قدرة
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The arguments presented are accepted truths in the author's view.
Correction: The author is reporting the views of a group and will later refute them; they are not presented as the author's own position.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the arguments given in this passage for the world's eternity?
A) The effect cannot be separate from the cause.
B) God's eternal generosity necessitates an eternal object.
C) The world is necessary in itself.
D) God's power and lack of impediment necessitate immediate action.

Key Takeaway
Each of the five arguments derives the world's eternity from a necessary attribute of God: causality, generosity, knowledge, wisdom, or power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe God's attributes forced Him to create, does that mean God had no choice? How does that affect your trust in His freedom and mercy?
?
When you call upon God as 'O He whose generosity is the cause of every existent,' do you see creation as a necessary outpouring or a free gift? What difference does that perception make in your gratitude?
?
If God's power could have been exercised later, why do you think He created when He did? Examine your heart: do you assume that everything about God's actions must be necessary because He is perfect?

The Origination of Motion and Cessation of Rest
Nasafi proves rest ceases when motion arises, refuting transference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rational impossibility of coexisting rest and motion proves that rest ceases and motion occurs at the moment of change, and why the transference theory is invalid.

Core Idea
Rest and motion cannot coexist; so one must cease for the other to begin.

This passage from Imam Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla defends the doctrine that accidents like motion and rest are created directly and cannot transfer. The author presents a logical argument: if motion occurs where rest was, rest must have ceased—otherwise the body would have two contradictory attributes. He then refutes the objection that rest might have transferred elsewhere.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice the author's style of objection-and-response.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the argument is complete up to that point.
2
Identify the core argument
Find where Nasafi states that if rest did not cease when motion comes, the body would be both at rest and in motion simultaneously. Understand why that is impossible.
This uses the principle of non-contradiction.
3
Analyze the first objection and rebuttal
The objector denies that rest ceases and motion occurs. Nasafi's rebuttal shows the impossibility of combining rest and motion in one subject.
4
Understand the transference objection and its refutation
The second objection suggests rest and motion transfer between bodies. Nasafi argues that transferring rest would itself be motion, leading to absurdity.
This reinforces the idea that accidents do not migrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووراء هذا دلائل تدل على استحالة العدم على القديم تركنا ذكرها مخافة التطويل، فإن قيل : هذا إنما يستقيم أن لو ثبت عدم السكون وحدوث الحركة وهذا ممنوع قلنا لو لم ينعدم السكون وقد وجدت الحركة لكان الجسم ساكنا متحركا وكذلك لو كانت الحركة موجودة قبل هذا لكان الجسم ساكنا متحركا وهو محال، وكذا اجتماع السكون والحركة محال في محل واحد وكذا الجسم كان قبل هذا ساكنا وكون ما قامت به الحركة ساكنا محال وبعد ما صار متحركا لو كان السكون موجودا فيه لما صار متحركا، لأن كون ما قام به السكون متحركا محال، وإذا كان كذلك علم أن السكون قد انعدم وأن الحركة قد حدثت فإن قيل : ما أنكرتم أن السكون لم ينعدم بل انتقل إلى محل آخر، وإن الحركة لم تحدث بل انتقلت إلى هذا الجسم عن محل آخر ؟ قلنا هذا محال، لأن الانتقال من محل إلى محل السكون يكون حركة وقيام الحركة بالحركة محال، وكذا قيام الحركة بالسكون محال فلا يجوز انتقال الحركة ولا انتقال السكون من محل إلى محل على أنكم وإن ركبتم هذا.
And beyond this are proofs indicating the impossibility of non-existence for the eternal; we have omitted mentioning them for fear of length. If it is said: This is only sound if the non-existence of rest and the occurrence of motion are established, and this is denied. We say: If rest had not become non-existent and motion had come to exist, then the body would be both at rest and in motion. Likewise, if motion had existed before this, then the body would be both at rest and in motion, and that is impossible. Likewise, the conjunction of rest and motion in one locus is impossible. Likewise, the body was before this at rest, and that which is characterized by motion being at rest is impossible. And after it becomes in motion, if rest existed in it, it would not become in motion, because that which is characterized by rest being in motion is impossible. And if that is so, it is known that rest has become non-existent and that motion has occurred. Then if it is said: What prevents you from [saying] that rest did not become non-existent but rather transferred to another locus, and that motion did not occur but rather transferred to this body from another locus? We say: This is impossible, because the transfer of rest from locus to locus would be motion, and the subsistence of motion by motion is impossible, and likewise the subsistence of motion by rest is impossible. So the transfer of motion nor the transfer of rest from locus to locus is not permissible. Even if you adopt this...
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the proof of the origination of accidents. The author first mentions that there are other proofs for the impossibility of non-existence for the eternal, but omits them. Then an objector states that the entire proof depends on establishing that rest ceases and motion occurs, which he denies. The reply is a logical demonstration: if rest did not cease when motion comes, the body would simultaneously be at rest and in motion, which is impossible. Similarly, if motion had preexisted, the body would again be both at rest and in motion. The impossibility of combining rest and motion in one subject is the key. Therefore, rest must have ceased and motion newly occurred. A second objection claims that rest might have transferred to another locus and motion transferred from elsewhere. The author rejects this because transferring rest would itself be a kind of motion, and motion cannot subsist in motion, nor motion in rest. Thus transference is impossible. The passage ends with 'even if you adopt this'—the argument is cut off, but the refutation is clear.

Key Terms
عدم السكون
حدوث الحركة
سكون
حركة
محل
انتقال
اجتماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument depends on empirical observation of motion and rest.
Correction: The argument is based purely on the logical impossibility of contradictory attributes coexisting in one subject, not on empirical evidence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the idea that rest could transfer to another locus when motion arrives?
A) Because rest is not a substance and cannot transfer.
B) Because the transfer of rest would itself be a motion, leading to impossibility.
C) Because transfer would require the coexistence of motion and rest in the same locus.
D) Because rest is an accident and accidents do not transfer.

Key Takeaway
The logical impossibility of contradictory accidents in one substance proves that accidents are created directly and cannot transfer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every moment of stillness and every moment of movement in your life is a freshly created accident from Allah. How does this reality change the way you view your own habits, your rest, and your motion? Can you truly say you rely on Allah for every breath, or do you assume your own body has inherent power?
?
The argument shows that contradictory states cannot coexist in one substance. How about in your heart? Do you hold contradictory beliefs about Allah—professing tawheed while relying on created causes? What specific areas of your life reveal a contradiction between your words and your actual trust?
?
If accidents cannot transfer from place to place, then every change you experience is a direct act of creation. When you struggle to change a bad habit or to begin a good one, are you crying out to the Creator of motion and rest, or are you trying to transfer your own 'rest' into action by your own power? What will you do differently today to acknowledge His direct involvement in your every movement?

The Incoherence of Rest and Motion in One Body
Author answers objection that rest ceasing and motion occurring is not established.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rest must cease for motion to occur, and how this proves the origination of accidents.

Core Idea
Rest and motion cannot coexist; change requires ceasing of one accident and creation of another.

This passage presents a foundational kalam argument: if rest did not cease when motion begins, a body would be simultaneously at rest and in motion, which is impossible. The author uses this to demonstrate that rest must have ceased and motion must have newly occurred. This lesson examines that objection and its answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial objection
Read the objection: the opponent claims it is not established that rest ceases and motion occurs. Then read the author's answer.
Notice the opponent's starting point: they deny the premise.
2
Identify the contradiction
The author argues that if rest did not cease when motion occurs, the body would be both at rest and in motion simultaneously. Consider why this is impossible.
This is a direct contradiction because rest and motion are opposites.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that rest must have ceased and motion must have newly occurred. Relate this to the proof that accidents are created.
This shows that accidents are not eternal; they come into existence.

واجب الوجود لذاته لا لمعنى لما مر في المعنى الأول، والله الموفق. ووراء هذا دلائل تدل على استحالة العدم على القديم تركنا ذكرها مخافة التطويل، فإن قيل: هذا إنما يستقيم أن لو ثبت عدم السكون وحدوث الحركة وهذا ممنوع قلنا: لو لم ينعدم السكون وقد وجدت الحركة لكان الجسم ساكنا متحركا وكذلك لو كانت الحركة موجودة قبل هذا لكان الجسم ساكنا متحركا وهو محال، وكذا اجتماع السكون والحركة محال في محل واحد وكذا الجسم كان قبل هذا ساكنا وكون ما قامت به الحركة ساكنا محال وبعد ما صار متحركا لو كان السكون موجودا فيه لما صار متحركا، لأن كون ما قام به السكون متحركا محال، وإذا كان كذلك علم أن السكون قد انعدم وأن الحركة قد حدثت. فإن قيل: ما أنكرتم أن السكون لم ينعدم بل انتقل إلى محل آخر، وإن الحركة لم تحدث بل انتقلت إلى هذا الجسم عن محل آخر؟ قلنا: هذا محال، لأن الانتقال من محل إلى محل السكون يكون حركة وقيام الحركة بالحركة محال، وكذا قيام الحركة بالسكون محال فلا يجوز انتقال الحركة ولا انتقال السكون من محل إلى محل على أنكم وإن ركبتم هذا المحل وخرجتم عن المعقول لم ينفعكم هذا، لأنا نقول لكم إذا انتقلت الحركة من محل إلى محل آخر وصارت متحركة، وكذا السكون إذا انتقل وصار متحركا كانت هذه الحركة موجودة في الحركة والسكون وقت كونهما في المكان الأول أم حدثت؟ فإن قلتم كانت موجودة، فقد ادعيتم محالا حيث جعلتم الحركة قائمة بالجسم الأول مع وجود انتقالها عنه إلى غيره والقول بجعل الشيء ثابتا في محل انتقاله مع عنه محال وإن قلتم حدثت تلك الحركة، فقد أقررتم بحدوث شيء من الأعراض. فإن قيل: ما أنكرتم أن السكون كان ظاهرا في الجسم فكمن فيه، والحركة كانت كامنة فظهرت فكان الجسم ساكنا غير متحرك لكون السكون ظاهرا وكون الحركة كامنة، ولما انقلب الأمر صار متحركا ولم يبق ساكنا؟ قيل: إن كان الكون والظهور بالانتقال من بعض أجزاء الجسم إلى بعض الأجزاء لزمكم جميع ما ألزمناكم في السؤال الأول، وإن لم يكن بالانتقال، فإذا محل السكون والحركة واحد فيكون فيه جمع بين الضدين، وفيه كون ما قام به السكون متحركا، وكون ما قامت به الحركة ساكنا، وهذا كله محال ممتنع. ثم نقول: ما كان ظاهرا فكمن فقد انعدم فيه الظهور وحدث فيه الكون وكذا ما كان كامنا فظهر فقد انعدم فيه الكمون وحدث الظهور، وفيه ما أردنا من إثبات الأعراض ولن ينفعكم ركوب
The Necessary Existent is necessary by itself not by a meaning according to what passed in the first meaning, and God is the grantor of success. And beyond this there are proofs indicating the impossibility of non-existence for the Eternal; we left their mention for fear of length. Then if it is said: This is only valid if the ceasing of rest and the occurrence of motion are established, and this is denied; we say: If rest had not ceased and motion had come to exist, then the body would be both at rest and in motion, and likewise if motion had existed before this, then the body would be both at rest and in motion, and this is impossible. Also, the combination of rest and motion in one locus is impossible. Also, the body was before this at rest, and it is impossible for that in which motion subsists to be at rest; and after it became moving, if rest existed in it, it would not have become moving, because it is impossible for that in which rest subsists to be moving. And if it is so, it is known that rest has ceased and that motion has occurred. Then if it is said: What prevents you from saying that rest did not cease but rather transferred to another locus, and that motion did not occur but rather transferred to this body from another locus? We say: This is impossible, because the transfer from one locus to another of rest would be motion, and the subsistence of motion in motion is impossible, and likewise the subsistence of motion in rest is impossible; therefore, neither the transfer of motion nor the transfer of rest from one locus to another is permissible. Moreover, even if you adopt this locus and depart from the rational, this will not benefit you, for we say to you: When motion transfers from one locus to another and becomes moving, and likewise when rest transfers and becomes moving, was this motion present in the motion and rest at the time they were in the first place, or did it occur? If you say it was present, then you claim something impossible, since you make motion subsist in the first body while it is transferring away from it to another, and the statement that a thing is fixed in the locus of its transfer while being away from it is impossible. And if you say that motion occurred, then you have admitted the occurrence of something from among the accidents. Then if it is said: What prevents you from saying that rest was apparent in the body and then became hidden in it, and that motion was hidden and then appeared, so that the body was at rest not moving because rest was apparent and motion was hidden, and when the situation reversed, it became moving and did not remain at rest? It is said: If the being and appearance are by transfer from some parts of the body to some parts, then you are subject to all that we required of you in the first question; and if it is not by transfer, then the locus of rest and motion is one, and there would be a combination of two opposites in it, and in it there is that in which rest subsists being moving, and that in which motion subsists being at rest, and all of this is impossible and precluded. Then we say: What was apparent and then became hidden, its appearance has ceased and hiding has occurred in it; and likewise what was hidden and then appeared, its hiding has ceased and appearance has occurred in it; and in this is what we intended of proving accidents. And mounting [this] will not benefit you.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a kalam argument that begins by affirming the necessary existence of God by Himself, then introduces proofs for the impossibility of non-existence of the Eternal. However, the author leaves these proofs aside for brevity and proceeds to a specific objection regarding the occurrence of accidents. The objector claims that the argument that rest must cease and motion must occur is not established. The author replies by demonstrating that if rest did not cease when motion occurs, the body would be both at rest and in motion simultaneously, which is impossible. He further argues that rest and motion cannot coexist in one locus, and that a body that was at rest cannot become moving if rest persists, because a locus of rest cannot be moving. Therefore, rest must have ceased and motion must have newly occurred. The objector then proposes that rest might transfer to another body and motion might transfer from another body. The author refutes this by noting that transfer itself would be a motion, and motion cannot subsist in motion, nor in rest. He then presents a dilemma: if motion transfers, either the motion during transfer existed in the original locus or occurred anew. If it existed, it is impossible for a thing to be both in a locus and transferring away from it. If it occurred anew, that admits the occurrence of an accident. Next, the objector suggests that rest was apparent and became hidden, while motion was hidden and became apparent. The author responds that if this involves transfer between parts, the previous argument applies; if not, then rest and motion occupy the same locus, leading to a contradiction of opposites. He concludes that in any case, change implies the ceasing and occurrence of accidents, which is precisely what the argument aimed to prove. The passage follows the sequence: initial assertion, objection, detailed answer with multiple refutations, and final conclusion.

Key Terms

سكون
حركة
عرض
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that rest and motion can coexist in the same body, or that motion can begin without rest ceasing.
Correction: The argument shows that rest and motion are opposites; they cannot coexist in the same locus. If motion occurs, rest must have ceased.

Checkpoint
According to the author's answer, if rest did not cease when motion occurs, what would be the result?
A) The body would be in motion only.
B) The body would be both at rest and in motion, which is impossible.
C) The body would remain at rest.
D) The body would cease to exist.

Key Takeaway
Rest and motion cannot coexist; change necessitates the ceasing of one accident and the occurrence of another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a change in your own state—from stillness to action—do you pause to acknowledge that this change is not a product of your own power but a new creation from Allah? How does this realization affect your daily habits?
?
Think of a habit or sin you are trying to leave behind but keep clinging to. This passage shows that two opposites cannot coexist. Are you trying to hold onto both comfort and change? What would it look like to decisively let the old 'rest' cease so that true 'motion' toward Allah can begin?
?
The argument in this passage rests on logical necessity. In your spiritual life, where do you deny the logical implications of Tawhid—like attributing change to secondary causes rather than directly to Allah? Commit today to verbally acknowledge, even in small moments, 'This change is from Allah.'

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
8 min
Refuting the Transfer and Hiding of Accidents
Author refutes two objections: transfer of accidents and hiddenness/appearance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the ideas of transfer (intiqal) and hiddenness (kumun) of accidents are impossible and how they lead to admitting the occurrence of accidents.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot transfer or hide; change always involves creation and annihilation.

In this continuation, the objector proposes alternative explanations: that rest and motion might transfer between bodies, or that they become hidden and apparent. The author refutes these by showing they lead to contradictions or implicitly admit the occurrence of new accidents. This lesson explores these refutations and their implications for proving that accidents are created.

Guided Path
1
Read the transfer objection and answer
Read the objection that rest and motion transfer between bodies. Then read the author's refutation: transfer itself is motion, and motion cannot subsist in motion or rest.
Pay attention to the dilemma: was the motion during transfer existing or new?
2
Read the hidden/apparent objection and answer
Read the objection that rest was apparent then hidden, and motion hidden then apparent. The author refutes: if transfer between parts, same problem; if same locus, impossible combination.
The author concludes that even this implies accidents ceasing and occurring.
3
Understand the cumulative conclusion
All alternatives fail. The only consistent explanation is that accidents are created directly by God. This supports the proof of the Eternal's impossibility of non-existence.
This is a standard kalam argument for the contingency of the world.

What does the author argue about the transfer of motion or rest?
A) Transfer is possible if it occurs between similar bodies.

?

What would it mean to truly live as if each moment's state is newly created?
?

How does this lesson challenge you to renew your reliance on Him every moment?
?

What would it cost you to admit that only Allah's constant creation can give you a new state?

Why Latentness and Manifestation Are Additional Meanings
Refutation of the claim that latentness and manifestation are not additional meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attributes of latentness and manifestation must be distinct additional meanings beyond the essence of motion and rest.

Core Idea
Denying the additional meanings leads to logical contradictions and infinite regress.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi refutes the view that the states of latentness (كمون) and manifestation (ظهور) are identical to the essence of motion and rest. He presents two powerful arguments: first, that allowing an accident to subsist in another accident leads to infinite regress; second, that identifying the attributes with the essence would force contradictory properties onto a single thing. Understanding this refutation clarifies why we must affirm extra-mental attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the argument's flow. Then read the English translation.
Notice the repetitive structure—the author builds two parallel cases.
2
Identify the first argument
The author argues that if an accident could inhere in another accident, it would lead to infinite regress. Locate where this is stated.
The regress applies to the opponent's position, not to the author's.
3
Identify the second argument
The author then argues that if latentness and manifestation are not additional meanings, then a single thing (e.g., rest) would have to be both manifest and latent by its own essence, which is impossible.
This argument uses the principle of non-contradiction.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that all proofs for accidents support his view, and that this leads to proving the origination of motion by sense and rest by inference.
This connects the discussion to broader theological goals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه المحالات وكذا لزمكم قيام الكمون والظهور بالحركة والسكون، وقيام العرض بالعرض محال، لأنه لو جاز قيام الثاني بالأول جاز قيام الثالث بالثاني وكذا قيام الرابع بالثالث فيتسلسل إلى غير النهاية، وكذا ما يستقل بذاته ولا يتصور قيامه بنفسه مستغنيا عن محل يحله، يستحيل حمله لغيره، ولو قلتم إن الظهور والكمون ليسا بمعنيين زائدين على ذات الحركة والسكون، ادعيتم ما هو محال لأن السكون إن كان ظاهر الذات فلا يتصور صيرورته كامنا مع الذات الذي هو علة كونه ظاهرا وكذا لو كان كامنا لذاته حين صار كامنا لكان هذا الذات قبل ذلك موجودا فكان ينبغي أن يكون حينئذ كامنا، ولأن فيه جعل الشيء واحد كامنا لذاته ظاهرا لذاته وهو محال، وكذلك هذا الإلزام في جانب الحركة، وكل دليل أقمنا على إثبات الأعراض فذلك الدليل يدل على كون الكمون والظهور معنيين زائدين على الذات وفي ثبوتهما معنيين زائدين على الذات فساد كون الحركة والسكون كامنين، وفي فساد ذلك ثبوت حدوث الحركة بالحس وثبوت حدوث السكون بالاستدلال، والله الموفق.
These impossibilities, and likewise you are compelled [to accept] the subsistence of latentness and manifestation in motion and rest, and the subsistence of an accident in an accident is impossible, because if the subsistence of the second in the first were permissible, then the subsistence of the third in the second would be permissible, and likewise the subsistence of the fourth in the third, leading to an infinite regress. Similarly, that which subsists by itself and whose subsistence in itself is not conceivable independent of a locus that contains it is impossible to be carried by another. And if you say that manifestation and latentness are not two additional meanings to the essence of motion and rest, you claim what is impossible, because if rest is manifest by its essence, then its becoming latent while with the essence that is the cause of its being manifest is inconceivable; and likewise, if it were latent by its essence when it became latent, then this essence would have existed before, so it should have been latent then. And because it would make a single thing latent by its essence and manifest by its essence, which is impossible. And likewise this imposition applies on the side of motion. And every proof we have established for the substantiation of accidents indicates that latentness and manifestation are two additional meanings to the essence, and in their being established as two additional meanings to the essence is the corruption of motion and rest being latent. And in the corruption of that is the proof of the origination of motion by sense-perception and the proof of the origination of rest by inference. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that the attributes of latentness (كمون) and manifestation (ظهور) are not additional meanings (معنيين زائدين) beyond the essence of motion and rest. The author begins by stating that it is impossible for an accident (عرض) to subsist in another accident, because if that were allowed, it would lead to infinite regress. Then he addresses the specific claim: if someone says that ظهور and كمون are not additional meanings, then they must explain how rest, which is manifest by its essence, could become latent without an additional attribute. Similarly, if rest were latent by its essence, it would have always been latent, contradicting its manifest nature. This would entail a single thing being both latent and manifest by itself, which is impossible. The same argument applies to motion. The author concludes that all proofs for the existence of accidents actually demonstrate that كمون and ظهور are additional meanings, and that recognizing this leads to the affirmation of the origination (حدوث) of motion by sensual perception and of rest by inference.

Key Terms
الكمون
الظهور
معنيين زائدين
الحركة
السكون
قيام العرض بالعرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the attributes of latentness and manifestation are identical to the essence of motion and rest, so they are not additional.
Correction: The author shows that if they were identical, then a single thing (e.g., rest) would have to be both latent and manifest by its own essence, which is contradictory. Therefore they must be additional meanings.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if it were permissible for an accident to subsist in another accident?
A) It would prove the accident is eternal.
B) It would lead to an infinite regress.
C) It would make motion and rest identical.
D) It would negate the existence of substances.

Key Takeaway
Latentness and manifestation must be additional meanings (معنيين زائدين) to motion and rest; otherwise, impossible contradictions follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe that your outward actions (manifest) are not separate from your inner intentions (latent), but rather identical to your essence, then how would you explain the possibility of hypocrisy or change of heart without contradicting the logical necessity of additional meanings taught in this lesson? What does this demand of you in examining the consistency between your inner state and outer deeds?
?
The author argues that denying additional meanings leads to absurdities like a thing being both latent and manifest by its essence. Can you identify an area in your own life where you have refused to admit that a quality is an added attribute (e.g., anger, patience) and instead identified it with your very self, causing a contradiction in how you see yourself? What would it take for you to accept that these are additional, not essential?
?
This lesson refutes the notion that change in a thing's state (e.g., from rest to motion) is merely a change in the thing's essence. When you observe your own spiritual or moral state fluctuating (e.g., turning to God then away), do you attribute these changes to your essence or to additional attributes? If you adopt the view of this lesson, how would that reshape your sense of accountability and hope for change?

Two Substances Cannot Be Free from Accidents
Al-Nasafi argues two substances must have accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why two substances cannot exist without the accidents of combination and separation, and how this refutes the materialist claim of an eternal world.

Core Idea
Two substances together necessitate accidents, proving the world is temporal.

Al-Nasafi builds a key argument against the materialist (dahri) who asserts the world is eternal and free from accidents. He establishes that while a single substance could be free, two substances cannot—they must have combination or separation. This shows that the observed world, composed of many bodies, cannot be free from accidents and therefore cannot be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the distinction between one substance and two.
The passage starts by stating two premises.
2
Identify the two premises
Find the first premise about two substances and combination/separation, and the second about place/motion. Understand how they lead to the conclusion.
3
Understand the concession
Al-Nasafi concedes that a single substance could be free. Note why this does not help the materialist.
The world has many substances.
4
Connect to the refutation
See how the argument forces the materialist to accept that the world has accidents and is therefore temporal.
What does not precede the temporal cannot be eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الاجتماع والافتراق، وكذا وجود المكان والمتمكن زمانين لا يخلو عن الحركة أو السكون، لنلزم الدهرى استحالة خلو أجرام العالم وأجسامه عن الأعراض، فأما إذا سلم لنا حدوثها كلها فلم يبق بنا حاجة إلى إثبات ذلك بواسطة إثبات استحالة تعريها عن الأعراض، على أنا ندعي استحالة تعري الجوهرين عن الاجتماع والافتراق والاستحالة تعري المتمكن في المكان في حالة البقاء عن الحركة والسكون ليظهر أن أعيان العالم ما نشاهد يمتنع خلوها عن الأعراض فيستحيل قدمها إذ قدم ما لا يسبق الحادث محال وذلك كما ادعينا، فأما نحن فنسلم أن الله تعالى لو خلق جوهرا واحدا لكان خاليا عن الاجتماع والافتراق والحركة والسكون فأما خلو جوهرين لو خلقهما الله تعالى عن الاجتماع والافتراق وخلو المكان والمتمكن في حالة البقاء عن الحركة والسكون فمحال، والكلام وقع في هذا، ثم نقول لهم: لو كانت هذه الأجسام خالية عن الاجتماع والافتراق ثم حدثا فيها، أي العرضين أسبق إليها الاجتماع أم الافتراق؟ فأي العرضين عينوا فقد ادعوا محالا، لأن اجتماع ما كان موجودا ولم يكن مفترقا، أو افتراق ما كان موجودا ولم يكن مجتمعا محال، وبهذا يبطل قول أصحاب الهيولى أنها من الأزل شيء واحد غير موصوف بالاجتماع والافتراق، فإنا نقول لهم: أي العرضين أسبق إلى الهيولى: الاجتماع أم الافتراق؟ فبأي الجوابين أجابوا كان محالا. والله الموفق.
From combination and separation, and likewise the existence of place and the thing located in place for two moments is not free from motion or rest, to compel the materialist (dahri) to admit the impossibility of the bodies of the world and their substances being free from accidents. But if he concedes to us the temporality of all of them, we no longer need to prove that by proving the impossibility of their being denuded of accidents, though we claim the impossibility of two substances being denuded of combination and separation, and the impossibility of the thing located in place in the state of permanence being denuded of motion and rest, so that it appears that the very entities of the world that we observe cannot be free from accidents, and thus their pre-eternity is impossible, for the pre-eternity of what does not precede the temporal is impossible. That is as we claimed. As for us, we concede that if God Most High had created a single substance, it would be free from combination and separation and motion and rest. But as for two substances, if God Most High created them, their being free from combination and separation, and the place and the thing located in place in the state of permanence being free from motion and rest, is impossible. The discussion is about this. Then we say to them: If these bodies were free from combination and separation, then they occurred in them, which of the two accidents is prior to them—combination or separation? Whichever of the two accidents they specify, they have claimed an impossibility. For the combination of what existed and was not separated, or the separation of what existed and was not combined, is impossible. And by this the doctrine of the proponents of prime matter (hayula) is refuted, that it is from pre-eternity a single thing not described by combination or separation. For we say to them: Which of the two accidents is prior to prime matter—combination or separation? Whichever of the two answers they give, it is impossible. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a two-part argument. First, al-Nasafi argues against the materialist (dahri) who claims the world is eternal and free from accidents. He says that even if we accept that a single substance could be free from accidents, two substances cannot be free from combination and separation, nor can a body in place be free from motion or rest over time. This shows that the observed world cannot be free from accidents, so it must be temporal. Second, he refutes the doctrine of prime matter (hayula) which claims an eternal single entity without accidents. He poses a dilemma: if accidents like combination and separation arise in it, which one comes first? Both answers are impossible because an accident cannot arise in something that was previously without its opposite. Thus the hayula doctrine is false.

Key Terms
دهري
اجتماع
افتراق
جوهر
عرض
قدم
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any substance must always have accidents; al-Nasafi specifies a single substance can be free.
Correction: The impossibility applies only to two substances together (or a body in place over time), not to a single substance in isolation.

Checkpoint
What is al-Nasafi's main premise in this passage?
A) All substances must have accidents.
B) Two substances cannot be free from combination and separation.
C) A single substance cannot be free from motion.
D) The world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Two substances together necessitate accidents, proving the world is temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that your very existence—every movement, every relationship (combination and separation)—is a temporal accident pointing to a Creator. When you interact with others today, will you consciously recognize each meeting and parting as a sign of Allah's constant creation, or will you treat them as mere chance?
?
If two substances cannot be free from accidents for even two moments, then every breath you take is a created accident. Are you living as though your next breath is guaranteed, or are you grateful for each one as a gift from the One who sustains you moment by moment?
?
Al-Nasafi concedes that a single substance could be free from accidents, but the world you live in is full of multiple substances—people, objects, your own body. How does this multiplicity demand humility, reminding you that nothing in your life is independent of Allah's continuous creation?

The Priority Dilemma Against Prime Matter
Al-Nasafi refutes the hayula doctrine using a priority dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma al-Nasafi poses against the hayula doctrine, showing that the concept of an eternal single entity without accidents is absurd.

Core Idea
If prime matter acquires accidents, which comes first? Either answer is impossible.

Al-Nasafi now targets the doctrine of prime matter (hayula), which posits an eternal single entity free from accidents. He uses a clever dilemma: If accidents like combination and separation arise in it, which one occurs first? Both options — combination first or separation first — lead to logical absurdities. Thus the hayula doctrine is refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the question posed: which accident is prior?
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the dilemma
Identify the two possible answers and why each is impossible.
Think about what combination and separation presuppose.
3
Apply to hayula
See how the same dilemma applies to the hayula doctrine, refuting it.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument shows that an eternal entity without accidents cannot later acquire them.

?

?
If every atom is directly created by Allah at every instant—not from any eternal matter—then nothing in your life is self-sustaining. Does your daily worry and planning reflect that truth, or do you act as if some things just 'are' without His constant maintenance? What would it take to align your heart with this reality?

Proof That Substances Cannot Be Free from Accidents: A Two-Part Dilemma
Al-Nasafi proves substances cannot be free from accidents via a two-part dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Nasafi's proof that substances cannot be free from accidents in pre-eternity, using a dilemma based on essence or a contrary meaning.

Core Idea
Substances cannot be free from accidents—either by essence or by a contrary meaning—proving they are temporal.

Al-Nasafi presents a rigorous proof for the temporality of substances. He divides the possibilities into two: freedom by essence or by a contrary meaning. This lesson walks through each branch and shows why both lead to contradiction, establishing that substances must have accidents and therefore are created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the logical connectors like 'إما... وإما' (either...or) and 'فإن... وإن' (if...and if).
2
Identify the two branches of the dilemma
The passage first states that any freedom from an accident is either by essence or by a meaning. Apply this to substances in pre-eternity and find where Al-Nasafi examines each branch.
3
Analyze the contradictions in each branch
For each branch, note the contradiction that arises: if by essence, the essence would still be present when accidents occur; if by a meaning, either the meaning persists (preventing accidents) or ceases (meaning itself temporal).
Consider why these contradictions force the conclusion that substances cannot be free from accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل عليه أن ما يخلو عن عرض من الأعراض إما أن كان خاليا عنه لذاته كالحركة تخلو عن السواد، والسواد عن الحركة، وكذا كل عرض، إذ يستحيل قبول عرض عرضا آخر يقوم به فكان متعريا عنه لذاته، وإما أن كان يخلو عنه لا لذاته بل لقيام معنى مناف لذلك العرض في هذا المحل، كما يخلو الجسم عن السواد لقيام البياض به، وكذا على القلب وكذا عن الحركة والسكون وكل عرضين متضادين قام أحدهما في محل، فخلو الجواهر عن الأعراض في الأزل من أي قبيل كان أكانت الجواهر خالية عن الأعراض لذاتها أم كانت متعرية خالية عنها لمعنى؟ فإن كانت خالية لذاتها فما بالها قبلت الأعراض من بعد، والذات الموجب للتعري قائم؟ ولو جاز يقبل ذات عرضا مع قيام ما يوجب تعريه عنه، لجاز أن يقبل الجسم البياض مع قيام السواد به، إذ لا فرق بينهما في أن الموجب للتعري قائم إلا أن هناك الموجب هو الذات، وهاهنا الموجب هو معنى وراء الذات وهذا محال، فكذا الأول، وإن كانت الجواهر متعرية خالية عن الأعراض لمعنى ينافي الأعراض فلا يخلو الأمر إذا حدثت فيها الأعراض من ارتفاع ذلك المعنى أو إبقائه على ما كان، ومحال حدوث الأعراض فيها مع وجود المعنى الموجب لتعريه عنها، ولو جاز ذا لجاز اجتماع السواد والبياض وكل متضادين في محل، وإن ارتفع ذلك المعنى علم أنه كان محدثا لما أن العدم على القديم محال، وإذا كان محدثا كان عرضا والجوهر لا يخلو عنه أو عن الأعراض وهو في نفس عرض فإذا لم تخل الجواهر عن الأعراض ويستحيل خلوها عنها والله الموفق.
And the proof of this is that whatever is free from an accident among accidents is either free from it by its essence, like motion being free from blackness, and blackness from motion, and likewise every accident, for it is impossible for an accident to receive another accident that subsists in it, so it is denuded of it by its essence; or it is free from it not by its essence but by the subsistence of a meaning that is opposite to that accident in this subject, just as a body is free from blackness by the subsistence of whiteness in it, and likewise conversely, and likewise free from motion and rest and any two opposite accidents when one of them subsists in a subject. So the freedom of substances from accidents in pre-eternity—of which category is it? Were the substances free from accidents by their essence, or were they denuded and free from them by a meaning? If they were free by their essence, then what is the matter with them that they accepted accidents afterwards, while the essence that necessitates denudation is present? And if it were permissible for a substance to accept an accident while that which necessitates its denudation is present, then it would be permissible for a body to accept whiteness while blackness subsists in it, for there is no difference between them in that the necessitator of denudation is present, except that there the necessitator is the essence, and here the necessitator is a meaning beyond the essence—and this is impossible. So likewise the first. And if the substances are denuded and free from accidents by a meaning that contradicts accidents, then the matter is not free, when accidents occur in them, from either that meaning ceasing or its remaining as it was. It is impossible for accidents to occur in them while the meaning that necessitates their denudation exists. And if this were permissible, it would be permissible for blackness and whiteness and every pair of opposites to coexist in a subject. And if that meaning ceases, it is known that it was temporal, for non-existence is impossible for the eternal. And if it is temporal, it is an accident, and a substance is not free from it or from [other] accidents, and it is itself an accident. So if substances are not free from accidents, and their being free from them is impossible. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage provides a rigorous proof (dālīl) that substances cannot be free from accidents in pre-eternity. The author divides the possibilities into two: either substances are free from accidents by their very essence (like how one accident cannot receive another), or they are free due to a contrary meaning (like how a body is free from blackness because whiteness is present). He then examines both. If by essence, then the essence that prevents accidents would still be present when accidents later occur, which is contradictory—just as a body with blackness cannot simultaneously have whiteness. If by a contrary meaning, that meaning either remains or ceases when accidents occur. If it remains, it prevents accidents, so they cannot arise. If it ceases, then that meaning must be temporal and itself an accident, leading to an infinite regress or the substance being never free. Thus, substances cannot be free from accidents, proving their temporality.

Key Terms
جوهر
عرض
ذات
معنى
مناف
تضاد
قدم
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves that substances must be constantly occupied by every accident.
Correction: The argument only shows that substances cannot be completely devoid of all accidents at any given time; they can have different accidents at different times.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the problem if substances were free from accidents by their essence?
A) They would never be able to accept any accident.
B) They would accept accidents while the essence that prevents them remains, which is contradictory.
C) They would be eternal and uncreated.
D) They would have no attributes at all.

Key Takeaway
The existence of accidents in substances proves they are temporal, as they cannot be eternally free from accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything you rely on in this world is temporal and bound to change, what is the one reality you are building your life upon that will never pass away? Are you investing in what perishes or in the Eternal?
?
This argument shows that even substances—the most fundamental things—cannot be independent of change. In your own life, where have you been acting as if something is permanent (a relationship, a skill, a status) when it is not? What would change if you truly lived knowing it will not last?
?
The proof forces us to admit that the universe is contingent and created. Does your daily routine reflect this belief, or do you live as if this world is self-sufficient? What one habit could you change today to make your reliance on the Creator more real?

The Self-Evidence of Alternating Accidents
Al-Nasafi asserts the self-evident impossibility of a subject lacking alternating accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Nasafi considers the impossibility of alternating accidents being absent from a subject to be rationally self-evident, and how this supports the argument for the necessity of accidents in substances.

Core Idea
A subject cannot be empty of both alternating accidents—this is as impossible as uniting opposites.

Al-Nasafi presents a powerful argument that does not rely on complex kalam reasoning but on a basic rational intuition. He points out that it is self-evidently impossible for a substance to be without either of two alternating accidents that have no intermediate state, such as motion and rest, or combination and separation. This is as plainly absurd as the idea that opposites could coexist. In this lesson, we'll unpack this claim and see how it strengthens the case for the necessity of accidents in substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two examples
Notice that Al-Nasafi gives two sets of alternating accidents: combination/separation for two substances, and motion/rest for a body in place. Understand what 'alternating' means here.
These are pairs that cannot both be absent at once.
3
Understand 'no intermediate state'
The phrase 'between which there is no intermediary' means there is no third state between the two. For example, a body is either in motion or at rest; there is no halfway state.
4
Appreciate the comparison
Al-Nasafi compares this impossibility to the impossibility of the union of opposites (like black and white coexisting). This is a basic rational principle everyone accepts.
5
Reflect on self-evidence
Note that he says this is established 'in the beginning of the intellect' and that there is no distinction between a novice and an expert in kalam. Why is it important that this is self-evident?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأن استحالة ارتفاع العرضين المتعاقبين اللذين لا واسطة بينهما كالاجتماع والافتراق عن الجوهرين والحركة والسكون مع وجود المكان المتمكن عن المحل متقررة في بداية العقل وشهادات المعارف لا تفرقة فيه بين الرايض والمرتاض والعقل الخالي عن صناعة الكلام والحاذق الماهر فيه كاستحالة اجتماع المتضادين
And because the impossibility of the removal of the two successive accidents between which there is no intermediary, such as combination and separation from two substances, and motion and rest with the existence of place and the thing located in place from the subject, is established in the beginning of the intellect and the testimonies of cognitions, with no distinction in it between the novice and the ascetic, and the intellect devoid of the art of kalam and the adept skilled therein, like the impossibility of the union of opposites.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage advances another proof that substances cannot be free from accidents. The author states that it is self-evident to all rational people—whether trained in kalam or not—that a subject cannot be without either of two alternating accidents that have no intermediate state (like combination/separation for two substances, or motion/rest for a body in place). This is as obviously impossible as the union of opposites (like black and white coexisting). The argument relies on immediate rational intuition, not on dialectical reasoning, making it universally compelling.

Refutation of Infinite Regress in the Past
The author refutes the claim that every event is preceded by another, showing it leads to infinite regress and impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why infinite regress in the past is impossible and how the distinction between past and future infinite series refutes the claim that every event must be preceded by another.

Core Idea
Infinite regress in the past is impossible; infinite progression in the future is possible.

This passage tackles a key theological argument: if every event requires a previous event, then the universe would have no beginning—an absurdity. The author carefully distinguishes between an infinite chain going backwards (impossible) and one going forwards (possible), using a simple analogy to make the point clear. The inability to grasp this distinction led the Mu'tazilite Abu Hudhayl to a heretical conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the core argument
Read the first part of the passage where the author states the claim and its absurd consequence: that the temporal would be eternal.
Notice how the author uses logical contradiction to refute the claim.
2
Understand the distinction
The author contrasts two types of infinite series: one before (regress) and one after (progress). Only the former is impossible. Find where he says 'As for our statement...'
3
Study the morsel analogy
The analogy of eating morsels with conditions 'before' and 'after' illustrates why one is impossible and the other is not. Understand how it mirrors the argument about accidents.
This analogy is central to the refutation.
4
See the consequence for Abu Hudhayl
Read the final part about Abu Hudhayl's failure to distinguish, which led him to deny the infinity of God's power and the eternity of reward and punishment.
This shows the practical importance of the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: ما يزعمون أن لا حادث إلا وقبله حادث يوجب محالا، يوجب وجود المحدث في القدم لأن الجوهر لما كان قديما وفي قدمه لا يخلو عن الحوادث لزعم هذا أو القول بقدم المحدث ممتنع محال، لأنه قول بأن لوجوده ابتداء وليس لوجوده ابتداء، وامتناع اجتماع السلب والإيجاب في محل واحد مما لا يخفي على المجانين، فأما قولنا: إنه لا عرض إلا وبعده عرض فلا استحالة فيه، وإثبات المستحيل بثبوت ما ليس بمستحيل جهل فاحش. ولأن القول أن لا عرض إلا وقبله عرض مما يعرف بطلانه بالحس، فإنا رأينا عرضا ما موجودا ثم قلنا لا وجود لهذا إلا وقبله عرض ثم كذا ذلك العرض لا وجود له إلا وقبله عرض آخر هكذا إلى ما لا يتناهى وما لا تناهي له لا يتحقق ثبوته فإذا لا يتحقق ثبوت هذا العرض وهو قد تحقق فيثبت أن وجوده لم يتعلق بما لا تحقق له، وبمثله لو قيل: لا عرض إلا وبعده عرض لم يمتنع ثبوت هذا العرض، بل وجوده يقتضي وجودا آخر بعده، ووجود الثاني ووجود الثالث كذا لا إلى نهاية نظيره أمر تضاعف الحساب أنه إذ لم يجعل له ابتداء منه يبدأ لا يجوز وجود شيء منه البتة، وإذا حصلت البداية يجوز أن يبقى فيه فيزيد ثم يزيد دائما وكذا من قال لغيره: لا تأكل لقمة إلا وتأكل قبلها لقمة لا يتمكن من الدخول في الأكل فإن كل لقمة يريد أن يأكلها كان من شرط أكلها أن يأكل قبلها أخرى فيبقى غير آكل أبدا، وبمثله لو قال له: لا تأكل لقمة إلا وتأكل بعدها لقمة أخرى لتمكن من الدخول في الأكل ثم يبقى أبد الدهر آكلا فكذا هذا، والعجز عن دفع هذا السؤال وتعذر التفرقة بين الماضي والمستأنف ألجأ أبا الهذيل إلى القول بتناهي مقدورات الله تعالى وانقطاع ثواب المؤمنين وعذاب الكفرة في الآخرة فإنه لما عجز عن التفرقة سوى بينهما.
Then we say: What they claim—that no event occurs except that there is an event before it—necessitates an absurdity: it necessitates the existence of the temporal in eternity. Because if the substance is eternal and in its eternity it is not free from events, according to this claim, then saying that the temporal is eternal is impossible and absurd. For it is a statement that it has a beginning and does not have a beginning, and the impossibility of combining negation and affirmation in one locus is not hidden even to the insane. As for our statement: 'There is no accident except that there is an accident after it'—there is no impossibility in it. And establishing the impossible by establishing what is not impossible is gross ignorance. And because the statement that there is no accident except that there is an accident before it is known to be false by sense perception. For we see an accident existing, then we say: 'This does not exist except that there is an accident before it'; then that accident likewise does not exist except that there is another accident before it, and so on indefinitely. But whatever has no end cannot have its existence verified. Thus the existence of this accident cannot be verified, yet it has been verified—so it is established that its existence is not contingent upon what has no verification. Similarly, if it is said: 'There is no accident except that there is an accident after it,' the existence of this accident is not prevented. Rather, its existence necessitates another existence after it, and the existence of the second and the third, likewise without end. An analogy is the doubling of numbers: if no beginning is assigned from which to start, no part of it can exist at all. But if a beginning exists, it is possible to continue in it, increasing then always increasing. Likewise, if someone says to another: 'Do not eat a morsel unless you eat a morsel before it,' he cannot begin eating; for every morsel he wants to eat, the condition is that he eats another before it, so he remains never eating forever. And similarly, if he says to him: 'Do not eat a morsel unless you eat a morsel after it,' he can begin eating, then remains eating forever. So is this. And the inability to refute this question and the difficulty of distinguishing between the past and the future compelled Abu al-Hudhayl to claim the finitude of God's powers and the cessation of the reward of believers and the punishment of disbelievers in the hereafter. For when he was unable to distinguish, he equated them.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that every event is preceded by another event (infinite regress in the past). The author argues that this claim leads to absurdity: if the substance (jawhar) of the world is eternal, and it is never free from events, then according to the claim, the temporal would be eternal—a contradiction of having a beginning and not having a beginning. This is self-evidently absurd. The author then distinguishes between two types of infinite series: (1) an infinite regress in the past (no event without a previous event) and (2) an infinite progression in the future (no event without a subsequent event). The former is impossible because it requires an infinite chain with no beginning, making existence impossible—analogous to counting numbers without a starting point. The latter is possible because a beginning can be established, and then the series can continue indefinitely. The author illustrates with the example of eating morsels: if the condition is that every morsel must be preceded by another, you can never start; if the condition is that every morsel must be followed by another, you can start and then continue forever. This argument is used to show that the opponent's view (that every event is preceded by an event) is impossible, while the correct view (that every event is followed by another event) is not. The inability of Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf to distinguish between past and future infinite regress led him to the heretical conclusion that God's powers are finite and that reward and punishment will end—a view the author rejects.

Key Terms
حادث
عرض
جوهر
قديم
محدث
تسلسل
تناهي
مقدورات الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that an infinite series of events in the past (no beginning) is possible just as an infinite series in the future is possible.
Correction: The passage shows that an infinite regress in the past prevents any event from existing because there is no starting point, while an infinite progression in the future is possible because a starting point exists.

Checkpoint
What compelled Abu al-Hudhayl to claim the finitude of God's powers?
A) His inability to distinguish between past and future infinite regress.
B) His belief in the eternity of the world.
C) His rejection of sense perception.
D) His acceptance of the claim that every event is preceded by an event.

Key Takeaway
An infinite chain of events with no beginning is impossible because nothing can exist without a starting point, whereas an infinite chain after a beginning is unproblematic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author argues that every chain must have a beginning. Think of a personal habit or thought pattern that you have never examined the origin of. What would it mean for you to trace it back to its first cause today, and what change might that require?
?
Abu al-Hudhayl's confusion between past and future infinite series led him to deny eternal punishment and reward. Are there any beliefs you hold where you have blurred clear distinctions set by the Quran and reason? How would you correct that, and what is at stake if you don't?
?
The morsel analogy shows that a condition of 'before' prevents any action from starting. Is there a good deed you have been putting off because you feel you need to prepare 'more' before starting? What is one step you can take right now to begin, trusting that Allah accepts the first step?
