Iskaafi's Causality Argument for Speech
Al-Iskaafi argues that speech can exist in multiple places due to multiple causes, unlike bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Iskaafi's argument that speech can exist in multiple places simultaneously unlike bodies, based on the nature of their causes.

Core Idea
Speech can be in many places because its causes can coexist; bodies cannot because their cause is singular and contradictory.

Al-Iskaafi, a prominent Mu'tazilite theologian, offers a distinctive argument to distinguish between speech (kalam) and physical bodies. He focuses on the possibility of existing in multiple places at once. This argument was used to support the created and multiple nature of the Qur'an. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parallel structure between speech and bodies.
2
Identify the two cases
Identify how Iskaafi treats speech and bodies separately, each with its own cause (ʿilla).
3
Understand the concept of cause
Ask yourself: What is the cause for speech? What is the cause for a body? How do they differ in terms of multiplicity?
The key is that speech has multiple possible causes that can coexist.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider why this argument matters - it supports the view that the Qur'an (as speech) is created and can be present in multiple recitations simultaneously.
This is a Mu'tazilite position against the Ash'ari view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم الإسكافي في الفرق بين الكلام وبين الأجسام في جواز وجوده في مكانين وأكثر وامتنع ذلك في الأجسام، أن الكلام إذا وجد في مكان فلا يوجد لنفسه وذاته، وإنما يوجد لعلة من العلل إما القراءة وإما الكتابة وإما الحفظ، فأينما وجدت إحدى هذه العلل فتوجب وجود الكلام. كما أن الجسم إذا وجد في مكان فلا يوجد لنفسه وذاته، ولكن بعلة من الكون، فأينما وجد كونه وجب أن يكون الجسم موجودا في ذلك المكان، لكن الجسم الواحد لا يجوز وجوده في مكانين في حالة واحدة، لأن لوجوده علة واحدة فقط وهو الكون، والكون في مكانين متضاد، وجود الجسم في مكانين لتضاد كونيه، أما لوجود الكلام في أماكن فعلل ليست بمضادة ويجوز اجتماعها، لأنه يجوز أن توجد القراءات في حالة واحدة مجتمعة، وكذا الكتابات، فلجواز وجود اجتماع عللها في وقت واحد، جاز وجود الكلام في أماكن متفرقة في حالة واحدة.
And Al-Iskaafi claimed in the difference between speech (kalam) and bodies (ajsam) regarding the permissibility of its existence in two or more places and the impossibility of that for bodies, that speech when it exists in a place does not exist by itself and its essence, but rather exists for some cause: either recitation or writing or memorization. So wherever one of these causes occurs, it necessitates the existence of speech. Just as a body when it exists in a place does not exist by itself and its essence, but by a cause of its location (al-kawn). So wherever its location (kawn) occurs, the body must exist in that place. But a single body is not permissible to exist in two places at one time, because its existence has only one cause, which is the location (al-kawn), and the location in two places is contradictory (mutadaad). So the existence of the body in two places is impossible due to the contradiction of its two locations (kawnayn). As for the existence of speech in multiple places, its causes are not contradictory and their combination is permissible, because it is permissible for recitations to exist together at one time, and likewise writings. So due to the permissibility of the combination of its causes at one time, it is permissible for speech to exist in multiple separate places at one time.
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage is an argument from Al-Iskaafi distinguishing speech (kalam) from bodies (ajsam) regarding the possibility of existing in multiple places simultaneously. The author presents Iskaafi's reasoning: speech does not exist by itself but by a cause—recitation, writing, or memorization. These causes can coexist, so speech can be in many places. Bodies, however, exist by a single cause—their location (kawn)—which cannot be in two places at once due to contradiction. Therefore, a body cannot be in two places, but speech can. This argument is a key part of the Mu'tazilite defense of the created and multiple nature of the Qur'an.

Key Terms
الكلام
الأجسام
علة
الكون
متضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Iskaafi is arguing for the uncreatedness of speech; he is actually supporting its created and multiple nature.
Correction: Iskaafi's argument is part of the Mu'tazilite defense of the created Qur'an, showing that speech can be multiple and present in many places due to multiple causes.

Checkpoint
According to Iskaafi, why can speech exist in multiple places while a body cannot?
A) Because speech is immaterial
B) Because speech has multiple causes that can coexist
C) Because bodies are permanent
D) Because speech is accidental

Key Takeaway
Al-Iskaafi's argument uses causality to show that speech, unlike bodies, can exist in multiple places simultaneously because its causes are not mutually exclusive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Iskaafi argues that speech can be in many places because its causes—recitation, writing, and memorization—can be many. Which of these three causes is weakest in your own life? What will you do today to strengthen it so that Allah's speech multiplies in your heart and home?
?
The argument shows that a body cannot be in two places at once. Your body is limited, but the speech of Allah can be everywhere. How does this reality challenge your current commitment to spreading the Quran? Are you content with only reciting to yourself, or will you become a cause for its presence in others' lives?

The Quran and Recitation: A Dilemma
Nasafi poses a dilemma: Is the Quran identical to our recitation or different?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opening move in the Maturidi refutation of Iskaafi's argument that distinguishes between the Quran and its recitation.

Core Idea
Is the Quran identical to our recitation or not? That is the key question.

Imam al-Nasafi begins his response to Iskaafi by posing a critical dilemma. Before engaging with the full argument, he forces Iskaafi to clarify whether the Quran is exactly the same as the act of recitation or something distinct. This initial question exposes a logical contradiction in Iskaafi's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the question posed.
2
Identify the dilemma
Notice that the author forces a choice between two options: identity or difference.
3
Understand the first consequence
If Iskaafi says the Quran is identical to recitation, then making recitation a cause for the Quran's existence leads to absurdity.
4
Recognize the incompleteness
The passage cuts off, so we only have the first horn of the dilemma. The second horn and full refutation are not given here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول في دفع هذه الشبهة – والله الموفق -: ماذا تقول : أن القرآن هو عين قراءتنا أو غير قراءتنا يوجد بها في محله وهو اللسان؟ فإن قال: القرآن هو عين قراءتنا . فقد أحال في جعلها علة لوجود...
So we say in refuting this doubt - and Allah grants success -: What do you say: Is the Quran the very same as our recitation or other than our recitation, existing by it in its place, which is the tongue? If he says: The Quran is the very same as our recitation. Then he has made it impossible by making it a cause for the existence...
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the author's refutation (daf') of Iskaafi's argument. He poses a dilemma: Is the Quran identical to our recitation (qira'atina) or not? If it is identical, then making the recitation a cause for its existence leads to contradiction. The passage cuts off, leaving only the first conditional statement. The argument likely continues with the second option and further consequences. This is an objection-and-answer style: the author presents a question that undermines Iskaafi's distinction.

Key Terms

قراءتنا
علة

The Principle of Attribute Consistency
Establishing a logical principle via speech analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical principle that the same subject is described by both aspects of an attribute, and how this is used in the debate about creation.

Core Idea
Attributes have consistent subjects: speaker is also truthful.

Imam Nasafi begins by establishing a logical principle that will be crucial for refuting al-Nazzam. He uses the example of speech: if someone is speaking, they are also the one who is truthful by that speech. This principle will then be applied to the attribute of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the analogy with speech.
2
Identify the principle
The principle is that the subject described by one meaning of an attribute is the same subject described by the other meaning.
Here the attribute is speech (speaking and truthful).
3
Understand the application
Nasafi says 'so too here' meaning the principle applies to creation: the one who creates is the one who is the creator, and the act of creation is not the same as the created thing.
This sets up the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يحقق هذا: أن من كان موصوفا بأحد معنى صفة، كان هو الموصوف بالمعنى الآخر. فإن الكلام لما كان كلاما وصدقا كان الموصوف بكونه متكلما هو الموصوف بكونه صادقا، ولو ادعى إنسان أن المتكلم به غير من هو صادق به نسب إلي الجهل، فكذا ها هنا والذي يدل على صحة ما ذهبنا إليه أن الخلق لو كان هو المخلوق، لكان خلق السماء هو السماء، وخلق الأرض هو الأرض وكذا هذا في جميع أجسام العالم. ثم الله تعالى إنما كان خالقا لها في ابتداء أحوال وجوها لوجود هذا الذات الذي هو خلقه، وهذا الذات بعينه في الزمان الثاني والثالث فصاعدا موجودا فيكون خلقا فيكون الله تعالى خالقا لها في كل حال، فيوصف بأنه يخلق السماوات والأرضين والجبال للحال، ويوجدها كما هو مذهب النظام، والقول بإيجاد الموجود وخلق المخلوق محال، إذ ليس يشك أن ما صار مثبت الكينونة حاصلا في الوجود والآنية، فهو ما دام في تلك الحال فمن المحال أن يوجد ثانيا ولا يلزمنا هذا، وإن كان الإيجاد الذي هو صفة الله تعالى موجودا للحال لأن المخلوق كان مخلوقا لتعلق تلك الصفة به، وهي تعلقت به عند خروجه من العدم إلى الوجود، فأما بعد ذلك فلا تعلق به لاستحالة إيجاد الموجود، هذا كما قلنا جميعا إن الموجود في أول أحوال وجوده مقدور الوجود لتعلق القدرة به في تلك الحال، وفي حالة البقاء لم يبق مقدورا لوجود لأنه لا تعلق له بعد ذلك بها لاستحالة تعلق الوجود بالموجد، وكذا الإرادة مع المراد على هذا خلاف ما ذهبوا إليه، فإن ما هو خلق هذا الجسم الذي كان الله تعالى لأجله خالقا له موجودا إذ هو كان خالقا له لهذا لا لتعلق الخلق به ترفع ذلك بعد ذلك بل لعين هذا الخلق وهو قائم فإن ساعدوا النظام تركوا مذهبهم ووقعوا فيما هو محال وهو القول بإيجاد الموجود، وإن خالفوه ناقضوا
And what confirms this is that whoever is described with one meaning of an attribute, he is the one described with the other meaning. For when speech is speech and truth, the one described as speaking is the one described as truthful. And if someone claimed that the speaker of it is other than the one who is truthful by it, he would be attributed to ignorance. So too here. And what indicates the correctness of what we hold is that if creation were the created, then the creation of the sky would be the sky, and the creation of the earth would be the earth, and likewise for all bodies of the world. Then Allah, exalted, is only Creator of them at the beginning of states and aspects due to the existence of this essence which is His creation, and this same essence in the second and third time and beyond exists, so it would be created, so Allah would be Creator of them in every state, and He would be described as creating the heavens and the earths and the mountains at the present moment, and bringing them into existence, as is the doctrine of al-Nazzam. And the statement of bringing into existence the existent and creating the created is impossible, for there is no doubt that what has become established in existence and now is, as long as it is in that state, it is impossible that it be brought into existence a second time. And this does not follow upon us, even though the bringing into existence which is an attribute of Allah exists presently, because the created was created due to the attachment of that attribute to it, and it attached to it upon its emergence from non-existence to existence. As for after that, there is no attachment to it due to the impossibility of bringing into existence the existent. This is as we all said: the existent in the first state of its existence is possible of existence due to the attachment of power to it in that state, and in the state of remaining it does not remain possible of existence because there is no attachment to it after that to them due to the impossibility of the attachment of existence to the one that is brought into existence. And likewise the will with the willed, contrary to what they held. For what is the creation of this body for which Allah was Creator of it exists, since He was Creator of it for this, not due to the attachment of creation to it being removed after that, but rather for this very creation and it is subsisting. If they assist al-Nazzam, they leave their doctrine and fall into what is impossible, namely the statement of bringing into existence the existent, and if they oppose him, they contradict.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage is a proof and a refutation. The author, Imam Nasafi, begins by establishing a principle: anyone described by one meaning of an attribute (like speech) is also described by the other meaning (like truthfulness). If someone claimed that the speaker of a speech is not the one who is truthful by that speech, that would be ignorance. He then applies this to the topic: if creation (khalq) were identical to the created (makhluq), then the creation of the sky would be the sky itself, and similarly for all bodies — which is absurd. Next, he argues that if creation were the same as the created, then Allah would be described as creating the heavens and earths at every moment, as al-Nazzam held, but this would lead to the impossible notion of creating what already exists (إيجاد الموجود). He explains that the attribute of creation (إيجاد) attaches to the thing upon its emergence from non-existence to existence, but after that it cannot attach again because you cannot bring into existence what already exists. Similarly, power (قدرة) only attaches at the moment of coming into existence, not during persistence. He then addresses the opponents: if they agree with Nazzam, they fall into the impossibility of creating the existent; if they disagree, they contradict themselves.

Key Terms
خلق (khalq)
مخلوق (makhluq)
كلام (kalam)
صدق (sidq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone claimed that the speaker of speech is not the one truthful by it, what would that person be attributed to?
A) Wisdom
B) Ignorance
C) Justice
D) Mercy

Key Takeaway
The same subject is described by both aspects of an attribute, so creation and creator are distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily interactions, do you consistently treat the same attribute as belonging to the same person? For example, do you ever praise someone's speech but accuse them of lying? How does this inconsistency reveal a deeper flaw in your understanding of divine attributes? Commit to examining your own consistency.
?
The passage warns that separating the speaker from the truthful one is ignorance. Are there areas in your life where you have separated Allah's attributes in your mind, such as believing in His mercy but doubting His justice? How will you correct this today?
?
If logical consistency is required for even human attributes, how much more for Allah's attributes? What steps will you take to ensure that your belief in Allah's attributes is internally consistent, especially when faced with intellectual doubts?

The Absurd Consequence of Identifying Creation with the Created
Proving by absurdity that creation cannot be identical to the created

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the absurd consequence that would follow if creation were identical to the created, and how that leads to the view of al-Nazzam.

Core Idea
If creation were the created, the sky would be its own creation.

Imam Nasafi now presents the core argument against the identity of creation and created. He shows that if they were identical, then the creation of the sky would be the sky itself, which is absurd. He then explains that this would lead to the doctrine of al-Nazzam, who held that Allah continuously creates everything at every moment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the passage focusing on the hypothetical 'if creation were the created'.
2
Identify the absurd consequence
The absurdity is that each thing's creation would be the thing itself.
For example, the creation of the sky would be the sky.
3
Connect to Nazzam's view
Nasafi says this leads to the doctrine that Allah creates at every moment, which is the view of al-Nazzam.
This sets up the refutation of continuous creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you thank Him today for bringing you into existence once, not every moment?
?

?

How does knowing that God's creation is distinct from Him yet reliant on Him change how you seek His presence? Commit to one way you will acknowledge God as the sole Creator today.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Impossibility of Creating the Already Existent
Proving that an existent cannot be brought into existence again, and refuting Nazzam's view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible to bring into existence what already exists, and how this refutes the idea of continuous creation.

Core Idea
You cannot create what already exists.

In this lesson, Imam Nasafi delivers the decisive refutation: the attribute of creation only attaches to a thing when it emerges from non-existence. After that, it cannot be created again. He extends this to power and will, and presents a dilemma for his opponents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the passage carefully; it contains the proof of impossibility.
2
Understand the impossibility
The key point: an existent thing cannot be brought into existence a second time.
This is self-evident.
3
Examine the temporal attachment
The attribute of creation attaches only at the moment of coming into existence, not during persistence.
Similarly for power and will.
4
Analyze the dilemma
Nasafi ends with a dilemma: if opponents agree with Nazzam, they fall into impossibility; if they disagree, they contradict themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يحقق هذا: أن من كان موصوفا بأحد معنى صفة، كان هو الموصوف بالمعنى الآخر. فإن الكلام لما كان كلاما وصدقا كان الموصوف بكونه متكلما هو الموصوف بكونه صادقا، ولو ادعى إنسان أن المتكلم به غير من هو صادق به نسب إلي الجهل، فكذا ها هنا والذي يدل على صحة ما ذهبنا إليه أن الخلق لو كان هو المخلوق، لكان خلق السماء هو السماء، وخلق الأرض هو الأرض وكذا هذا في جميع أجسام العالم. ثم الله تعالى إنما كان خالقا لها في ابتداء أحوال وجوها لوجود هذا الذات الذي هو خلقه، وهذا الذات بعينه في الزمان الثاني والثالث فصاعدا موجودا فيكون خلقا فيكون الله تعالى خالقا لها في كل حال، فيوصف بأنه يخلق السماوات والأرضين والجبال للحال، ويوجدها كما هو مذهب النظام، والقول بإيجاد الموجود وخلق المخلوق محال، إذ ليس يشك أن ما صار مثبت الكينونة حاصلا في الوجود والآنية، فهو ما دام في تلك الحال فمن المحال أن يوجد ثانيا ولا يلزمنا هذا، وإن كان الإيجاد الذي هو صفة الله تعالى موجودا للحال لأن المخلوق كان مخلوقا لتعلق تلك الصفة به، وهي تعلقت به عند خروجه من العدم إلى الوجود، فأما بعد ذلك فلا تعلق به لاستحالة إيجاد الموجود، هذا كما قلنا جميعا إن الموجود في أول أحوال وجوده مقدور الوجود لتعلق القدرة به في تلك الحال، وفي حالة البقاء لم يبق مقدورا لوجود لأنه لا تعلق له بعد ذلك بها لاستحالة تعلق الوجود بالموجد، وكذا الإرادة مع المراد على هذا خلاف ما ذهبوا إليه، فإن ما هو خلق هذا الجسم الذي كان الله تعالى لأجله خالقا له موجودا إذ هو كان خالقا له لهذا لا لتعلق الخلق به ترفع ذلك بعد ذلك بل لعين هذا الخلق وهو قائم فإن ساعدوا النظام تركوا مذهبهم ووقعوا فيما هو محال وهو القول بإيجاد الموجود، وإن خالفوه ناقضوا
And what confirms this is that whoever is described with one meaning of an attribute, he is the one described with the other meaning. For when speech is speech and truth, the one described as speaking is the one described as truthful. And if someone claimed that the speaker of it is other than the one who is truthful by it, he would be attributed to ignorance. So too here. And what indicates the correctness of what we hold is that if creation were the created, then the creation of the sky would be the sky, and the creation of the earth would be the earth, and likewise for all bodies of the world. Then Allah, exalted, is only Creator of them at the beginning of states and aspects due to the existence of this essence which is His creation, and this same essence in the second and third time and beyond exists, so it would be created, so Allah would be Creator of them in every state, and He would be described as creating the heavens and the earths and the mountains at the present moment, and bringing them into existence, as is the doctrine of al-Nazzam. And the statement of bringing into existence the existent and creating the created is impossible, for there is no doubt that what has become established in existence and now is, as long as it is in that state, it is impossible that it be brought into existence a second time. And this does not follow upon us, even though the bringing into existence which is an attribute of Allah exists presently, because the created was created due to the attachment of that attribute to it, and it attached to it upon its emergence from non-existence to existence. As for after that, there is no attachment to it due to the impossibility of bringing into existence the existent. This is as we all said: the existent in the first state of its existence is possible of existence due to the attachment of power to it in that state, and in the state of remaining it does not remain possible of existence because there is no attachment to it after that to them due to the impossibility of the attachment of existence to the one that is brought into existence. And likewise the will with the willed, contrary to what they held. For what is the creation of this body for which Allah was Creator of it exists, since He was Creator of it for this, not due to the attachment of creation to it being removed after that, but rather for this very creation and it is subsisting. If they assist al-Nazzam, they leave their doctrine and fall into what is impossible, namely the statement of bringing into existence the existent, and if they oppose him, they contradict.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage is a proof and a refutation. The author, Imam Nasafi, begins by establishing a principle: anyone described by one meaning of an attribute (like speech) is also described by the other meaning (like truthfulness). If someone claimed that the speaker of a speech is not the one who is truthful by that speech, that would be ignorance. He then applies this to the topic: if creation (khalq) were identical to the created (makhluq), then the creation of the sky would be the sky itself, and similarly for all bodies — which is absurd. Next, he argues that if creation were the same as the created, then Allah would be described as creating the heavens and earths at every moment, as al-Nazzam held, but this would lead to the impossible notion of creating what already exists (إيجاد الموجود). He explains that the attribute of creation (إيجاد) attaches to the thing upon its emergence from non-existence to existence, but after that it cannot attach again because you cannot bring into existence what already exists. Similarly, power (قدرة) only attaches at the moment of coming into existence, not during persistence. He then addresses the opponents: if they agree with Nazzam, they fall into the impossibility of creating the existent; if they disagree, they contradict themselves.

Key Terms
إيجاد الموجود (ijad al-mawjud)
خلق المخلوق (khalq al-makhluq)
قدرة (qudra)
إرادة (irada)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah creates everything at every moment continuously
Correction: Creation occurs only at the first moment of existence; after that the thing persists without re-creation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does the attribute of creation (إيجاد) attach to a thing?
A) At every moment
B) Upon its emergence from non-existence to existence
C) After it exists
D) Only in the mind

Key Takeaway
An existent thing cannot be created again; creation only occurs when something comes from non-existence to existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You exist right now because Allah created you once from non-existence. Does your life reflect the awe of that one-time miracle, or do you take it for granted as if it were ordinary? What would change if you remembered today that you cannot be 're-created'—this is your only chance?
?
If Allah's power and will attached to you only at the moment of your creation, not during your persistence, does that make you feel more dependent on Him or more abandoned? Examine your heart: do you live as if His power is still actively sustaining you each moment, or as if you now stand on your own?
?
The passage argues against re-creating what already exists. Consider a sin you are struggling with. Do you secretly hope that Allah will 're-create' you into a different person without effort? How does the truth that creation is a one-time act push you to seek real change now rather than wait for a miraculous re-do?

Analogical Proof from Witness to Unseen and Objection from Knowledge
Nasafi gives an analogical proof and an objection from knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi uses analogical reasoning from the witnessed world to establish the distinction between act and object, and the objection raised regarding the union of knowledge and known.

Core Idea
Acts and their objects are distinct; this is clear from the seen world and applies to the unseen.

Imam Nasafi continues defending the distinction between act and object. He presents a key argument from the witnessed realm (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib), using intuitive examples like hitting and being hit. Then he anticipates an objection based on the case of knowledge and known, but the response is cut off in this passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from proof to objection.
The objection is incomplete; the answer is not given here.
2
Understand the analogical proof
The author uses examples like hitting and the hit to show that act and object are distinct. This is known intuitively. Linguists define act as a relation between agent and object.
3
See how the proof extends to the unseen
Since this relation holds in the witnessed world, it must also hold in the unseen, because the meaning is the same: every act requires an agent and an object.
This is a standard kalam argument from shahid to gha'ib.
4
Identify the objection about knowledge
The opponents argue that just as knowledge and the known are united (Allah knows His knowledge), so act and object could be united. Note that the author's response is missing here.
Think about how you would answer this objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالله العصمة عن كل ضلال والذي يدل على صحة ما ذهبنا إليه أن القول باتحاد الفعل والمفعول قول باتحاد الضرب والمضروب والأكل والمأكول والقتل والمقتول، وجميع الأفعال مع مفعولاتها، والقول بذلك مما يعرف فساده بالبديهة، وهذا لأن أهل اللغة قالوا: إن الفعل علاقة بين الفاعل والمفعول لا يكون الفاعل فاعلا إلا باعتبار قيامه به، كما لا يكون المفعول مفعولا إلا بوقوعه عليه كما في الضرب والمضروب، وغير ذلك من النظائر . وإذا ثبت هذا في الشاهد، ثبت في الغائب، لاستوائهما في المعنى لما مر من اقتضاء الفعل فاعلا يقوم به، ومفعولا يتعلق به، فيقع عليه، فيكون الفاعل فاعلا لقيام الفعل به والمفعول مفعولا لوقوع الفعل عليه، كما في القادر والمقدور فإن القادر في الشاهد لما كان قادرا باعتبار قيام القدرة به والمقدور كان مقدورا لتعلق القدرة به لم يجز قادر لم تقم القدرة به و لا مقدور لا تتعلق به القدرة بل هو غير المقدور فكذا هذا. فإن قالوا: أليس أن اتحاد العلم والمعلوم جائز فإن الله تعالى يعلم علمه بعلمه، فكان العلم علما معلوما، فكذا الفعل يجوز أن يكون فعلا ومفعولا، وإن كان
And with Allah is protection from every error. And what indicates the correctness of what we hold is that the statement of the union of the act and the object is a statement of the union of the hitting and the hit, the eating and the eaten, the killing and the killed, and all acts with their objects. And the statement of that is known to be corrupt by intuition. This is because the linguists said: the act is a relation between the agent and the object; the agent is only an agent by virtue of its being performed by him, just as the object is only an object by virtue of its occurring upon it, as in the case of hitting and the hit, and other similar examples. And if this is established in the witnessed (shahid), it is established in the unseen (gha'ib), because they are equivalent in meaning, due to what was mentioned earlier of the act requiring an agent to perform it and an object to which it attaches and upon which it occurs. So the agent is an agent due to the act being performed by him, and the object is an object due to the act occurring upon it, as in the case of the powerful (qadir) and the object of power (maqdur). For the powerful in the witnessed, when he is powerful by virtue of the power being established in him, and the object of power is an object of power due to the attachment of power to it, it is not permissible that there be a powerful one without power established in him, nor an object of power without power attaching to it; rather it is not an object of power. So too this. Then they said: 'Is it not that the union of knowledge and the known is permissible? For Allah knows His knowledge by His knowledge, so knowledge is a known knowledge. So likewise, the act may be permissible to be both act and object, even though...'
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage contains a second proof and then an objection. The author begins by seeking divine protection. He then provides an argument from the witnessed world (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib). He states that the claim that the act and its object are one and the same is as absurd as saying that hitting and the hit are the same, or eating and the eaten. This absurdity is known intuitively (بديهة). Linguists define the act as a relation between agent and object: the agent is agent because the act issues from him, and the object is object because the act occurs upon it. This is evident in examples like striking. Since this is established in the witnessed realm, it must also hold in the unseen, because the meanings are equivalent: every act requires an agent and an object. Just as a powerful being (qadir) is only powerful by power (qudra) subsisting in him, and the object of power (maqdur) is only an object of power by power attaching to it, likewise in this case. Then an objection is raised: the opponents say that the union of knowledge (ilm) and the known (ma'lum) is permissible, because Allah knows His knowledge by His knowledge, so knowledge is known. By analogy, the act could also be both act and object. The passage cuts off at 'even though' (وإن كان), leaving the answer incomplete.

Key Terms
فعل (fi'l)
مفعول (maf'ul)
شاهد (shahid)
غائب (gha'ib)
قادر (qadir)
مقدور (maqdur)
علم (ilm)
معلوم (ma'lum)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because knowledge and the known are united in Allah's case, act and object can also be united, thereby disproving the author's argument.
Correction: The author would likely distinguish between knowledge (which is an attribute) and act (which is a relation), but the full answer is not present here.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to prove that the act and object are distinct?
A) Knowledge and known
B) Hitting and the hit
C) Creator and created
D) Will and willed

Key Takeaway
Acts and their objects are distinct; this is established by intuitive examples and linguistic analysis in the witnessed world, and by analogy it applies to the unseen as well.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you ever consider it as separate from its result? How does distinguishing between your act and its object affect your sense of responsibility for the act itself, regardless of outcome?
?
The passage relies on intuitive (بديهة) knowledge. Think of a time when you knew something was wrong intuitively but allowed a sophisticated argument to convince you otherwise. What was that situation, and how can you guard against such rationalization?

The Critic's Objection and Its Inconsistency
The critic's objection and its internal inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the critic's objection and why his citation of scholars is self-contradictory.

Core Idea
Contradictory sources cannot form a valid objection.

The author begins by rebuking the critic for repeating a flawed analogy after being told that act and object are distinct. The critic then attempts to support his objection by citing two earlier scholars, but the author shows that these scholars themselves disagreed on a key point, undermining the critic's consistency. Additionally, the critic's own argument about the striker and the struck actually reinforces the author's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the author's harsh tone and the critic's attempted objection.
The critic cites two scholars: Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi and Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub.
2
Identify the critic's sources
Note that the critic uses the concepts of 'state' (ḥāl) and 'inherence' (ḥulūl). The author reveals that these two scholars actually disagree on inherence.
Ibn Furak reports that al-Qalansi permitted inherence while al-Ash'ari did not.
3
Analyze the critic's own argument
The critic argues that the 'struck' becomes struck only after an act, implying a separate meaning. This argument actually shows that act and object are distinct.
The critic fails to see that his own reasoning supports the author's position.
4
Evaluate the flaw
The critic's objection fails because his sources contradict each other, and his own logic supports the distinction he opposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوجب نوع خيال، فأما إذا كنا أبينا عليك القول باتحاد الفعل والمفعول ثم اشتغلت بإيراد مثل هذا النظير، كان ذلك منك جهلا بمواضع الإلزام والالتزام، وبالله العصمة والنجاة. ثم إن هذا المشنع رأى الاعتراض بفصل الحال والحلول من عبد القاهر البغدادي، والقول باستحالة الحلول على الأعراض من أبي منصور بن أيوب، فكان ضدا لكلا الصالحين ولم يعلم لغفلته أن بينهما تناقضا على أن ابن فورك حكى خلافا بين الأشعري والقلانسي فقال: جوز القلانسي القول بأن العرض حل في الجسم، والأشعري لا يجوز ذلك، وإذا كان الأشعري لا يجوز ذلك كيف يقال الحال والحلول شيء واحد، ثم قال: هذا القائل إنما يكون الضرب غير المضروب لوجود المضروب غير مضروب، فلما صار مضروبا بعد أن لم يكن مضروبا افتقر إلى معنى لأجله كان مضروبا، ويستحيل وجود المخلوق مع عدم الخلق، فكان هذا هو الفرق بين البابين نقل هذا بعينه عن كلام عبد القاهر. والجواب عنه: قلنا: إنك لا تزال تأتي بكلام يوجب زيادة التعجب من غفلتك، أليس أن المعتزلة يقولون في مسألة الصفات: إن العلم إنما كان في الشاهد معنى وراء العالم لوجودنا إياه مرة عالما ومرة غير عالم، والله تعالى يستحيل عليه وجوده غير عالم فلم يوجد دليل كون علمه معنى وراء العلم، واعتمد على هذا الكلام الكعبي وغيره في الفرق بين الشاهد والغائب، أكان ذلك صحيحا؟ فإن قال: نعم. ترك مذهبه في الصفات، وإن قال كان ذلك فاسدا قيل وما وجه فساده؟ فإن قال: لأن الدليل قام على أن العلم معنى وراءه فيكون في الغائب كما هو في الشاهد، قلنا: والدلائل أيضا قامت هنا على أن الخلق غير المخلوق. ثم نقول له: أليس أن أصحاب الصفات أجابوا عن كلام المعتزلة وقالوا: إذا ثبت في موضع وهو الشاهد أن العلم معنى وراء العالم لما قام من دليل وجودنا للعالم مرة غير عالم ثبت ذلك في المواضع أجمع، وإن لم ينتقل هذا الدليل إليه، لأن نقل الدليل مع المدلول أينما دار ليس بشرط، كما أن الدليل لما قام أن السواد معنى وراء الأسود، والبياض معنى وراء الأبيض، والحركة معنى وراء المتحرك، لوجودنا الأسود مرة غير أسود وكذا الأبيض والمتحرك نحكم بكون سواد الغراب والفأر، وبياض الثلج والكافور، وحركة النار والأفلاك معاني وراء الذات، وإن لم يوجد نقل الدليل، فإنا لا نجد هذه الذوات متعرية عن هذه الصفات، فكذا ها هنا، لما ثبت أن الفعل في الجملة غير المفعول لثبوت دليل الانفكاك ثبت ذلك في كل فعل ولم يشترك نقل الدليل وإلا فما الفرق.
It necessitates a kind of imagination. But if we have forbidden you the claim of the union of the act and the object, and then you occupied yourself with presenting such an analogy, that is from you ignorance of the places of obligation and commitment. And by God is protection and salvation. Then this critic saw the objection by the chapter of the state (ḥāl) and inherence (ḥulūl) from ʿAbd al-Qāhir al-Baghdādī, and the claim of the impossibility of inherence in accidents from Abū Manṣūr ibn Ayyūb, and he was contradictory to both of the righteous ones, and did not know due to his heedlessness that between them is a contradiction, and that Ibn Fūrak narrated a disagreement between al-Ashʿarī and al-Qalānisī and said: al-Qalānisī permitted the claim that the accident inheres in the body, and al-Ashʿarī does not permit that. And if al-Ashʿarī does not permit that, how can it be said that the state and inherence are one thing? Then he said: this speaker only considers the struck to be other than the striker because he finds the striker not struck. Then when it became struck after it was not struck, it needed a meaning for which it was struck, and it is impossible for the created to exist with the absence of creation. So this is the difference between the two chapters. He transmitted this exactly from the speech of ʿAbd al-Qāhir. And the answer to him: we said: you continue to bring speech that necessitates increasing wonder at your heedlessness. Is it not that the Muʿtazila say in the question of attributes: knowledge in the witnessed (shāhid) is a meaning beyond the knower because we find him sometimes knowing and sometimes not knowing, and for God Most High it is impossible for Him to exist not knowing, so there is no proof that His knowledge is a meaning beyond knowledge. And al-Kaʿbī and others relied on this speech in the difference between the witnessed and the absent (ghāʾib). Is that correct? If he says: yes, he abandons his school in attributes. And if he says: that is invalid, it is said: and what is the face of its invalidity? If he says: because the proof is established that knowledge is a meaning beyond it, so it is in the absent as it is in the witnessed. We say: and the proofs are also established here that creation is other than the created. Then we say to him: is it not that the proponents of attributes answered the speech of the Muʿtazila and said: if it is established in a place, namely the witnessed, that knowledge is a meaning beyond the knower, due to the proof that we find the knower sometimes not knowing, it is established in all places, even if this proof does not transfer to it, because transferring the proof with the thing proved wherever it goes is not a condition. Just as when the proof is established that blackness is a meaning beyond the black thing, and whiteness a meaning beyond the white thing, and movement a meaning beyond the moving thing, because we find the black thing sometimes not black, and likewise the white and the moving, we judge that the blackness of the crow and the mouse, the whiteness of snow and camphor, and the movement of fire and the spheres are meanings beyond the essence, even if no transfer of the proof exists, for we do not find these essences divested of these attributes. So here as well, when it is established that the act in general is other than the object due to the proof of separability (infikāk), it is established for every act, and the transfer of the proof is not required. Otherwise, what is the difference?
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the first part of the author's response. The author begins by rebuking the critic who, despite being told that act and object are distinct, brings an alleged inconsistency from the views of earlier scholars. The critic adduces the concept of 'state' (ḥāl) and 'inherence' (ḥulūl) from ʿAbd al-Qāhir al-Baghdādī and Abū Manṣūr ibn Ayyūb, claiming a contradiction. The author then shows that these two scholars themselves disagree (as per Ibn Fūrak) on whether an accident can inhere in a body, so their positions cannot be used consistently. The critic then argues that 'struck' and 'striker' are distinct because of a change of state, and analogously act and object must be distinct. But this very point supports the author's position. The author's answer begins by drawing a parallel with the Muʿtazila argument on attributes: they argued that knowledge in the witnessed world is a separate meaning because the knower fluctuates between knowing and not knowing, but since God never lacks knowledge, that proof does not apply. The author forces the critic to either accept the Muʿtazila reasoning (abandoning his own school) or reject it. If he rejects it, he must show the flaw. The author then clarifies that the proponents of attributes replied to the Muʿtazila by stating that once a proof is established in one case, it applies universally without requiring direct transfer of the same evidence. Examples are given: blackness, whiteness, and movement are proven to be meanings distinct from their subjects even when the subjects are never found without them. Similarly, the proof that act is distinct from object, once established by the possibility of separation (infikāk) in some cases, applies to all acts, even if not directly observed in every case. Thus the critic's objection is invalid.

Key Terms
فعل
مفعول
حال
حلول
معنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Citing two scholars with different views on a related topic creates a valid objection against the author.
Correction: The author shows that the scholars themselves disagree, so they cannot be used as a unified objection; in fact, the critic's argument backfires.

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Furak report about al-Ash'ari and al-Qalansi?
A) They agreed on the permissibility of inherence.
B) al-Qalansi permitted inherence of accidents while al-Ash'ari did not.
C) Both prohibited inherence.
D) The report is not mentioned.

Key Takeaway
The critic's objection is self-defeating because his sources contradict each other and his own reasoning supports the author's position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a truth because you assumed two scholars held the same view, only to later learn they disagreed? What does this lesson teach you about the danger of heedlessness in seeking knowledge?
?
The critic’s own argument proved the opposite of what he intended. Is there a belief you hold that, if thought through carefully, might actually undermine your own position? Are you ready to be corrected by evidence?
?
The author says, 'By God is protection and salvation.' Where in your life are you depending on your own reasoning rather than seeking God's protection from error? Will you pause and ask Him to guide you to consistency and truth?

The Author's Refutation Using Universal Proof
The author's answer using Mu'tazila analogy and universal proof principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the Mu'tazila argument on attributes to refute the critic and establish the universal proof for act-object distinction.

Core Idea
A proof once established applies universally, even without direct transfer of evidence.

The author now delivers his answer. He rebukes the critic's continued heedlessness and draws a powerful analogy to the Mu'tazila argument about God's attributes. The Mu'tazila claimed that knowledge is a meaning separate from the knower in the witnessed world, but they denied it for God. The author forces the critic to accept the logical consequence: either abandon his school or reject the Mu'tazila reasoning. Then he clarifies that once a proof (like the possibility of separation) is established in some cases, it applies to all similar cases, as illustrated by blackness, whiteness, and movement.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the author's strong language and the analogical argument.
The author begins with a rebuke before presenting the analogy.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila analogy
The author compares the critic's position to the Mu'tazila argument on attributes, showing inconsistency.
The Mu'tazila argued that knowledge is a separate meaning in the witnessed world but not for God. The author forces a choice.
3
Grasp the universal proof principle
The author explains that proofs don't need to be transferred separately; a single proof suffices for all instances.
The proponents of attributes used this principle to counter the Mu'tazila.
4
Apply the principle to act-object
See how the examples of blackness and movement demonstrate that act is distinct from object in all cases, even without direct observation.
The proof of separability (daliil al-infikak) is sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوجب نوع خيال، فأما إذا كنا أبينا عليك القول باتحاد الفعل والمفعول ثم اشتغلت بإيراد مثل هذا النظير، كان ذلك منك جهلا بمواضع الإلزام والالتزام، وبالله العصمة والنجاة. ثم إن هذا المشنع رأى الاعتراض بفصل الحال والحلول من عبد القاهر البغدادي، والقول باستحالة الحلول على الأعراض من أبي منصور بن أيوب، فكان ضدا لكلا الصالحين ولم يعلم لغفلته أن بينهما تناقضا على أن ابن فورك حكى خلافا بين الأشعري والقلانسي فقال: جوز القلانسي القول بأن العرض حل في الجسم، والأشعري لا يجوز ذلك، وإذا كان الأشعري لا يجوز ذلك كيف يقال الحال والحلول شيء واحد، ثم قال: هذا القائل إنما يكون الضرب غير المضروب لوجود المضروب غير مضروب، فلما صار مضروبا بعد أن لم يكن مضروبا افتقر إلى معنى لأجله كان مضروبا، ويستحيل وجود المخلوق مع عدم الخلق، فكان هذا هو الفرق بين البابين نقل هذا بعينه عن كلام عبد القاهر. والجواب عنه: قلنا: إنك لا تزال تأتي بكلام يوجب زيادة التعجب من غفلتك، أليس أن المعتزلة يقولون في مسألة الصفات: إن العلم إنما كان في الشاهد معنى وراء العالم لوجودنا إياه مرة عالما ومرة غير عالم، والله تعالى يستحيل عليه وجوده غير عالم فلم يوجد دليل كون علمه معنى وراء العلم، واعتمد على هذا الكلام الكعبي وغيره في الفرق بين الشاهد والغائب، أكان ذلك صحيحا؟ فإن قال: نعم. ترك مذهبه في الصفات، وإن قال كان ذلك فاسدا قيل وما وجه فساده؟ فإن قال: لأن الدليل قام على أن العلم معنى وراءه فيكون في الغائب كما هو في الشاهد، قلنا: والدلائل أيضا قامت هنا على أن الخلق غير المخلوق. ثم نقول له: أليس أن أصحاب الصفات أجابوا عن كلام المعتزلة وقالوا: إذا ثبت في موضع وهو الشاهد أن العلم معنى وراء العالم لما قام من دليل وجودنا للعالم مرة غير عالم ثبت ذلك في المواضع أجمع، وإن لم ينتقل هذا الدليل إليه، لأن نقل الدليل مع المدلول أينما دار ليس بشرط، كما أن الدليل لما قام أن السواد معنى وراء الأسود، والبياض معنى وراء الأبيض، والحركة معنى وراء المتحرك، لوجودنا الأسود مرة غير أسود وكذا الأبيض والمتحرك نحكم بكون سواد الغراب والفأر، وبياض الثلج والكافور، وحركة النار والأفلاك معاني وراء الذات، وإن لم يوجد نقل الدليل، فإنا لا نجد هذه الذوات متعرية عن هذه الصفات، فكذا ها هنا، لما ثبت أن الفعل في الجملة غير المفعول لثبوت دليل الانفكاك ثبت ذلك في كل فعل ولم يشترك نقل الدليل وإلا فما الفرق.
It necessitates a kind of imagination. But if we have forbidden you the claim of the union of the act and the object, and then you occupied yourself with presenting such an analogy, that is from you ignorance of the places of obligation and commitment. And by God is protection and salvation. Then this critic saw the objection by the chapter of the state (ḥāl) and inherence (ḥulūl) from ʿAbd al-Qāhir al-Baghdādī, and the claim of the impossibility of inherence in accidents from Abū Manṣūr ibn Ayyūb, and he was contradictory to both of the righteous ones, and did not know due to his heedlessness that between them is a contradiction, and that Ibn Fūrak narrated a disagreement between al-Ashʿarī and al-Qalānisī and said: al-Qalānisī permitted the claim that the accident inheres in the body, and al-Ashʿarī does not permit that. And if al-Ashʿarī does not permit that, how can it be said that the state and inherence are one thing? Then he said: this speaker only considers the struck to be other than the striker because he finds the striker not struck. Then when it became struck after it was not struck, it needed a meaning for which it was struck, and it is impossible for the created to exist with the absence of creation. So this is the difference between the two chapters. He transmitted this exactly from the speech of ʿAbd al-Qāhir. And the answer to him: we said: you continue to bring speech that necessitates increasing wonder at your heedlessness. Is it not that the Muʿtazila say in the question of attributes: knowledge in the witnessed (shāhid) is a meaning beyond the knower because we find him sometimes knowing and sometimes not knowing, and for God Most High it is impossible for Him to exist not knowing, so there is no proof that His knowledge is a meaning beyond knowledge. And al-Kaʿbī and others relied on this speech in the difference between the witnessed and the absent (ghāʾib). Is that correct? If he says: yes, he abandons his school in attributes. And if he says: that is invalid, it is said: and what is the face of its invalidity? If he says: because the proof is established that knowledge is a meaning beyond it, so it is in the absent as it is in the witnessed. We say: and the proofs are also established here that creation is other than the created. Then we say to him: is it not that the proponents of attributes answered the speech of the Muʿtazila and said: if it is established in a place, namely the witnessed, that knowledge is a meaning beyond the knower, due to the proof that we find the knower sometimes not knowing, it is established in all places, even if this proof does not transfer to it, because transferring the proof with the thing proved wherever it goes is not a condition. Just as when the proof is established that blackness is a meaning beyond the black thing, and whiteness a meaning beyond the white thing, and movement a meaning beyond the moving thing, because we find the black thing sometimes not black, and likewise the white and the moving, we judge that the blackness of the crow and the mouse, the whiteness of snow and camphor, and the movement of fire and the spheres are meanings beyond the essence, even if no transfer of the proof exists, for we do not find these essences divested of these attributes. So here as well, when it is established that the act in general is other than the object due to the proof of separability (infikāk), it is established for every act, and the transfer of the proof is not required. Otherwise, what is the difference?
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the first part of the author's response. The author begins by rebuking the critic who, despite being told that act and object are distinct, brings an alleged inconsistency from the views of earlier scholars. The critic adduces the concept of 'state' (ḥāl) and 'inherence' (ḥulūl) from ʿAbd al-Qāhir al-Baghdādī and Abū Manṣūr ibn Ayyūb, claiming a contradiction. The author then shows that these two scholars themselves disagree (as per Ibn Fūrak) on whether an accident can inhere in a body, so their positions cannot be used consistently. The critic then argues that 'struck' and 'striker' are distinct because of a change of state, and analogously act and object must be distinct. But this very point supports the author's position. The author's answer begins by drawing a parallel with the Muʿtazila argument on attributes: they argued that knowledge in the witnessed world is a separate meaning because the knower fluctuates between knowing and not knowing, but since God never lacks knowledge, that proof does not apply. The author forces the critic to either accept the Muʿtazila reasoning (abandoning his own school) or reject it. If he rejects it, he must show the flaw. The author then clarifies that the proponents of attributes replied to the Muʿtazila by stating that once a proof is established in one case, it applies universally without requiring direct transfer of the same evidence. Examples are given: blackness, whiteness, and movement are proven to be meanings distinct from their subjects even when the subjects are never found without them. Similarly, the proof that act is distinct from object, once established by the possibility of separation (infikāk) in some cases, applies to all acts, even if not directly observed in every case. Thus the critic's objection is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of act-object distinction must be directly observed in every case to be valid.
Correction: Once a proof is established in some cases (e.g., where act and object separate), it applies to all similar cases by virtue of the general principle, even if direct observation is not possible.

A) The critic has no evidence.
B) The same logic used by the Mu'tazila for attributes can be applied to show act is distinct from object.
C) The critic is ignorant of the Qur'an.
D) The author concedes the objection.

Key Takeaway
The author establishes that the proof of act-object distinction is universal, using the same reasoning that proponents of attributes used against the Mu'tazila.

?

Distinction Between Unity of Type and Genus in Acts
Author distinguishes unity types and gives proof from effectiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between unity of type (ittiḥād al-nawʿ) and unity of genus (ittiḥād al-jins) in the context of acts and objects, and how the proof from effectiveness establishes their distinction.

Core Idea
Effectiveness proves distinction: the effective cannot be identical to the affected.

Having previously argued that act and object are distinct, the author now anticipates an objection based on the unity of attributes. He sharpens his analysis by distinguishing between unity of type and unity of genus, and then introduces a decisive proof from effectiveness. This lesson unpacks that subtle argument and shows why the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction of types versus genera
The author contrasts two kinds of unity: unity of type (e.g., two instances of knowledge are the same type) and unity of genus (e.g., act and object belong to the same broad category but are distinct). Notice how the former applies to attributes and the latter to acts.
Unity of type implies sameness of essence; unity of genus only implies shared category.
2
Identify the critic's objection
The objection targets the author's earlier apparent claim that act and object are one. The author clarifies that the objection only hits the superficial wording, not his deeper meaning.
3
Grasp the proof from effectiveness
The author argues that the act is effective (muʾaththir) in producing the object, just as power (qudra) is effective in producing its object. Since a thing cannot affect itself, the effective and affected must be distinct.
This is a self-evident logical principle.
4
See why the objection fails
Even if the previous proof (based on separability) were absent, this new proof from effectiveness stands independently. The objection does not apply to this deeper argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير أن هناك بين ما قام دليل كونه معنى وراء الذات اتحاد النوع عند اختلاف المحل، وفيما نحن فيه اتحاد الجنس عند اختلاف المحل، وذا محال يوجب التفرقة بينهما على أن هذا الاعتراض إنما يرد على ظاهر كلامنا، فأما ما بينا من المعنى الموجب أن يكون الفعل غير المفعول كما يوجب أن تكون القدرة غير المقدور، وهو كون الفعل مؤثرا في المفعول كالقدرة سواء، والمؤثر غير المتأثر إذ الشيء لا يؤثر في ذاته، فلا يرد عليه الاعتراض لأن ما ذكر من دليل المغايرة وإن لم يوجد فقد وجد دليل غيره وهو ما ذكرنا.
However, between what is established by proof of being a meaning beyond the essence, there is unity of type (ittiḥād al-nawʿ) with difference of place, while in our case there is unity of genus (ittiḥād al-jins) with difference of place, and that is impossible, necessitating a distinction between them. Moreover, this objection only applies to the apparent meaning of our speech. As for what we have explained of the meaning that necessitates that the act is other than the object, just as it necessitates that power (qudra) is other than the object of power (maqdūr), namely that the act is effective (muʾaththir) in the object like power equally, and the effective is other than the affected, since a thing does not affect itself, so the objection does not apply to it, because what has been mentioned of the proof of difference (al-mughāyara) – even if it does not exist – another proof has been found, namely what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This passage provides a second, more precise argument for the distinction between act and object. The author first notes a crucial difference between the case of attributes (where the proof shows that attribute is a meaning beyond the essence) and the present case of act and object. In the attributes case, the attribute and the essence share a unity of type (ittiḥād al-nawʿ) across different loci – e.g., all instances of knowledge are of the same type. But in the act-object case, the relationship is one of unity of genus (ittiḥād al-jins) – i.e., act and object belong to the same broad category but are distinct in their specific reality. This difference, the author says, actually necessitates a distinction between them, not a union. He then addresses the critic's objection more directly: the objection only applies to the superficial understanding of his earlier words. The deeper proof he now offers is based on the concept of effectiveness (taʾthīr). Just as power (qudra) is distinct from its object (maqdūr) because power is what brings about the object, so too the act (fiʿl) is distinct from the object (mafʿūl) because the act is effective (muʾaththir) in producing the object, and the effective cannot be the same as the affected – a thing cannot act upon itself. This is a self-evident logical principle. Even if the earlier proof (based on separability) were not applicable in all cases, this new proof from effectiveness stands independently and suffices to establish the distinction. Thus the critic's objection is rebutted.

Key Terms
اتحاد النوع
اتحاد الجنس
مؤثر
متأثر
قدرة
مقدور
مغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The distinction between act and object relies solely on the proof of separability (i.e., they can be conceptually separated).
Correction: The author provides an additional independent proof from effectiveness: the act is effective (muʾaththir) and the object is affected, and a thing cannot affect itself, so they must be distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the objection invalid against his argument from effectiveness?
A) Because the proof from separability is always present
B) Because the effective and the affected cannot be identical
C) Because the critic misunderstood the definition of act
D) Because the proof from effectiveness is weaker than the earlier proof

Key Takeaway
The act and object are distinct because the act is effective and the object is affected, and a thing cannot be effective in itself.

Can you recall a specific situation where treating two distinct things as identical led to a misunderstanding? How might the distinction between type and genus change your approach?
?
The passage argues that a thing cannot affect itself. In your spiritual journey, where might you be trying to change yourself by your own power, forgetting that true effectiveness comes from God alone? What is one tangible step you can take today to acknowledge that only God is the ultimate effective agent?
?
Think of a time when you separated an action from its result—maybe for your own growth. How does the proof from effectiveness (that the effective is other than the affected) deepen your understanding that your actions are distinct from their outcomes? How does this affect your sense of responsibility and reliance on God?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Analogy on Acts and Effects
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy between hitting (ضرب) and act (فعل) fails to disprove that human actions are created by God.

Core Idea
A thing can be both an act (from human) and an effect (from God).

This lesson examines a key objection raised by the Mu'tazila against the Maturidi position that God creates human actions. The opponent draws an analogy from the physical world, but Imam Nasafi shows how the analogy breaks down due to the specific nature of the terms involved. Understanding this refutation clarifies the compatibility of human agency and divine creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the opponent's claim and Nasafi's response.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the opponent's analogy
The Mu'tazilite argues: just as hitting (ضرب) cannot be the same as the hit object (مضروب), an act (فعل) cannot be the same as its effect (مفعول). Understand the logical structure of this analogy.
3
Analyze Nasafi's refutation
Nasafi points out that the analogy is flawed because 'hitting' is a specific kind of act (causing pain) and an accident that cannot receive pain, while 'act' is a general term. Therefore, a human act can be both an act (from the human) and an effect (from God) without contradiction.
Focus on the distinction between general and specific terms.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider how this refutation preserves the Maturidi view that human actions are created by God while still allowing human agency. This is a key point in Kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال هذا القائل: لما كان المضروب غير الضرب استحال وجود مضروب يكون ضربا فلو كان المفعول غير الفعل على قياس الضرب لاستحال وجود مفعول هو فعل، ولما كان فعل الإنسان مفعولا لله تعالى عندنا وعندهم بطل قولهم بوجوب كون المفعول غير الفعل. فنقول في جواب ذلك: إن هذا منك تمويه وعدول عن سنن الكلام. وبيان ذلك في كل فعل في الشاهد مع مفعوله، وبينا المعنى في ذلك أن المؤثر ينبغي أن يكون غير المتأثر، واستوى الضرب والمضروب مع التكوين والمكون والإيجاد والموجود فيستويان. فبعد ذلك ثبوت الفرق بين الضرب والفعل: أن الضرب لا يكون مضروبا والفعل يكون مفعولا لا يوجب بطلان ما أثبتنا من التسوية على أن كل فعل في الشاهد مفعول الله تعالى ضربا كان أو غير ضرب فإنه مخلوقه غير أن اسم الفعل والمفعول عام فيتناول كلا والضرب اسم لفعل خاص وهو الإيلام وهو عرض والعرض لا يقبل الألم بإيلام غيره، وهو يقبل إيجاد غيره إياه فكان موجودا وكان مفعولا ولم يكن مضروبا لهذا.
Then this speaker said: Since the hit object is not the hitting, it is impossible for a hit object to be a hitting. So if the effect were not the act, by analogy with hitting, it would be impossible for an effect to be an act. And since the act of man is an effect of God—according to us and them—their statement that the effect must be other than the act is invalid. We say in reply to that: This from you is obfuscation and deviation from the path of discourse. The demonstration of this is in every act in the witnessed world with its effect, and we have explained the meaning in that: the agent must be other than the recipient, and hitting and the hit object are equivalent with bringing into existence and the brought into existence and creating and the created, so they are equivalent. After that, the proof of the difference between hitting and act: that hitting is not a hit object, and the act is an effect—this does not necessitate the invalidity of what we have established of equivalence. Rather, every act in the witnessed world is an effect of God, whether hitting or otherwise, for it is His creation, but the name 'act' and 'effect' is general, encompassing both, while 'hitting' is the name of a specific act, which is causing pain, and it is an accident, and an accident does not receive pain from causing pain of another, and it accepts the bringing into existence of another of it, so it was existent and was an effect and was not a hit object because of this.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. The Mu'tazilite opponent argues by analogy: just as hitting (ضرب) cannot be the same as the hit object (مضروب), so an act (فعل) cannot be the same as its effect (مفعول). Since human acts are effects of God, the act and effect must be distinct, contradicting the Maturidi view. The author responds by pointing out that the opponent's reasoning is deceptive. He explains that in the witnessed world, every act is related to its effect, but the agent must be distinct from the recipient. However, the distinction between 'hitting' and 'hit object' is due to the specific nature of 'hitting' as an accident that causes pain and cannot receive pain; thus it cannot be a hit object, but it can be created and thereby be an effect of God. Thus the analogy fails because 'act' is a general term that includes both created things and actions, while 'hitting' is a specific type of action. The conclusion is that a single entity can be both an act (from the human) and an effect (from God) without contradiction.

Key Terms
ضرب
مضروب
فعل
مفعول
إيلام
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is an action of a human, it cannot also be created by God.
Correction: The author explains that a human act can be both an act (from the human in terms of acquisition) and an effect (created by God). The analogy fails due to the specific nature of 'hitting'.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Nasafi, why does the analogy between 'hitting' (ضرب) and 'act' (فعل) fail?
A) Because 'hitting' and 'act' are synonymous terms.
B) Because 'hitting' is a specific accident that cannot receive pain, unlike 'act' which is general.
C) Because the opponent's argument is based on a false premise about divine attributes.
D) Because the Mu'tazila do not accept that God creates human actions.

Key Takeaway
The analogy between hitting and act fails because 'hitting' is a specific accident that cannot be a hit object, while 'act' is general, allowing a human act to be both an act and an effect of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every action you perform is also created by God, how does that change the way you see your own choices and your reliance on Him? Are you truly accountable for your deeds, and how does this teaching affect your sense of responsibility before God?
?
When you reflect on your own actions, do you tend to attribute them entirely to yourself, forgetting that they are also created by Allah? How can the truth from this passage—that an act can be both human and divine—reshape your daily gratitude and humility?
?
The Mu'tazilite analogy seemed logical on the surface, but it was refuted because it overlooked a crucial distinction. Consider an area in your own understanding of Islam where you might be drawing a false analogy. Are you willing to examine your assumptions and submit to the deeper teaching, even if it challenges your initial reasoning?

The Mu'tazila Objection to Dual Agency
The Mu'tazila analogize one act from two agents to one speech from two speakers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that a single act cannot belong to two agents, based on the analogy of speech.

Core Idea
One act from two agents is as impossible as one speech from two speakers.

In the debate on human action, the Mu'tazila raised a logical objection: if an act is both created by God and performed by the servant, it would be one act from two agents, which they deemed impossible. They supported this with an analogy to speech, which cannot be attributed to two speakers. This lesson presents that objection and its underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. This passage sets up the parallel between act of two agents and speech of two speakers.
2
Identify the parallel
Notice the parallel: just as a single speech cannot be from two speakers, a single act cannot be from two agents. Why do the Mu'tazila think this is a valid analogy?
3
Understand the objection
The Mu'tazila claim that if an act is created by God and performed by the servant, it would be one act from two agents, which is impossible. This is the core of their argument against the Maturidi position.
This objection will be answered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نظير هذا على قول من يسلم منكم أن العبد له فعل في الفرق بين فعل الفاعلين وكلام المتكلمين، فإن فعل العبد لما كان مخلوقا كان خلقا وفعلا الله تعالى فيكون فعلا الله تعالى وللعبد فيكون فعلا لفاعلين. فتدعى المعتزلة أن هذا محال، ويعتبرونه بكلام واحد أنه لا يكون كلاما لمتكلمين ويستحيل أن يكون ذلك فكذا هذا. أجابهم من سلم منكم أن للعبد فعلا وهو عبد الله بن سعيد القطان وأبو العباس القلانسي فقال: بأن الفعل اسم عام لكل ما هو مقدور مفعول، وقد ثبت أن فعلنا مقدور الله تعالى ومخلوقه فكان خلقه وفعله، والكلام اسم لفعل خاص وهو المعنى المنافي للسكوت والخرس والآفة، وهو ينافي سكوت المحل الذي قام به آفته ويستحيل قيامه بمحلين لنفي السكوت والآفة عن محلين فلم يكن كلاما لمحلين. فأما الفعل فهو اسم لما يقع بالقدرة وهو مقدور الله تعالى ومقدور العبد، فكان فعلا لهما، حتى إن الكلام لما كان يقع بقدرة الله خلقا وقدرة العبد كسبا كان أيضا فعلا لفاعلين أما لم يكن كلاهما لمتكلمين لما مر، فكذا الضرب عندنا لا يكون مضروبا لأنه اسم لنوع فعل وهو الإيلام والمضروب اسم للمتألم والضرب لا يقبل الألم فلم يكن مشروبا. فأما الضرب فإنه يقبل التخليق فكان مخلوقا مفعولا، فكان من حيث إنه فعل للعبد مفعولا لله تعالى، فأما من حيث إنه ضرب لم يكن مضروبا. ثم نحن إنما ندعي إحالة كون الفعل مفعولا لمن هو فعله، وإحالة كون المفعول فعلا لمن هو مفعوله وننكر اتحاد الفعل والمفعول وفيها ألزمت الأمر بخلافه، فإن فعل العبد عندنا كان مفعولا الله تعالى لا للعبد، وكان هو فعلا للعبد لا الله تعالى فمن كان هو فعلا له لم يكن مفعولا، ومن كان مفعولا له لم يكن فعلا له ونحن لا ننكر هذا وإنما ننكر اتحاد الفعل والمفعول بجهة واحدة وهو إضافته.
Then the parallel to this, according to the statement of one among you who concedes that the servant has an act, regarding the distinction between the act of two agents and the speech of two speakers: for the act of the servant, when it is created, is creation and act of God, so it is an act of God and of the servant, thus it is an act of two agents. The Mu'tazila claim this is impossible, and they analogize it to a single speech not being speech of two speakers, and that it is impossible; so likewise this. Those among you who concede that the servant has an act—namely Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan and Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi—replied to them, saying: 'Fi'l is a general name for everything that is possible and effected, and it has been established that our act is possible to God and His creation, so it is His creation and act. Kalam is a name for a specific act, namely the meaning opposed to silence, muteness, and defect, and it opposes the silence of the locus in which it inheres, and it is impossible for it to subsist in two loci because silence and defect are negated from two loci; thus it is not speech for two loci. As for fi'l, it is a name for what occurs by power, and it is possible to God and possible to the servant, so it is an act of both. Even kalam, when it occurs by God's power as creation and by the servant's power as acquisition, is also an act of two agents, but it is not speech of two speakers for the reason mentioned. So similarly, hitting according to us is not a hit object, because it is the name of a type of act, which is causing pain, and the hit object is the name of the one who suffers pain, and hitting does not receive pain, so it is not a hit object. As for hitting, it accepts being created, so it is created and effected; thus it is, insofar as it is an act of the servant, an effect of God; but insofar as it is hitting, it is not a hit object.' Then we only claim the impossibility of the act being an effect for the one for whom it is an act, and the impossibility of the effect being an act for the one for whom it is an effect, and we deny the union of act and effect. In this, they have imposed the contrary, for the act of the servant according to us is an effect of God, not of the servant, and it is an act of the servant, not of God. So that which is an act of Him is not an effect of Him, and that which is an effect of Him is not an act of Him. We do not deny this; we only deny the union of act and effect from one aspect, which is its attribution.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage develops the argument with a parallel from the debate on human action. The Mu'tazila object that one act cannot be from two agents, analogizing it to speech which cannot be from two speakers. The author cites the response of Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan and Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi, who distinguish between 'fi'l' (action) and 'kalam' (speech). 'Fi'l' is a general term for anything brought about by power, so it can be attributed to both God (as creator) and the servant (as acquirer). In contrast, 'kalam' is a specific type of act that requires a single locus because it negates silence and defect; thus it cannot be from two speakers. Similarly, 'hitting' (ضرب) is a specific type of act that causes pain and cannot receive pain, so it cannot be a hit object, but as a general act it can be created and thus be an effect of God. The author concludes by clarifying his own position: he denies that the same entity can be both an act and an effect from the same aspect, but affirms that something can be an act of the servant and an effect of God from different aspects.

Key Terms
فعل
كلام
فاعلين
متكلمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazila objection is based on divine omnipotence or scriptural evidence.
Correction: Actually, the objection is purely logical: they argue that one act cannot have two agents, using the analogy of speech, which is a specific type of act that cannot be shared.

Checkpoint
What analogy do the Mu'tazila use to argue against one act from two agents?
A) Fire and heat
B) Speech from two speakers
C) Creation from nothing
D) Motion and rest

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that a single act cannot belong to two agents, using the analogy of speech which cannot be shared between two speakers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every action you perform is also an act of Allah, how does that change your sense of ownership over your sins? When you disobey, do you feel the weight of your own choice, or do you excuse yourself because 'Allah created it'? What would it mean to take full responsibility without denying Allah's creation?
?
The Mu'tazila insisted that a single act cannot belong to two agents. But you live as if your actions are entirely your own. Are you comfortable with the idea that your most private thoughts, your intentions, your very movements are simultaneously willed by Allah? Does that unsettle you, or does it bring you peace? Examine your heart: is there any part of your life you wish Allah were not involved in?
?
The analogy of speech is used to show impossibility. But consider: when you recite the Qur'an, is it your speech or Allah's? If the words are Allah's but your tongue moves by His power, are you truly a speaker? This lesson forces you to consider how you relate to every word of worship you utter. Are you merely a vessel, or an agent? How does that answer affect your sincerity?

Al-Qattan and al-Qalanisi's Distinction Between Fi'l and Kalam
Al-Qattan and al-Qalanisi define fi'l and kalam to show fi'l can be from two agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Qattan and al-Qalanisi distinguish between general action (fi'l) and specific action (kalam) to answer the Mu'tazila objection.

Core Idea
Fi'l is a general term for anything brought about by power; kalam is a specific act requiring a unique locus.

In response to the Mu'tazila analogy, scholars like Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan and Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi pointed out that not all acts are like speech. They explained that 'fi'l' is a broad term applicable to anything that occurs by power, while 'kalam' is a specific type of act with special restrictions. This distinction allows them to affirm that an act can be attributed to both God and the servant without contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the response
Read the Arabic and translation of the response. Notice how they start by defining 'fi'l' and 'kalam' differently.
2
Compare the definitions
Fi'l is defined as a general name for everything possible and effected. Kalam is a specific act that negates silence and defect, requiring a single locus.
This difference is key to their argument.
3
Apply the distinction
Because fi'l is general, it can be attributed to multiple agents in different respects. Kalam cannot. Even kalam, as an act, can be from two agents but not as speech.
The hitting example illustrates that the same entity can be an act from one perspective and not from another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
