Three Theories of Annihilation and the Mu'tazilite Claim
Nasafi enumerates three theories of annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main theories of how annihilation (fanā') occurs according to different Islamic theological schools, especially the Mu'tazilite claim that a newly arriving contrary accident causes destruction.

Core Idea
Annihilation is not a positive act but a cessation of existence by God.

Nasafi begins by surveying the landscape: how do theologians explain the annihilation of bodies? He identifies three positions, including the Mu'tazilite view that a newly arriving contrary accident causes the destruction of a persistent one. Understanding these views sets the stage for the refutation that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the three views
Carefully read the source_arabic and translation to identify the three distinct theories of annihilation mentioned by Nasafi.
The passage starts by mentioning al-Ka'bi's view, then al-Bāqillānī's, then the Mu'tazilite view.
2
Examine al-Ka'bi's view on survival
Al-Ka'bi holds that survival (baqā') is an accident renewing moment by moment; annihilation occurs when God stops creating it. Note why this is problematic for the Mu'tazila themselves.
This view is mentioned but not fully critiqued here.
3
Examine al-Bāqillānī's view on cutting off accidents
Al-Bāqillānī, a later Ash'arite, suggests God cuts off colors and spaces in bodies. Nasafi notes this only applies to bodies that contain such accidents.
This view is not the focus of the refutation.
4
Focus on the Mu'tazilite view
The Mu'tazilite view is that annihilation occurs by the arrival of a contrary accident (ḍidd) onto the body. Nasafi immediately states this is impossible, setting up the refutation in the next lesson.
This is the main target of Nasafi's critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكعبي، فإن من مذهب هؤلاء أن البقاء عرض حادث في الأجسام لا يبقى بنفسه بل يتجدد ساعة فساعة، فإذا أراد الله تعالى إفناء الجسم قطع عنه البقاء فلم يخلقه فيه فينعدم، والعرض ليس بمحل لخلق البقاء فيه ليقال إنه إذا لم يخلق فيه البقاء في الثاني انعدم، فلم يتصور الفناء بهذا الطريق على مذهب هؤلاء. وأما أن يكون يقطع الألوان والأكوان كما ذهب إليه أبو بكر الباقلاني من متأخري متكلمي أهل الحديث؛ لأن هذا لن يتصور إلا في الأجسام التي يحلها الألوان والأكوان. وأما أن يكون بطريان ضده عليه بأن يطرأ عليه ضده فيعدمه وهذا هو مذهب هؤلاء المخالفين لنا من المعتزلة في المسألة وهذا محال، لأن هذا الضد القائم الثابت المتقرر في المحل ليس بأولى بالعدم من الضد الطارئ الحادث آنفًا، ولا معنى لقول بعض أغبيائهم: إن الحدوث أقوى من البقاء؛ لأن هذا ليس بأولى ممن يقول: البقاء أقوى من الحدوث. ثم الدليل على أن الباقي أقوى من الحادث في الوجود أن الباقي لم يتعلق وجوده بقدرة يصح منع القادر منه ويتوهم ذلك فيه، والحدث يتعلق وجوده بقدرة يصح منع القادر منه ويتوهم ذلك في الجملة، والدليل عليه أن الباقي واجب الوجود في حال طريان ضده عليه، والحادث جائز الوجود في حال حدوثه، ولا شك أن واجب الوجود أقوى في الوجود من جائز الوجود، على أن قوته ليس بأكثر من وجوده، ولا وجود للحادث قبل حالة الحدوث، فعدمه قبل حدوثه لا يكون موجبًا قوته في وجوده، والباقي له وجود قبل وجوده في هذه الحالة التي حدث فيه الضد فوجوده قبل وجوده موجب قوته في وجوده. والذي يقرأ هذا أن العرض الحادث يرفع الثابت المتقرر، والباقي يرفع ما لم يتقرر بعد ولم يثبت بعد، ولا شك أن دفع ما لم يثبت أسهل من رفع ما تقرر، فكان رفع الثابت المتقرر لما لم يثبت بعد أولى في عقل من له عقل من رفع ما ليس بثابت في نفسه لما هو ثابت متقرر. والذي يقطع شغب هؤلاء الجهلة أن يقال لهم: إن الطارئ متى يوجب عدم الباقي قبل وجوده أم في حال وجوده أم بعدما وجد؟ لا جائز أن يقال يوجب عدم الباقي قبل وجوده لأنه قبل وجوده معدوم، والمعدوم لا يوجب عدم الموجود، فكان انعدام الباقي قبل وجود الطارئ لعلة سوى وجود الطارئ. ولا جائز أن يوجب عدمه في حال وجوده لأنا نقول: يوجد في هذا المحل أم لا في محل آخر؟ فإن قال: يوجد في هذا، فهذا محال، لأن وجوده في هذا المحل قبل ارتفاع الأول عن المحل محال؛ لأنه يوجب البقاء للضدين في الوجود، وإذا التقيا في الوجود لم يكونا ضدين. وإن وجد لا في محل فوجود العرض لا في محل محال أيضا، ووجوده لا في محل هذا الضد لا يوجب عدمه؛ لأن وجوده ضد لا في محل ضده لا يوجب عدم ضده إن وجد في محل آخر، فذاك لا يوجب عدم العرض الباقي لأن السواد الموجود في المحل لا يوجب عدم البياض الثابت في محل آخر إلى أن ينتقل إلى هذا المحل، ولا يتصور انتفاء له إلى هذا المحل لاستحالة الانتقال من محل إلى محل على الأعراض. ولأن هذا العرض الطارئ عندهم علة لانعدام الباقي، ومن أصلهم أن العلة تكون سابقة على المعلول، فيكون وجود الطارئ سابقا على انعدام الموجود ثم لا يوجب عدمه.
Al-Ka'bi — for it is part of the doctrine of these people that survival (al-baqā') is an accident (ʻaraḍ) that comes to be (ḥādith) in bodies, not persisting by itself but being renewed moment by moment. So when God Most High wishes to annihilate (ifnā') a body, He cuts off survival from it and does not create it in it, so it becomes nonexistent. And an accident is not a locus (maḥall) for the creation of survival in it such that it could be said that if He does not create survival in it in the second moment, it becomes nonexistent. Thus annihilation (al-fanā') is not conceivable by this path according to the doctrine of these people. And as for it being by cutting off colors and spaces (al-alwān wa-al-akwān) as Abū Bakr al-Bāqillānī, one of the later theologians of the People of Hadith, held — because this is only conceivable in bodies that contain colors and spaces. And as for it being by the arrival of its contrary (bi-ṭiryān ḍiddihi) upon it, such that its contrary arrives upon it and annihilates it — this is the doctrine of those who oppose us among the Mu'tazila in this matter, and this is impossible. For this contrary that is subsistent (qāʼim), fixed (thābit), and established (mutaqarrir) in the locus is not more deserving of nonexistence than the arriving contrary that has newly occurred just now. And the saying of some of their ignorant ones — that temporal origination (al-ḥudūth) is stronger than survival — has no meaning, because this is not more deserving than one who says: survival is stronger than temporal origination. Then the proof that the persistent (al-bāqī) is stronger than the newly occurring (al-ḥādith) in existence is that the existence of the persistent is not attached to a power (qudra) such that the capable one can prevent it and that can be imagined concerning it, while the existence of the newly occurring is attached to a power such that the capable one can prevent it and that can be imagined in general. And the proof of it is that the persistent is necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd) in the state of the arrival of its contrary upon it, while the newly occurring is contingent of existence (jāʼiz al-wujūd) in the state of its occurrence, and there is no doubt that the necessary of existence is stronger in existence than the contingent of existence — given that its strength is not more than its existence. And there is no existence for the newly occurring before the state of its occurrence, so its non-existence before its occurrence does not necessitate its strength in its existence. And the persistent has existence before its existence in this state in which the contrary occurred to it, so its existence before its existence necessitates its strength in its existence. And one who reads this [understands] that the newly occurring accident removes the fixed and established, while the persistent removes what has not yet been established and has not yet become fixed. And there is no doubt that repelling what has not been established is easier than removing what is fixed. So removing the fixed and established by what has not yet been established is more appropriate, in the mind of one who has a mind, than removing what is not fixed in itself by what is fixed and established. And what cuts off the wrangling of these ignorant ones is to say to them: When does the arriving [contrary] necessitate the non-existence of the persistent? Before its existence, or in the state of its existence, or after it has existed? It cannot be said that it necessitates the non-existence of the persistent before its existence, because before its existence it is nonexistent, and the nonexistent does not necessitate the non-existence of the existent. So the annihilation of the persistent before the existence of the arriving one is for a cause other than the existence of the arriving one. And it cannot be said that it necessitates its non-existence in the state of its existence, because we say: Does it exist in this locus or in another locus? If he says: It exists in this [locus], then this is impossible, because its existence in this locus before the removal of the first [contrary] from the locus is impossible; for it would necessitate the persistence of both contraries in existence, and when they meet in existence they would not be contraries. And if it exists not in a locus, then the existence of an accident not in a locus is also impossible. And its existence not in the locus of this contrary does not necessitate its non-existence, because the existence of a contrary not in the locus of its contrary does not necessitate the non-existence of its contrary if it exists in another locus. For that does not necessitate the non-existence of the persistent accident, because blackness (al-sawād) existing in a locus does not necessitate the non-existence of whiteness (al-bayāḍ) fixed in another locus until it transfers to this locus, and its transfer to this locus is inconceivable because of the impossibility of an accident transferring from one locus to another. And because this arriving accident, according to them, is a cause (ʻilla) for the annihilation of the persistent, and it is an axiom of theirs that the cause precedes the effect (al-maʻlūl), so the existence of the arriving one would be prior to the annihilation of the existent, yet it does not necessitate its non-existence.
Source pages p. 802

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite theory of annihilation (fanā') by a contrary accident. The author (al-Nasafi) first outlines three views on how annihilation occurs: 1) al-Ka'bi's view that survival (baqā') is an accident that is continually renewed and God annihilates by ceasing to create it; 2) al-Baqillani's view that God cuts off the accidents of colors and space; 3) the Mu'tazilite view that annihilation occurs by the arrival of a contrary accident. The author dismisses the first two briefly and then focuses on refuting the third, which is the Mu'tazilite position. The refutation proceeds as follows: First, he argues that the existing contrary (persistent) is not more deserving of non-existence than the newly arriving contrary, and the claim that 'origination is stronger than survival' is arbitrary. Then he provides a proof that the persistent is stronger in existence because its existence does not depend on a power that could prevent it, whereas the newly occurring does; also, the persistent is necessary of existence in that moment while the newly occurring is contingent, and what is necessary is stronger. He also points out that the newly occurring removes what is established, whereas the persistent removes what is not yet established, and it is easier to repel what is not established than to remove what is fixed. Finally, he poses a decisive argument: he asks when the arriving contrary causes non-existence — before its existence, at the moment of its existence, or after? If before, it is nothing, so cannot cause. If at the moment, then either it exists in the same locus (impossible because that would require coexistence of contraries) or in another locus (which does not affect the first), and transfer is impossible for accidents. Also, the cause must precede the effect, so if the arrival is the cause, it must precede the effect, but then it would not cause it; this is a contradiction in the Mu'tazilite position.

Key Terms
بقاء (baqā')
عرض (ʻaraḍ)
ضد (ḍidd)
فناء (fanā')
حدوث (ḥudūth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Annihilation is a positive act of destruction, like a contrary accident actively destroying a body.
Correction: According to Nasafi, annihilation is simply the cessation of existence by God not creating survival; it is not a positive act by a contrary.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three views on annihilation mentioned by Nasafi in this passage?
A) Al-Ka'bi: Annihilation by not creating survival.
B) Al-Bāqillānī: Annihilation by cutting off colors and spaces.
C) Mu'tazila: Annihilation by arrival of a contrary accident.
D) Ash'ari: Annihilation by direct divine action without a contrary.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi introduces three theories of annihilation, with the Mu'tazilite theory of annihilation by a contrary accident being the focus of his refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your continued existence depends on God creating survival in you every moment, what does that mean for how you live the next hour? Will you live it in conscious dependence on Him, or in forgetful self-reliance? What one action can you take right now to remind yourself of this dependence?
?
The Mu'tazilites believed a contrary accident could annihilate a body. But you believe that only God gives and takes existence. How does that certainty shape your response to the things that threaten your life—illness, accidents, or even death? Do you truly entrust your survival to God, or do you rely on secondary causes?
?
Consider the reality that your very existence is a creation from God, not a given. How might this truth transform your prayer, your humility, and your love for Him today? Will you let this awareness move you to a deeper gratitude and surrender, or will you ignore it and live as if your life is self-sustaining?

Causality and Created Things
advanced
10 min
Refutation: Why a Contrary Cannot Annihilate the Persistent
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite theory with logical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical arguments that refute the Mu'tazilite claim that a newly arriving contrary accident can annihilate an existing accident.

Core Idea
A newly arising accident cannot remove an already established one because it is weaker and logically impossible.

Having outlined the Mu'tazilite position, Nasafi now delivers a rigorous refutation. He presents a series of arguments: the persistent is stronger because its existence is not dependent on power; the arriving contrary is contingent while the persistent is necessary in that moment; and a devastating trilemma about when the contrary causes nonexistence. This lesson unpacks these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation arguments
Read the source_arabic and translation for the refutation part. Pay attention to the structure: first arguments about strength, then the trilemma.
The passage is dense; you may need to read it twice.
2
Identify the proof that the persistent is stronger
Nasafi gives two reasons: 1) The persistent's existence is not attached to a power that can prevent it, while the new one's is. 2) The persistent is necessary of existence in that moment, while the new one is contingent.
Note the use of 'wājib al-wujūd' and 'jāʼiz al-wujūd' in a relative sense.
3
Analyze the trilemma about timing
Nasafi asks: when does the arriving contrary cause non-existence? Before existence, at the moment, or after? He shows each option leads to a contradiction or impossibility.
This is the most powerful part of the refutation.
4
Grasp the locus argument
If the contrary causes non-existence at the moment, it must exist either in the same locus (which would imply coexistence of contraries) or in another locus (which doesn't affect the first). Transfer of accidents is impossible.
This relies on the principle that accidents cannot move between loci.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكعبي، فإن من مذهب هؤلاء أن البقاء عرض حادث في الأجسام لا يبقى بنفسه بل يتجدد ساعة فساعة، فإذا أراد الله تعالى إفناء الجسم قطع عنه البقاء فلم يخلقه فيه فينعدم، والعرض ليس بمحل لخلق البقاء فيه ليقال إنه إذا لم يخلق فيه البقاء في الثاني انعدم، فلم يتصور الفناء بهذا الطريق على مذهب هؤلاء. وأما أن يكون يقطع الألوان والأكوان كما ذهب إليه أبو بكر الباقلاني من متأخري متكلمي أهل الحديث؛ لأن هذا لن يتصور إلا في الأجسام التي يحلها الألوان والأكوان. وأما أن يكون بطريان ضده عليه بأن يطرأ عليه ضده فيعدمه وهذا هو مذهب هؤلاء المخالفين لنا من المعتزلة في المسألة وهذا محال، لأن هذا الضد القائم الثابت المتقرر في المحل ليس بأولى بالعدم من الضد الطارئ الحادث آنفًا، ولا معنى لقول بعض أغبيائهم: إن الحدوث أقوى من البقاء؛ لأن هذا ليس بأولى ممن يقول: البقاء أقوى من الحدوث. ثم الدليل على أن الباقي أقوى من الحادث في الوجود أن الباقي لم يتعلق وجوده بقدرة يصح منع القادر منه ويتوهم ذلك فيه، والحدث يتعلق وجوده بقدرة يصح منع القادر منه ويتوهم ذلك في الجملة، والدليل عليه أن الباقي واجب الوجود في حال طريان ضده عليه، والحادث جائز الوجود في حال حدوثه، ولا شك أن واجب الوجود أقوى في الوجود من جائز الوجود، على أن قوته ليس بأكثر من وجوده، ولا وجود للحادث قبل حالة الحدوث، فعدمه قبل حدوثه لا يكون موجبًا قوته في وجوده، والباقي له وجود قبل وجوده في هذه الحالة التي حدث فيه الضد فوجوده قبل وجوده موجب قوته في وجوده. والذي يقرأ هذا أن العرض الحادث يرفع الثابت المتقرر، والباقي يرفع ما لم يتقرر بعد ولم يثبت بعد، ولا شك أن دفع ما لم يثبت أسهل من رفع ما تقرر، فكان رفع الثابت المتقرر لما لم يثبت بعد أولى في عقل من له عقل من رفع ما ليس بثابت في نفسه لما هو ثابت متقرر. والذي يقطع شغب هؤلاء الجهلة أن يقال لهم: إن الطارئ متى يوجب عدم الباقي قبل وجوده أم في حال وجوده أم بعدما وجد؟ لا جائز أن يقال يوجب عدم الباقي قبل وجوده لأنه قبل وجوده معدوم، والمعدوم لا يوجب عدم الموجود، فكان انعدام الباقي قبل وجود الطارئ لعلة سوى وجود الطارئ. ولا جائز أن يوجب عدمه في حال وجوده لأنا نقول: يوجد في هذا المحل أم لا في محل آخر؟ فإن قال: يوجد في هذا، فهذا محال، لأن وجوده في هذا المحل قبل ارتفاع الأول عن المحل محال؛ لأنه يوجب البقاء للضدين في الوجود، وإذا التقيا في الوجود لم يكونا ضدين. وإن وجد لا في محل فوجود العرض لا في محل محال أيضا، ووجوده لا في محل هذا الضد لا يوجب عدمه؛ لأن وجوده ضد لا في محل ضده لا يوجب عدم ضده إن وجد في محل آخر، فذاك لا يوجب عدم العرض الباقي لأن السواد الموجود في المحل لا يوجب عدم البياض الثابت في محل آخر إلى أن ينتقل إلى هذا المحل، ولا يتصور انتفاء له إلى هذا المحل لاستحالة الانتقال من محل إلى محل على الأعراض. ولأن هذا العرض الطارئ عندهم علة لانعدام الباقي، ومن أصلهم أن العلة تكون سابقة على المعلول، فيكون وجود الطارئ سابقا على انعدام الموجود ثم لا يوجب عدمه.
Al-Ka'bi — for it is part of the doctrine of these people that survival (al-baqā') is an accident (ʻaraḍ) that comes to be (ḥādith) in bodies, not persisting by itself but being renewed moment by moment. So when God Most High wishes to annihilate (ifnā') a body, He cuts off survival from it and does not create it in it, so it becomes nonexistent. And an accident is not a locus (maḥall) for the creation of survival in it such that it could be said that if He does not create survival in it in the second moment, it becomes nonexistent. Thus annihilation (al-fanā') is not conceivable by this path according to the doctrine of these people. And as for it being by cutting off colors and spaces (al-alwān wa-al-akwān) as Abū Bakr al-Bāqillānī, one of the later theologians of the People of Hadith, held — because this is only conceivable in bodies that contain colors and spaces. And as for it being by the arrival of its contrary (bi-ṭiryān ḍiddihi) upon it, such that its contrary arrives upon it and annihilates it — this is the doctrine of those who oppose us among the Mu'tazila in this matter, and this is impossible. For this contrary that is subsistent (qāʼim), fixed (thābit), and established (mutaqarrir) in the locus is not more deserving of nonexistence than the arriving contrary that has newly occurred just now. And the saying of some of their ignorant ones — that temporal origination (al-ḥudūth) is stronger than survival — has no meaning, because this is not more deserving than one who says: survival is stronger than temporal origination. Then the proof that the persistent (al-bāqī) is stronger than the newly occurring (al-ḥādith) in existence is that the existence of the persistent is not attached to a power (qudra) such that the capable one can prevent it and that can be imagined concerning it, while the existence of the newly occurring is attached to a power such that the capable one can prevent it and that can be imagined in general. And the proof of it is that the persistent is necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd) in the state of the arrival of its contrary upon it, while the newly occurring is contingent of existence (jāʼiz al-wujūd) in the state of its occurrence, and there is no doubt that the necessary of existence is stronger in existence than the contingent of existence — given that its strength is not more than its existence. And there is no existence for the newly occurring before the state of its occurrence, so its non-existence before its occurrence does not necessitate its strength in its existence. And the persistent has existence before its existence in this state in which the contrary occurred to it, so its existence before its existence necessitates its strength in its existence. And one who reads this [understands] that the newly occurring accident removes the fixed and established, while the persistent removes what has not yet been established and has not yet become fixed. And there is no doubt that repelling what has not been established is easier than removing what is fixed. So removing the fixed and established by what has not yet been established is more appropriate, in the mind of one who has a mind, than removing what is not fixed in itself by what is fixed and established. And what cuts off the wrangling of these ignorant ones is to say to them: When does the arriving [contrary] necessitate the non-existence of the persistent? Before its existence, or in the state of its existence, or after it has existed? It cannot be said that it necessitates the non-existence of the persistent before its existence, because before its existence it is nonexistent, and the nonexistent does not necessitate the non-existence of the existent. So the annihilation of the persistent before the existence of the arriving one is for a cause other than the existence of the arriving one. And it cannot be said that it necessitates its non-existence in the state of its existence, because we say: Does it exist in this locus or in another locus? If he says: It exists in this [locus], then this is impossible, because its existence in this locus before the removal of the first [contrary] from the locus is impossible; for it would necessitate the persistence of both contraries in existence, and when they meet in existence they would not be contraries. And if it exists not in a locus, then the existence of an accident not in a locus is also impossible. And its existence not in the locus of this contrary does not necessitate its non-existence, because the existence of a contrary not in the locus of its contrary does not necessitate the non-existence of its contrary if it exists in another locus. For that does not necessitate the non-existence of the persistent accident, because blackness (al-sawād) existing in a locus does not necessitate the non-existence of whiteness (al-bayāḍ) fixed in another locus until it transfers to this locus, and its transfer to this locus is inconceivable because of the impossibility of an accident transferring from one locus to another. And because this arriving accident, according to them, is a cause (ʻilla) for the annihilation of the persistent, and it is an axiom of theirs that the cause precedes the effect (al-maʻlūl), so the existence of the arriving one would be prior to the annihilation of the existent, yet it does not necessitate its non-existence.
Source pages p. 802

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite theory of annihilation (fanā') by a contrary accident. The author (al-Nasafi) first outlines three views on how annihilation occurs: 1) al-Ka'bi's view that survival (baqā') is an accident that is continually renewed and God annihilates by ceasing to create it; 2) al-Baqillani's view that God cuts off the accidents of colors and space; 3) the Mu'tazilite view that annihilation occurs by the arrival of a contrary accident. The author dismisses the first two briefly and then focuses on refuting the third, which is the Mu'tazilite position. The refutation proceeds as follows: First, he argues that the existing contrary (persistent) is not more deserving of non-existence than the newly arriving contrary, and the claim that 'origination is stronger than survival' is arbitrary. Then he provides a proof that the persistent is stronger in existence because its existence does not depend on a power that could prevent it, whereas the newly occurring does; also, the persistent is necessary of existence in that moment while the newly occurring is contingent, and what is necessary is stronger. He also points out that the newly occurring removes what is established, whereas the persistent removes what is not yet established, and it is easier to repel what is not established than to remove what is fixed. Finally, he poses a decisive argument: he asks when the arriving contrary causes non-existence — before its existence, at the moment of its existence, or after? If before, it is nothing, so cannot cause. If at the moment, then either it exists in the same locus (impossible because that would require coexistence of contraries) or in another locus (which does not affect the first), and transfer is impossible for accidents. Also, the cause must precede the effect, so if the arrival is the cause, it must precede the effect, but then it would not cause it; this is a contradiction in the Mu'tazilite position.

جائز الوجود (jāʼiz al-wujūd)
علة (ʻilla)
معلول (maʻlūl)
ضد (ḍidd)
بقاء (baqā')
عرض (ʻaraḍ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A newly arriving contrary accident can cause the destruction of an already existing accident by overpowering it.
Correction: Nasafi shows this is impossible because the persistent is stronger, and the timing of causation leads to contradictions.

Checkpoint
In Nasafi's trilemma, why is it impossible for the arriving contrary to cause non-existence 'at the moment of its existence'?
A) Because the arriving contrary is weaker than the persistent.
B) Because if it exists in the same locus, two contraries would coexist; if in another locus, it doesn't affect the first.
C) Because the arriving contrary is non-existent at that moment.
D) Because the persistent is necessary of existence.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite theory by showing the persistent accident is stronger and that the timing of causation leads to logical impossibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a doubt (like a newly occurring contrary) enters your mind about a firmly held belief (the persistent), do you let it annihilate your conviction without examining when and how it came? This passage shows that a non-existent doubt cannot destroy an existent belief; so why do you sometimes let a mere whisper undo years of certainty? What does that reveal about the strength of your faith?
?
The persistent accident is described as 'necessary of existence' in the state of the contrary's arrival. Reflect: Is your faith only contingent—dependent on circumstances—or is it necessary, rooted in such certainty that no newly occurring doubt can remove it? If it is contingent, what would it take for it to become necessary?
?
Nasafi argues that a cause must precede its effect. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes act as if an effect (like a good deed) can occur without a prior cause (like intention or knowledge)? Or worse, do you allow afterthoughts (like regret or rationalization) to claim they are the cause of your actions? How does this logical principle help you order your heart and actions correctly?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Impossibility of Ability Prior to the Act (Part 1)
Al-Nasafi presents the first part of his proof that ability must be simultaneous with the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi argues that if ability were prior to the act, it would cease, leading to the absurd conclusion that the act occurs without a capable agent.

Core Idea
Ability, as a non-subsisting accident, must be present exactly when the act is performed.

Al-Nasafi begins by establishing a key premise: ability (qudra) is an accident, and accidents cannot persist over time. He then shows that if ability were to precede the act, it would vanish before the act occurs, leaving the agent without ability. This leads to a contradiction because the act would then be performed by someone who is incapable. This lesson unpacks the first part of his proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Recognize that al-Nasafi asserts ability is an accident and cannot subsist, a point agreed upon by many Mu'tazila.
2
Follow the reductio
If ability precedes and then ceases, it is non-existent at the moment of the act, so the act occurs without ability.
3
See the contradiction
Without ability, the agent is incapable, yet the act exists; this contradicts the definition of incapacity as that with which the act is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت أن الاستطاعة التي يتعلق بها وجود الفعل عرض وهو مستحيل البقاء فنقول : إن القدرة لما كانت غير قابلة للبقاء، وساعدنا عليه كثير من المعتزلة، وأثبتنا ذلك بالدليل على من أبي القول، ولو كانت هي متقدمة على الفصل ولا بقاء إلى الثاني من الأوقات كانت عدمًا وقت وجود الفعل، فيوجد الفعل ولا قدرة، وهي لا تنعدم عمن يجوز قيامها به إلا عما يضادها وهو العجز، ومن انعدمت قدرته يستحيل اتصافه بكونه قادرًا، ومن قام به العجز يستحيل أن لا يكون عاجزا، فعلى هذا التقدير يكون الفعل مستحيل الوجود من القادر واجب الوجود من العاجز، ومن هذا قوله، فهو قد أصيب بعقله ولم يشعر. يحققه أن الفعل لو جاز وجوده مع انعدام القدرة بل وجب هي لا تنعدم إلا إلى عجز، واستحال وجوده مع وجودها لكانت القدرة هي العجز والعجز هو القدرة، فإنا لا نعرف العجز إلا بأن يستحيل معه الفعل والقدرة إلا بأن يجب بها الفعل ويثبت.
And if it is established that the ability (al-istita'a) by which the existence of the act is connected is an accident and it is impossible for it to subsist, then we say: When power (al-qudra) is not receptive of subsistence, and many of the Mu'tazila agree with us on this, and we have proved it by evidence against whoever refuses the statement, and if it were prior to the act and there is no subsistence to a second moment, it would be non-existence at the time of the act's existence, so the act would occur while there is no power, and it (power) does not cease from one for whom it is possible to subsist except by what is contrary to it, which is incapacity (al-'ajz), and whoever's power ceases, it is impossible for him to be described as capable, and whoever is characterized by incapacity, it is impossible for him not to be incapable. So on this estimation, the act would be impossible of existence from the capable and necessary of existence from the incapable. And among this is his statement; he has been struck in his intellect and does not perceive. Its verification: If it were permissible for the act to exist with the non-existence of power—rather it would be necessary—while power only ceases to incapacity, and its existence with the existence of power were impossible, then power would be incapacity and incapacity would be power. For we do not know incapacity except that the act is impossible with it, and power except that the act is necessary by it and is established.
Source pages p. 808

Commentary
This passage is a proof that ability (istita'a or qudra) must be simultaneous with the act. Al-Nasafi first establishes that ability is an accident, and accidents cannot subsist (based on earlier arguments, and with agreement from many Mu'tazila). If ability were prior to the act and then perished before the second moment, it would become non-existent at the moment of the act. Consequently, the act would occur without ability. But ability only ceases due to its opposite, which is incapacity ('ajz). If ability is gone, the person is no longer capable; he is now incapable. An incapable person cannot perform an act. Thus, the act would be impossible from the capable (since ability is gone) and necessary from the incapable (since the act occurs despite his incapacity), which is contradictory and absurd. This shows the opponent is logically blinded. Then he reinforces the argument: if the act could exist without ability (and indeed it would be necessary if ability ceased), while ability only ceases to incapacity, and the act is impossible when ability exists (which is false), then ability and incapacity would be indistinguishable. But we define incapacity as that with which the act is impossible, and ability as that with which the act is necessary. So the distinction collapses, which is absurd. Therefore, ability must exist at the time of the act.

Key Terms
استطاعة
قدرة
عرض
بقاء
عجز
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think ability can precede the act and still be effective later.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that because ability is an accident that cannot persist, it would be absent at the time of the act, leading to the act occurring without a capable agent.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, if ability (qudra) were prior to the act and then ceased, what would be the consequence?
A) The act would occur with ability
B) The act would occur without ability, making the agent incapable
C) The ability would persist
D) The act would be impossible

Key Takeaway
If ability precedes the act, it ceases by the time of the act, leading to the absurdity that the act occurs without a capable agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your ability to do any good deed is created by Allah exactly at the moment you do it, what does that imply about procrastination? Are you delaying an act assuming you have stored ability, when in reality you may become incapable?
?
This lesson shows that without simultaneous ability, you are actually incapable. How does this truth about ability affect your daily reliance on Allah? Do you pray for ability at the moment of action, or do you take it for granted?
?
Al-Nasafi refutes the idea that ability can precede the act. In your own life, when you resolve to do something but keep putting it off, are you acting on a false assumption that your ability will still be there later? What change does this lesson demand from you?

The Verification: Ability and Incapacity Would Be Indistinguishable (Part 2)
Al-Nasafi presents the verification argument against ability preceding the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second part of al-Nasafi's proof, which shows that if the act could exist without ability, then the definitions of ability and incapacity would collapse.

Core Idea
If the act can occur without ability, then ability and incapacity become the same.

In this lesson, we examine the second argument (taḥqīq). Al-Nasafi pushes the logic further: if the act could exist without ability, and ability only ceases to incapacity, then the distinction between ability and incapacity vanishes. Because we know incapacity as that with which the act is impossible, and ability as that with which the act is possible. This absurdity reinforces that ability must be present at the time of the act.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hypothesis
Al-Nasafi considers the possibility that the act could exist without ability.
2
Trace the implication
If ability only ceases to incapacity, then the act occurring without ability implies it occurs during incapacity.
3
See the collapse of definitions
Then incapacity would be defined by the possibility of the act, and ability by its impossibility, reversing their meanings and proving the original assumption false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refutation of the Survival of Accidents
Al-Nasafi refutes the survival of accidents by citing established proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi considers the survival of accidents (like power) impossible and how this refutes the opponent's first claim.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot persist; they vanish before the action occurs.

The opponents argue that accidents, such as the power to act, survive until the moment of action. Al-Nasafi counters by invoking the established proof that accidents cannot persist. This lesson examines that exchange and its implications for divine obligation and human capability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
Read the first part of the passage and note that the opponents assert that accidents (like power) survive until the action occurs.
2
Understand al-Nasafi's response
Al-Nasafi refers to a previously established proof that accidents cannot survive. Therefore, the power becomes nonexistent in the second moment.
The second moment is the time when the action would occur.
3
Reflect on the consequences
Without a surviving power, the opponent's basis for obligation (taklīf) and capability collapses. This shows the necessity of a concurrent power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لا نسلم أن بقاءها مستحيل بل هي باقية إلى وقت وجود الفعل فيكون ذلك الفعل بقدرة موجودة ويكون التكليف ثابتا والفاعل قادراً. قيل لهم : قد أقمنا الدلالة على استحالة القول ببقاء الأعراض، وإذا استحال بقاؤها ولم تبق كانت منعدمة في الثاني من زمان وجودها، وذلك هو زمان وجود الفعل. فإن قالوا: إن انعدمت تلك القدرة إلا أن قدرة أخرى تحدث عقيبها إذ القدرة تحدث تباعًا في الصحيح السليم. قيل لهم : تلك القدرة الموجودة في الثاني أهي عندكم قدرة هذا الفعل أم قدرة فعل آخر يعقبها؟ وهل يجوز وجود هذا الفعل في الثاني بهذه القدرة الموجودة في الثاني؟ فإن قالوا: هي قدرة هذا الفعل، ويجوز وجود الفعل بهذه القدرة المقارنة له في الوجود. فقد تركوا مذهبهم، حيث جعلوا الفعل حاصلاً بالقدرة المقارنة له في الوجود، وبقيت القدرة السابقة على الفعل فضلاً لا فائدة في وجودها ولا تعلق لها بفعل ما البتة، وهذا محال فساعدوا أهل الحق في الحقيقة وأثبتوا زيادة لا فائدة فيها، فلم يحصلوا من المخالفة إلا على سفه.
If they say: We do not accept that their survival is impossible; rather they persist until the time of the action's existence, so that action occurs by an existing power, and the obligation remains and the agent is capable. It is said to them: We have established the proof for the impossibility of the statement that accidents survive, and since their survival is impossible and they do not persist, they become nonexistent in the second [moment] of the time of their existence, and that is the time of the action's existence. If they say: That power ceases to exist, except that another power occurs after it, since powers occur successively in the healthy sound [person]. It is said to them: That power existing in the second [moment], is it in your view the power for this action or the power for another action that follows it? And is it permissible for this action to exist in the second [moment] by this power existing in the second [moment]? If they say: It is the power for this action, and it is permissible for the action to exist by this power concurrent with it in existence. Then they have abandoned their school, where they made the action occur by the power concurrent with it in existence, and the power preceding the action remains superfluous with no benefit in its existence and no connection to any action at all, and this is impossible. So they have agreed with the people of truth in reality and affirmed an addition with no benefit in it, and they gained nothing from opposition but folly.
Source pages p. 813

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation. The opponents first claim that accidents (like power) survive until the time of the action, so the action is performed with an existing power. Al-Nasafi responds that they have already proved the impossibility of the survival of accidents; thus the power would be nonexistent in the second moment, which is the time of the action. The opponents then shift: although the first power ceases, another power arises successively. Al-Nasafi asks whether this new power is the power for this action or another. If they say it is for this action and the action occurs with this concurrent power, then they have abandoned their own position (that power precedes action) and admitted that the prior power was useless—an absurdity. Thus their opposition yields only foolishness.

Key Terms
بقاء الأعراض
القدرة
الفعل
التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that accidents can persist unchanged over time.
Correction: Al-Nasafi affirms that accidents are inherently transient and cannot survive into the next moment.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi's response, what happens to the power in the second moment?
A) It persists until the action.
B) It becomes nonexistent.
C) It is replaced by another power.
D) It becomes stronger.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's refutation shows that if accidents cannot survive, the opponent's first objection fails because no power remains when the action occurs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you unconsciously assume that your ability to obey Allah (such as praying or giving charity) remains with you until you decide to act? How does knowing that your power to act vanishes each moment—recreated only by Allah—change your trust in your own resolve?
?
If your capacity to do good is not continuously yours, but only exists in the instant Allah grants it, what does that mean for your excuses when you delay obedience? Are you waiting for a power you think is still there, but which has already ceased to exist?
?
When you fail to act righteously, do you attribute it to a lack of continuous ability? How might this lesson shift your understanding of blame—both your own and others'—since the power is not self-sustained?

Refutation of Sequential Powers
Al-Nasafi refutes the notion of sequential powers by revealing their absurd consequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi exposes the absurdity of the claim that powers arise successively to perform an action.

Core Idea
Sequential powers lead to admitting a useless prior power.

After conceding that a single power does not persist, the opponents propose that powers arise in succession. Al-Nasafi scrutinizes this new claim, asking whether the second power is for the same action or a different one. The answer forces them into a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Track the opponent's shift
Note that the opponents now claim that although the first power ends, another power arises immediately after.
2
Analyze al-Nasafi's question
Al-Nasafi asks two key questions: Is the new power for this same action or a subsequent one? Can this action exist by this concurrent power?
3
Identify the consequence
If they say it is for this action, then the prior power becomes useless, contradicting their own school.
4
Understand the conclusion
Al-Nasafi declares that the opponents have inadvertently agreed with the truth and added a useless element, showing their folly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What does al-Nasafi claim the opponents have effectively done by admitting the concurrent power causes the action?

The Impossibility of Action by a Prior Power: The Hand Analogy
Al-Nasafi refutes the opponent's claim and proves equality of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's dialectical response to the opponent's claim that action can be caused by a power preceding it, and the key analogy of the hand that demonstrates the impossibility.

Core Idea
No action can exist by a power that is absent at the time of action.

Al-Nasafi continues the debate by addressing a nuanced objection: the opponent claims that the power in the second moment is actually meant for a future action, not the concurrent one. He shows that this does not solve the problem, and then provides a powerful analogy: just as striking with a hand that no longer exists is impossible regardless of how long ago it ceased, so too an action cannot be caused by a power that preceded it by any amount of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim and al-Nasafi's response
Carefully read the Arabic passage from the beginning up to 'ولزمكم جميع ما ألزمناكم'. Note how al-Nasafi refutes the opponent's position by showing that the power in the second moment cannot cause the concurrent action.
2
Focus on the proof of equality
Next, examine the proof starting from 'والذي يدل على صحة ما ذهبنا إليه'. Al-Nasafi argues that the impossibility of action from a power that preceded by many moments applies equally to one moment. Understand why he says they are 'equal in nonexistence'.
This is the core logical move.
3
Reflect on the hand analogy
Finally, study the hand analogy (البطش بيد كانت ثم انعدمت). Al-Nasafi uses this to concretize the abstract principle: if the hand is absent at the time of striking, striking is impossible, whether the hand vanished long ago or just now. Ask yourself how this analogy strengthens the proof.
The analogy illustrates the equality of times.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لا يجوز وجود الفعل في الثاني بهذه القدرة المقارنة له بل وجوده بالقدرة المتقدمة، وتلك القدرة الموجودة في الثاني لفعل يوجد في الثالث لا لفعل يوجد مقارناً لها. قيل لهم : إذا كانت هذه القدرة الثابتة لفعل آخر لا لهذا الفعل ويستحيل تعلق الفعل المقارن لها بها بل تعلقه بالقدرة الأولى وحصوله بها لا بهذه، وكان وجود هذه القدرة في حق الفعل وعدمها بمنزلة أن القدرة على فعل لا يوجب زوال العجز عن فعل آخر ، ولزمكم جميع ما ألزمناكم، ولا تخلص لكم بوجه من الوجوه. والذي يدل على صحة ما ذهبنا إليه أن الخصوم ساعدونا على استحالة وجود الفعل بقدرة متقدمة عليه بأوقات كثيرة، فكذا يستحيل وجوده بقدرة متقدمة عليه بوقت واحد لاستوائهما في العدم وقت الفعل، ألا ترى أن البطش بيد كانت ثم انعدمت محال وإن اتصل وجود الفعل بزمان وجود اليد إذا كانت منعدمة وقت الفعل. كما أنه محال في زمان وجد بعد زمان انعدام اليد بمدة مديدة لاستوائهما في عدم اليد زمان البطش فكذا هذا، ويخرج هذا الكلام على طريق السؤال من وجهين: أحدهما من حيث القدرة ، وهو أن القدرة لما لم تكن إلا للفعل وقد تخلو عنه وقتا لم لا يجوز أن تخلو عنه أوقاتا؟ وهذا السؤال يرد أيضا على من يقول ببقاء القدرة من البصريين. والثاني: من حيث الفعل، وهو أنه لما جاز الفصل بالقدرة في الوقت الثاني وإن كانت القدرة فيه منعدمة لم لا يجوز الفعل بها في العاشر وإن كانت منعدمة وتقرير السؤال بالآلة كاليد والسكين وغيرهما، أنه لما استحال حصول الفعل بهما بعد العدم في الوقت العاشر من زمان العدم، فكذا استحال في الثاني منه لاستواء الوقتين في العدم.
If they say: It is not permissible for the action to exist in the second [moment] by this power concurrent with it, rather its existence is by the preceding power, and that power existing in the second [moment] is for an action that exists in the third [moment], not for an action that exists concurrent with it. It is said to them: If this power established is for another action not for this action, and it is impossible for the action concurrent with it to be connected to it, rather its connection is to the first power and its occurrence by it not by this [power], and the existence of this power with respect to the action and its non-existence are equivalent to the fact that the power for an action does not necessitate the removal of incapacity for another action, and all that we have imposed upon you applies, and you have no escape in any way. And what indicates the correctness of what we hold is that the opponents have agreed with us on the impossibility of an action existing by a power that precedes it by many moments; similarly, it is impossible for it to exist by a power that precedes it by one moment, because they are equal in nonexistence at the time of the action. Do you not see that striking with a hand that existed and then ceased to exist is impossible, even if the existence of the action is connected to the time of the hand's existence when it is nonexistent at the time of the action. Just as it is impossible in a time that occurs after the time of the hand's non-existence by a long period, because of the equality of the two times in the non-existence of the hand at the time of striking, so too this. And this discourse proceeds by way of a question in two respects: One of them is with respect to power, which is that since power exists only for the action and it may be devoid of it at a time, why is it not permissible for it to be devoid of it at multiple times? And this question also applies to those who affirm the survival of power among the Basrans. The second is with respect to the action, which is that since the separation by power in the second moment is allowed even though the power in it is nonexistent, why is the action not permissible by it in the tenth [moment] even though it is nonexistent? And the formulation of the question by means of the instrument, such as the hand, the knife, and others, is that since it is impossible for the action to occur by them after non-existence in the tenth time of non-existence, so too it is impossible in the second of it because of the equality of the two times in non-existence.
Source pages p. 813

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectic. The opponents now claim that the power in the second moment is not for the concurrent action but for a future action in the third moment. Al-Nasafi responds that if this power is for another action, then the concurrent action cannot be connected to it; it must be connected to the first power. But then the existence of this second power is irrelevant with respect to the first action, and the opponents face all the previous objections. Al-Nasafi then provides a proof: the opponents already admit that an action cannot be caused by a power that precedes it by many moments; similarly, it cannot be caused by a power that precedes it by one moment, because in both cases the power is nonexistent at the time of action. He uses the analogy of striking with a hand that later ceases to exist: if the hand is gone at the time of striking, striking is impossible, whether the hand ceased long ago or just now—both involve non-existence at the time of action. Finally, he presents two ways of formulating the question: one from the perspective of power (if power can be empty of an action at one moment, why not many?) and the other from the perspective of the action (if a gap of one moment is allowed, why not ten?). The instrument analogy (hand, knife) reinforces the point: an action cannot occur with an instrument that no longer exists, regardless of how long ago it ceased.

Key Terms
القدرة المتقدمة
البطش
اليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think a short time gap between power and action is different from a long time gap.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that both involve nonexistence of power at the time of action, and thus both are equally impossible.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi's hand analogy, why is it impossible for an action to occur by a power that preceded it by a single moment?
A) Because power cannot exist without action.
B) Because both the hand and power are nonexistent at the time of action, just as if they had ceased long before.
C) Because power must be concurrent with action.
D) Because the hand is a necessary condition.

Key Takeaway
An action cannot be caused by a power that is not present at the moment of action, regardless of how recently it existed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there actions in your daily life that you perform mechanically, relying on past intentions or habits, while your heart and mind are absent? How does al-Nasafi's argument that power must be present at the moment of action challenge you to make every act a conscious, present moment of worship?
?
Consider a sin you thought you had left behind because you stopped desiring it long ago. But do you still have the capacity to fall into it at any moment? What does the hand analogy teach you about your constant need for Allah's support every second to avoid sin?
?
When you stand in prayer, do you assume that the salah will be accepted because of your general practice or because you made the intention earlier? Does your soul experience the power of being with Allah at that very instant, or is it absent? What will you change starting today to ensure your power (qudra) is fully present in every moment of worship?

Two Objection Modes: Power, Action, and the Instrument Analogy
Al-Nasafi presents two objection modes and the instrument analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways al-Nasafi formulates the objection against the opponent, and how the instrument analogy reinforces the argument.

Core Idea
If power can lack an action for one moment, it can for many; if action can be delayed by one moment, it can by ten.

Al-Nasafi now presents two distinct ways to question the opponent's position: one focusing on the nature of power, and the other on the action itself. He also extends the analogy to instruments like a hand or knife, showing that once the instrument ceases to exist, action is impossible regardless of how short the interval.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objection modes
Read the passage carefully and find the two distinct ways of formulating the question: one from the perspective of power, and another from the perspective of action. Note how each questions the opponent's view.
The first objection is about power being empty of action at a time; the second is about action occurring after the power is gone.
2
Understand the instrument analogy
Examine how al-Nasafi uses the example of instruments (hand, knife) to illustrate the same point: if the instrument does not exist at the time of action, the action is impossible, regardless of when it ceased to exist.
This analogy reinforces the equality of time gaps.
3
Connect to the broader argument
Reflect on how these two objections strengthen al-Nasafi's overall proof against the opponent. They show that no matter how you look at it, a gap between power and action leads to logical impossibility.

Source Text

فإن قالوا: لا يجوز وجود الفعل في الثاني بهذه القدرة المقارنة له بل وجوده بالقدرة المتقدمة، وتلك القدرة الموجودة في الثاني لفعل يوجد في الثالث لا لفعل يوجد مقارناً لها. قيل لهم : إذا كانت هذه القدرة الثابتة لفعل آخر لا لهذا الفعل ويستحيل تعلق الفعل المقارن لها بها بل تعلقه بالقدرة الأولى وحصوله بها لا بهذه، وكان وجود هذه القدرة في حق الفعل وعدمها بمنزلة أن القدرة على فعل لا يوجب زوال العجز عن فعل آخر ، ولزمكم جميع ما ألزمناكم، ولا تخلص لكم بوجه من الوجوه. والذي يدل على صحة ما ذهبنا إليه أن الخصوم ساعدونا على استحالة وجود الفعل بقدرة متقدمة عليه بأوقات كثيرة، فكذا يستحيل وجوده بقدرة متقدمة عليه بوقت واحد لاستوائهما في العدم وقت الفعل، ألا ترى أن البطش بيد كانت ثم انعدمت محال وإن اتصل وجود الفعل بزمان وجود اليد إذا كانت منعدمة وقت الفعل. كما أنه محال في زمان وجد بعد زمان انعدام اليد بمدة مديدة لاستوائهما في عدم اليد زمان البطش فكذا هذا، ويخرج هذا الكلام على طريق السؤال من وجهين: أحدهما من حيث القدرة ، وهو أن القدرة لما لم تكن إلا للفعل وقد تخلو عنه وقتا لم لا يجوز أن تخلو عنه أوقاتا؟ وهذا السؤال يرد أيضا على من يقول ببقاء القدرة من البصريين. والثاني: من حيث الفعل، وهو أنه لما جاز الفصل بالقدرة في الوقت الثاني وإن كانت القدرة فيه منعدمة لم لا يجوز الفعل بها في العاشر وإن كانت منعدمة وتقرير السؤال بالآلة كاليد والسكين وغيرهما، أنه لما استحال حصول الفعل بهما بعد العدم في الوقت العاشر من زمان العدم، فكذا استحال في الثاني منه لاستواء الوقتين في العدم.
If they say: It is not permissible for the action to exist in the second [moment] by this power concurrent with it, rather its existence is by the preceding power, and that power existing in the second [moment] is for an action that exists in the third [moment], not for an action that exists concurrent with it. Checkpoint
What is the second objection mode that al-Nasafi presents?
A) If power can be devoid of action at one time, it can be devoid at many times.

?

?

?
If the instrument analogy shows that any non-existence of power makes action impossible, why do you still ascribe effectiveness to means like your skills or wealth as if they cause results independently? How can you change your thinking today to see them as mere occasions for Allah's creation?

The Body's Single State and Capacity's Empty Beginning
Al-Nasafi refutes an objection by distinguishing the body's necessary single state from capacity's initial emptiness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a body cannot have two states at once, and how capacity differs by being empty of action at the moment of its existence.

Core Idea
A body always has one state; capacity can begin empty.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi addresses an objection that tries to equate the body's inability to be empty of two actions with capacity's emptiness. He clarifies that the body must always have one state, but capacity, at the moment it comes into existence, can be empty of action. This distinction is crucial for understanding the relationship between capacity and action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then the English translation. Get a feel for the argument before diving into details.
2
Identify the objection
The objector claims that the body can be empty of two actions, similar to capacity. Note the parallel they are trying to draw.
3
Notice the distinction between body and capacity
Al-Nasafi shows that a body always has one state and cannot have two, while capacity at its first moment is empty of action. This breaks the analogy.
4
Understand the refutation
The author concludes that if the objector knows this distinction, he is a sophist; if not, he is ignorant. Reflect on how this refutes the objection.
5
Reflect on key terms
Examine the key Arabic terms: كون (state), قدرة (capacity), فعل (action). Their precise meanings are central to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفعل أصلاً. فعارضوا بخلو الجسم عن فعلين في زمان واحد، وخلو الجسم عن كونين جائز في كل وقت من أوقات البقاء بل واجب كما هو جائز بل واجب في أول أحوال وجوده. فإذا قط لا يوجد في زمان ما للجسم إلا كون واحد، ولا يخلو عنه في زمان ما لا زمان وجوده ولا زمان بقائه. فأما القدرة فإنها خالية عن الفعل حال وجودها وإن تحقق ذاتها، ويستحيل بعد ذلك خلوها عن الفعل وإن لم تتغير ذاتها، فالمعترض بفعل خلو الجسم عن الحركة والسكون إن كان عالما بما بينا من حقيقة الحركة والسكون واستحالة خلو الجسم في أول أوقاته عن كون، واستحالة وجود كونين في حال ما وإن كانت حالة البقاء وثبوت المساواة بين الأحوال أجمع في وجوب وجود كون فيه واستحالة وجود كونين فيه مع علمه بذلك اعترض بهذا الاعتراض ؛ فهو مغالط، وإن لم يكن عالمًا بذلك ; كان السؤال صادرا عن الجهل بالحقائق.
Action originally. So they objected with the emptiness of the body from two actions in one time, and the emptiness of the body from two states (كونين) is permissible at every time of duration, rather obligatory, just as it is permissible, rather obligatory, in the first state of its existence. Then never at any time does the body have more than one state, nor is it empty of it at any time, neither at the time of its existence nor at the time of its duration. As for capacity (القدرة), it is empty of action at the moment of its existence even though its essence is realized, and it is impossible after that for it to be empty of action even if its essence does not change. So the objector by the action of the body being empty of motion and rest, if he knows what we have explained about the reality of motion and rest, and the impossibility of the body being empty of a state in its first times, and the impossibility of the existence of two states at any moment even if it is a state of duration, and the establishment of equality among all states in the necessity of a state existing in it and the impossibility of two states existing in it, and he knows that, then he objects with this objection; so he is a sophist. And if he does not know that, then the question arises from ignorance of realities.
Source pages p. 816

Commentary
This passage defines the distinction between the state of a body and the state of capacity. The author begins by stating that the body cannot be empty of two actions at one time; rather, it must have one state at any given moment, whether at the first moment of existence or during duration. Capacity, however, can be empty of action at the moment it exists, but after that it cannot be empty. The objector who draws an analogy between the body's emptiness of motion/rest and capacity's emptiness is either knowingly engaging in sophistry or is ignorant of the true nature of these concepts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Capacity must always be accompanied by action; it cannot exist without acting.
Correction: Capacity can exist without action at the first moment of its existence, as stated: 'فأما القدرة فإنها خالية عن الفعل حال وجودها'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, at the moment of its existence, what is the state of capacity with respect to action?
A) It is necessarily accompanied by action.
B) It is empty of action.
C) It has two actions simultaneously.
D) It is impossible for it to exist without action.

Key Takeaway
A body always has exactly one state at any moment, while capacity begins empty of action but cannot remain so.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your body can never have more than one state at a time, what does that mean for your current spiritual state? Are you truly in a single state of submission to Allah, or are you trying to hold contradictory intentions? What must you do to align your state with tawheed?
?
Your capacity (قدرة) for good actions begins empty — have you left it empty, or have you filled it with obedience? What specific act of worship or kindness will you perform today to ensure your capacity is not idle?
?
The body cannot be empty of a state; you are always in some state before Allah. Right now, are you in a state of mindfulness (ihsan) or heedlessness? Take a breath and commit to one conscious act of remembrance.

Abu Mansur on Motion and Stillness at the First Moment
Abu Mansur al-Maturidi explains the difference between the first moment and duration regarding motion and stillness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, a body cannot be described as moving or still at the first moment of its existence, and why analogies to duration fail.

Core Idea
Motion and stillness require two states, impossible at the first moment.

In this passage, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi addresses a key objection in kalam: can a body be described as moving or still at the very first instant of its existence? He argues that these terms inherently denote two states, which cannot coexist at a single moment. He then refutes an invalid analogy, showing the need for a common cause in reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Abu Mansur states his position clearly at the beginning.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand 'كونان'
The author says each of 'moving' and 'still' is a name for two states. Reflect on why that makes them impossible at the first moment of existence.
Consider that a single moment cannot contain two distinct states.
3
Recognize the difference between first moment and duration
Notice that Abu Mansur distinguishes between the first moment (where impossibility holds) and duration (where no impossibility). This is key.
4
Examine the refutation of the analogy
He rejects an analogy by pointing out the lack of a common cause (علة جامعة). Understand why this weakens the opponent's argument.
A valid analogy requires a shared underlying reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجاب الشيخ أبو منصور - رحمه الله - وقال : إنما لم يجز أن يكون متحركًا أو ساكنا في أول أحوال وجوده لما فيه من الإحالة، فإن كل واحد منهما اسم كونين، وجودهما في أول وجوده محال، ولا إحالة في حالة البقاء فوقع الفرق. فأما فيما نحن فيه فلا استحالة، وقياس ما لا استحالة في ثبوته على ما لثبوته استحالة في المنع قياس من غير علة جامعة. وإن قال فيما نحن فيه استحالة، فالواجب عليه بيان الاستحالة دون الاشتغال بإيراد النظر.
And Sheikh Abu Mansur - may Allah have mercy on him - answered and said: The reason why it is not permissible for it to be moving or still in the first state of its existence is because of the impossibility in that, for each one of them is a name for two states (كونين), their existence at the first moment of its existence is impossible, and there is no impossibility in the state of duration, so the difference occurred. As for what we are discussing, there is no impossibility, and measuring that which has no impossibility in its affirmation to that which its affirmation has impossibility in preventing is a measurement without a common cause. And if he says that in what we are discussing there is impossibility, then it is incumbent upon him to explain the impossibility without occupying himself with presenting the argument.
Source pages p. 816

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Mansur al-Maturidi's specific response to the objection about the body being moving or still at the first moment. He explains that at the first moment, it is impossible for the body to be moving or still because each of these terms denotes two states (كونين), and two states cannot exist at the first moment. However, during duration, there is no such impossibility. The objector cannot analogize the case of capacity (which has no impossibility) to the case of the body's first moment (which has impossibility), because this analogy lacks a common reason. If the objector insists that there is impossibility in the case of capacity, he must demonstrate that impossibility rather than merely raising objections.

ساكن
كونان
أول أحوال الوجود
بقاء
إحالة
علة جامعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A body can be described as moving or still at the very first moment of its existence.
Correction: Abu Mansur explains that these terms denote two states (كونين), which cannot exist at a single first moment, so such description is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does Abu Mansur say a body cannot be described as moving or still at the first moment of its existence?
A) Because motion and stillness are impossible for any body.
B) Because each of these terms implies two states, and two states cannot exist at the first moment.
C) Because capacity does not exist at that moment.
D) Because the body is empty of all actions at that moment.

Key Takeaway
Motion and stillness are names for two states, making them impossible at the first moment of existence, while no such impossibility exists in duration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you begin a new spiritual practice (like prayer or fasting), do you expect immediate stillness of the heart or movement of devotion? Reflect on a time you judged yourself harshly for not 'moving' perfectly from the first moment. How can Abu Mansur's insight about two states help you give yourself the grace of duration? Write one commitment to allow time for your actions to take shape.
?
You often compare your current struggles to others' successes without considering their 'first moment' versus your 'duration'. Is your analogy fair? Identify one relationship or situation where you are measuring without a common cause. What would it take to acknowledge that difference and stop the unfair comparison?
?
The lesson says motion and stillness require two states—they cannot exist in a single instant. Think of an area in your life where you demand immediate change (e.g., anger, laziness). Are you expecting a 'first moment' transformation without allowing the time for two states? What is one small step you can take today to begin the process, trusting that Allah works through duration?

Resolving the Timing of Capacity for Motion
The author refutes an objection about the timing of capacity for motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author resolves an objection about the timing of capacity for motion by referring to different definitions of motion.

Core Idea
Where capacity exists depends on what motion means.

This passage addresses a classic objection in kalam about the relationship between capacity (qudra) and motion. The objector tries to trap the theologian into either affirming capacity before motion (which is impossible) or motion without capacity (also impossible). The author shows that the objection only works under one definition of motion; under another definition, the problem disappears. This lesson unpacks that response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the two horns of the dilemma: capacity in the second place implies transition without capacity; capacity in the first place implies capacity before motion.
The objection is a classical dilemma.
2
Identify the two definitions of motion
The answer hinges on two views: motion as a single state in the second place (conditioned by a previous state) vs. motion as two states in two places. Find these in the passage.
3
See where capacity is placed in each view
In the first view, capacity is in the second place. In the second view, capacity for each state is in its respective place. Confirm this from the text.
Each action requires a concurrent capacity.
4
Understand why the objection dissolves
Ask yourself: Does the dilemma still hold under both definitions? The author shows it only arises if one assumes a fixed definition. The answer reframes the discussion.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يجابون عن قولهم: إن الإنسان متى يكون قادرًا على الحركة، يقدر عليها وهو في المكان الأول أم يقدر وهو في المكان الثاني؟ فإن قلتم : إنه يقدر وهو في المكان الثاني فقد أثبتم الانتقال من المكان الأول إلى المكان الثاني بلا قدرة، وهذا محال - وإن قلتم : إنه يقدر وهو في المكان الأول فقد أثبتم الاستطاعة قبل الحركة. فيقال لهم : هذا السؤال بناء على أن الحركة في أي مكان توجد؟ فمن قال: توجد في المكان الثاني فالقدرة عليها توجد في المكان الثاني - ومن قال إنها توجد فيهما القدرة توجد فيهما، وكشفه ما بينا أن من زعم أن الحركة اسم للكون فهي المكان الثاني ويستحق الاسم بشريطة تقدم كون آخر في مكان آخر قبله يقول : توجد قدرة الحركة في المكان الثاني. ومن قال: إن الحركة اسم لكونين في مكانين يقول : توجد قدرة الكون الأول في المكان الأول، وقدرة الكون الثاني في المكان الثاني إذ هما فعلان لابد لكل فعل من قدرة تقارنه، والله الموفق.
And with this they are answered regarding their statement: When is a person capable of motion—does he have the capacity while in the first place or while in the second place? If you say: he has capacity while in the second place, then you have affirmed the transition from the first place to the second place without capacity, and this is impossible. And if you say: he has capacity while in the first place, then you have affirmed capability before motion. So it is said to them: This question is based on: in which place does motion occur? Whoever says: it occurs in the second place, then capacity for it occurs in the second place. And whoever says: it occurs in both places, then capacity occurs in both places. And its exposition is what we have explained: whoever claims that motion is a name for a state (كون) in the second place, and the name is deserved on condition of a preceding state in another place before it, says: the capacity for motion occurs in the second place. And whoever says: motion is a name for two states (كونين) in two places, says: the capacity for the first state occurs in the first place, and the capacity for the second state occurs in the second place, for they are two actions, and every action must have a capacity that accompanies it. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 816

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific objection about the timing of capacity for motion: if a person is capable of motion while in the first place, that would imply capacity before motion; if while in the second place, that would imply transition without capacity. The author responds by pointing out that the objection depends on a particular definition of motion. There are two views: some define motion as a single state in the second place, conditioned by a preceding state in the first place—on this view, capacity for motion is in the second place. Others define motion as two states in two places—on this view, capacity for each state is in its respective place, because each action requires a concurrent capacity. The objection is thereby dissolved.

كون
فعل
استطاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that capacity must precede or coincide with motion in the same location, leading to an insoluble dilemma.
Correction: The passage shows that the location of capacity depends on the definition of motion. Under the two-state definition, capacity is concurrently in each place, resolving the dilemma.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone defines motion as a single state in the second place, where does the capacity for that motion exist?
A) In the first place.
B) In the second place.
C) In both places.
D) In neither place.

Key Takeaway
The apparent dilemma about capacity for motion dissolves once we clarify the definition of motion; capacity is placed where the action occurs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that a seemingly unanswerable dilemma dissolves when the definition of a term is clarified. Think of a personal struggle or a theological doubt you’ve held. What definition or assumption might be at its root? Are you willing to re-examine that assumption today?

The objector assumed a fixed meaning of motion, but the author showed multiple valid definitions. How might God be calling you to expand your understanding?
?
The capacity for motion is placed where the action occurs. In your spiritual life, have you ever tried to muster capacity (intention, strength) before the moment of action, only to feel paralyzed? The Objection About Sound Person Lacking Capacity and Its Refutation
Refutation of the claim that a sound person can be free of capacity initially.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that soundness of limbs necessarily entails capacity for action, and how this refutes the objection of initial incapacity.

Core Idea
Soundness of limbs necessarily implies capacity.

In this passage, the author addresses a key objection: can a physically sound person be initially devoid of capacity? The response clarifies a foundational Maturidi principle: soundness and capacity are inseparable. This lesson unpacks that principle and shows how it neutralizes the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to understand the flow: objection, response, and conclusion.
2
Identify the objection
Find the claim made by the opponent about the sound person lacking capacity initially.
The objection is stated in the first sentence.
3
Understand the Maturidi principle
The author says that according to his school, soundness of limbs implies capacity. This is the key principle that refutes the objection.
This principle turns the objection into an impossible scenario.
4
Trace the logic of necessity
Since soundness necessitates capacity, the objection about initial lack of capacity cannot stand. The author says capacity exists necessarily whenever soundness is present.
Notice the word 'ضرورة' (by necessity).
5
Note the historical reference
The author mentions that al-Ash'ari also used this argument against al-Ka'bi.
This shows cross-school agreement on this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما زعم أن الصحيح السليم يخلو عن القدرة على الفعل في أول خلقه ثم لا يجوز بعد ذلك. قلنا: من زعم من أصحابنا أن الإنسان لا يكون صحيح الجوارح سليمًا إلا إذا كان قادراً ، كان هذا السؤال على أصله محالاً، لأنه لما وجدت صحة الجوارح وجدت القدرة ضرورة في أي حالة كانت، فاندفع هذا السؤال على أصله، اعتمد الأشعري في كتابه الذي رد فيه كتاب أوائل الأدلة لأبي القاسم الكعبي.
And what he claimed that the sound, healthy person is empty of capacity for action at the beginning of his creation, then is not permissible after that. We say: Whoever among our companions claims that a person is not sound of limbs and healthy unless he is capable, this question according to his principle is impossible, because when soundness of limbs exists, capacity necessarily exists in whatever state it may be. So this question is repelled according to his principle. Al-Ash'ari relied on this in his book in which he refuted the book 'Awa'il al-Adilla' by Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi.
Source pages p. 816

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that a sound person initially lacks capacity for action, although later he cannot lack it. The author responds that according to the school of the Maturidis, a person is only considered sound of limbs if he is capable. Therefore, if soundness exists, capacity necessarily exists at that very state. The objection is thus invalid on this premise. The author notes that al-Ash'ari also relied on this argument in his refutation of al-Ka'bi's work.
