Maturidi's Argument for Differentiating the First State
Al-Maturidi distinguishes the first state from later states regarding power and act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Maturidi's reasoning for why power cannot exist in the first state of a created being, and how this differentiates the first state from subsequent states.

Core Idea
Power always accompanies an act; no act exists in the first state, so no power.

In this passage, al-Maturidi engages with a theological debate about whether a human's first state is essentially the same as later states regarding power and action. He offers a clear argument for why the first state is unique: power must accompany an act, and the very first moment of existence cannot contain any act. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument from the opposing view to al-Maturidi's rebuttal.
The passage is dense with technical terms; don't worry about understanding everything immediately.
2
Identify the opposing view
Locate the view that al-Maturidi rejects: those who say soundness and safety are not power, and who treat the first state the same as later states. Notice how they allow possibility for power or incapacity equally in all states.
This view is attributed to unnamed opponents.
3
Understand al-Maturidi's differentiation
Find al-Maturidi's claim that the first state differs. He argues that power is always with the act, and the act of an originated being is motion or rest. Since these cannot exist in the first state (the moment of origination), power cannot exist there.
The key phrase: 'power is with the act' (al-qudra takūn ma'a al-fi'l).
4
Note the lenient answer and alternative definition
The passage mentions that al-Maturidi's answer is given leniently, treating act as motion/rest. For those who define motion and rest as two 'kawans', the argument reduces to a different form. Observe how the conclusion remains consistent.
This layer shows flexibility in argumentation without changing the core position.
5
Reflect on the takeaway
Summarize in your own words why the first state is different and why this matters for kalam discussions of causality and divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن قال منهم : إن الصحة والسلامة غير القدرة فإنه يقول: إنه يخلق في أول أحواله إما صحيحًا سليمًا قادرًا على الفعل، وإما صحيحًا سليمًا عاجزًا عن الفعل، ويجوز أن يكون في الحالة الثانية وما بعدها هكذا، فسوى بين الأحوال ولم يفرق بين الحالة الأولى وبين غيرها من الأحوال. وإليه ذهب الشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي - رحمه الله - في كتاب التوحيد وذكر في كتابه الذي ورد في كتاب وائل الأدلة على الكعبي الفرق بين الحالة الأولى وبين غيرها من الأحوال وقال: إنما لم يجز أن يكون قادرًا لأن القدرة تكون مع الفعل وفعل المحدث هو إما الحركة وإما السكون، ووجود كل واحد منهما محال في تلك الحالة فيستحيل ثبوت القدرة عليهما، ولا استحالة في ثبوت الحركة والسكون في غير تلك الحالة فلم يستحل ثبوت القدرة عليهما في غير تلك الحالة، ففرقنا بينهما لقيام دليل المفارقة وأنتم فيما نحن فيه تفرقون عند وجود دليل المساواة وانعدام دليل التفرقة. ثم هذا الجواب من الشيخ رحمه الله على طريق المساهلة وبناء الأمر على ما هو المتعارف من الفعل وهو الحركة والسكون فأما الكلام على الحقيقة على قول من يجعل الحركة والسكون كل واحد منهما كونين فهو أن كل حركة فعلان وكذا كل سكون وهو في ابتداء أحوال وجوده يفعل كونًا واحدًا، فيخرج على أحد الجوابين المتقدمين وهو التسوية بين الأحوال كلها إما جوازًا وإما منعًا، والله الموفق.
And among them is he who said: soundness and safety are not power, for he says: He creates in his first state either as sound, safe, and able to act, or as sound, safe, and incapable of acting, and it is permissible that he be in the second state and thereafter likewise, so he made equal between the states and did not differentiate between the first state and other states. And to this went the Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi - may Allah have mercy on him - in the Book of Tawhid, and he mentioned in his book which came in the book of Wail al-Adilla against al-Ka'bi the difference between the first state and other states, and said: It is not permissible that he be capable only because power is with the act, and the act of the originated thing is either motion or rest, and the existence of each of them is impossible in that state, so the establishment of power over them is impossible, and there is no impossibility in the establishment of motion and rest in other than that state, so the establishment of power over them is not impossible in other than that state, so we differentiated between them due to the presence of evidence of difference, while you in what we are discussing differentiate when evidence of equality exists and evidence of difference is absent. Then this answer from the Shaykh - may Allah have mercy on him - is on the path of leniency and building the matter upon what is customary of the act, namely motion and rest. As for the speech on the truth according to the one who makes motion and rest each of them two kawans, it is that every motion is two acts, and likewise every rest, and he in the beginning of his states of existence does one kawan, so it comes out according to one of the two previous answers, which is equality between all states, either permissively or prohibitively, and Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 818

Commentary
This passage presents al-Maturidi's argument for differentiating the first state of a created being from subsequent states regarding the presence of power (qudra) and act (fi'l). The passage begins by stating the view of those who hold that soundness and safety (al-sihha wa al-salama) are not identical with power. According to this view, God creates the human in the first state either as sound, safe, and capable of action, or as sound, safe, and incapable. The same possibilities apply to later states, so there is no differentiation made between the first state and others. Al-Maturidi rejects this undifferentiated view. In his Book of Tawhid and in his refutation of al-Ka'bi, he argues that the first state must be distinguished because power (qudra) always accompanies the act (fi'l). Since the act of an originated being is either motion (haraka) or rest (sukun), and neither motion nor rest can exist in the first state (the moment of origination), it is impossible for power to exist at that moment. In later states, however, motion and rest are possible, so power can exist. Thus, a differentiation is made based on evidence of difference (dāll al-mufāraqa). The passage then notes that al-Maturidi's answer is given in a lenient manner, building on the conventional understanding of act as motion and rest. For those who define motion and rest as two 'kawans' (entities), each motion is two acts, and the being in its first state performs one kawan; the argument then reduces to one of the two earlier answers (either allowing or forbidding equality between states). The passage concludes with 'And Allah is the Grantor of success.'

Key Terms
قدرة
فعل
حركة
سكون
حالة أولى
كون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the first state of a created being is identical to later states regarding the presence of power, because soundness and safety are considered separate from power.
Correction: Al-Maturidi shows that power cannot exist in the first state because power always accompanies an act, and no act (motion or rest) occurs in the state of origination. This necessitates a differentiation between the first state and subsequent states.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Maturidi hold that power cannot exist in the first state of a created being?
A) Because the first state is a state of incapacity by definition.
B) Because power always accompanies the act, and no act (motion or rest) is possible in that state.
C) Because the being has not yet been given power by God.
D) Because the first state is only a potential state, not actual.

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi argues that the first state of a created being is unique because power requires an act, and no act exists at the moment of origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often tell yourself, 'I could do it, I just haven't yet.' But this lesson says power is only with the act. In what area of your life are you deceiving yourself by treating potential as reality? When will you move from 'first state' thinking to actual motion?
?
Al-Maturidi argues that in the first state, motion and rest are impossible, so power is impossible. Are there spiritual acts—prayer, charity, repentance—that you've postponed, imagining you have the ability but simply haven't used it? What one concrete action will you perform today to break that illusion?

The Argument from Power: Establishing Taswiya Between States
The author presents a conditional argument in the aspect of power to establish equality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues that power (qudra) can be devoid of its act in multiple states, proving that the act is not an essential requisite of power, thus supporting taswiya.

Core Idea
If power can be empty of act once, it can be empty many times – the act is not essential.

The Mu'tazila objected that equality between the first state of existence and other states is invalid. The author refutes this by focusing on the aspect of power (qudra). He argues that if power can exist without the act at one moment, that shows the act is not a necessary concomitant of power's essence, so it can be absent in many moments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a conditional chain.
2
Identify the conditional reasoning
The author argues: if emptiness at one time → act not a requisite → many times possible. Conversely, if many times impossible → act is requisite → emptiness at one time impossible. Trace this logic.
3
Understand the conclusion
The act is not an essential requisite of power, so power can be empty many times. This supports equality between the first state and later states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن هذا الاعتراض فاسد على طريق النظر فإنا سوينا بين حالة الوجود وبين غيرها من الأحوال في الجانبين جميعًا - أعني جانب القدرة وجانب الفعل- فإنا قلنا في جانب القدرة : إنها لما لم تكن إلا للفعل وقد تخلو عنه وقتا مع وجود ذاتها فلم لا تخلو عنه أوقاتاً؟ إذ خلوها عنه وقتاً دليل أن ثبوت الفعل ليس من مقتضيات ذاتها إذ الذات لا تنفك عما تقتضيه، وإذا لم يكن الفعل من مقتضيات ذاتها جاز خلوها عنه أوقاتاً كثيرة، وإذا لم يجز خلوها عنه أوقاتا كثرة دل أنه من مقتضيات ذاتها فلا يتصور خلوها عنه وقتا. وقد قلنا في جانب الفعل : إن حصوله لما جاز مع عدم القدرة في الوقت الثاني جاز في الوقت العاشر لاستوائهما في العدم، فيكون الاعتراض الصحيح ببيان التفرقة وإبطال التسوية بين الحالة الأولى وبين غيرها من الأحوال في شيء آخر، فإنا نقول : لما ثبتت التسوية بما بينا فيما نحن فيه، ثبتت التسوية في الحكم وفي غيره من الأشياء لما ظهرت التفرقة في الحكم على التفرقة في المعنى، ولا حاجة بنا إلى بيان المعنى الموجب للتفرقة، وإنما يلزمنا ما اعترضوا به أن لو ادعينا التسوية بين أول الأحوال وبين غيرها من الأحوال مطلقا في المواضع أجمع، فأما إذا ادعينا ذلك في موضع مخصوص وأقمنا دلالة التسوية فالاشتغال بإثبات التفرقة في موضع آخر عن سنن الصواب وعدول عن طريق النظر. وبهذا تبين جهل المعتزلة بالحقائق وبناء الأمر على ظواهر لا دليل عليها ولا معنى تحتها.
Moreover, this objection is invalid in the way of speculation, for we made equal between the state of existence and other states in both aspects - I mean the aspect of power and the aspect of act. For we said in the aspect of power: when it is not but for the act, and it may be devoid of it at a time despite the existence of its essence, then why may it not be devoid of it at times? For its being devoid of it at one time is evidence that the establishment of the act is not from the requisites of its essence, since the essence is not free from what it necessitates. And if the act is not from the requisites of its essence, then its being devoid of it for many times is permissible. And if its being devoid of it for many times is not permissible, it indicates that it is from the requisites of its essence, so its being devoid of it at one time is inconceivable. And we said in the aspect of act: when its occurrence is permissible with lack of power in the second moment, it is permissible with its lack in the tenth moment, due to their equality in non-existence. So the correct objection is by stating the differentiation and invalidating the equality between the first state and other states in something else. For we say: when equality is established by what we have explained in what we are discussing, equality is established in the ruling and in other things, because the differentiation in the ruling appears based on differentiation in meaning, and we have no need to state the meaning necessitating differentiation. And what they object with would only be incumbent upon us if we claimed equality between the first state and other states absolutely in all matters. But if we claim that in a specific matter and establish the evidence of equality, then occupying oneself with establishing differentiation in another matter is deviation from the path of correctness and turning away from the method of speculation. And by this, the ignorance of the Mu'tazila of realities is made clear, and that they base matters on appearances with no evidence nor meaning beneath them.
Source pages p. 819

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection made against the position that establishes equality (taswiya) between the first state of existence and other states regarding power (qudra) and act (fi'l). The author argues that the objection is invalid according to the method of theological speculation. He first explains the reasoning in the aspect of power: power exists only for the act, yet it can be empty of the act at some moment while its essence remains. If it can be empty once, why not many times? If it can be empty once, that shows that the act is not an essential requisite (muqtada) of the power, because an essence is never free from its requisite. If the act were a requisite, power could never be empty of it. Thus, if many moments of emptiness are possible, that confirms the act is not a requisite. Conversely, if many moments are impossible, then the act is a requisite, and emptiness even at one moment is inconceivable. In the aspect of act, the author argues that if the act can occur without power in the second moment (as the opponent might allow), then it can also occur without power in the tenth moment, because both moments are equal in lacking power. Therefore, the only valid objection would be to prove a real differentiation between the first state and others on some other basis. However, once equality is established through the evidence provided, the ruling is consistent across all cases. The author concludes that the Mu'tazila are ignorant of true realities and rely on superficial appearances without evidence or sound meaning.

Key Terms

فعل
مقتضى ذات
تسوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila object that equality between states is invalid because the first state is unique.
Correction: The author proves that emptiness at one time is possible, so the act is not essential, allowing many times of emptiness.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude if power can be devoid of the act at one time?

B) The act is not a requisite of power's essence.
C) Power must always have the act.
D) The first state is unique.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of power being empty of act at one time proves the act is not an essential requisite, supporting equality across states.

?

?
This logical equality between states challenges you to see that God's power is not bound to produce constantly. Are you willing to accept that God might not act according to your timeline, and still believe His power is fully there?

The Argument from Act and the Refutation of the Objection
The author presents the argument from the aspect of act and concludes the refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from the aspect of act (fi'l) and how the author refutes the Mu'tazila objection by showing that equality is established and that the burden of proof lies on them.

Core Idea
If an act can occur without power in one moment, it can in any moment – equality is established.

Having dealt with power, the author now turns to the aspect of act. He argues that if the act can occur without power in the second moment, it can also occur without power in the tenth moment, because both moments are equal in lacking power. This leads to the conclusion that the only valid objection would be to show a real differentiation, but the author has already proven equality.

The author argues that if an act can occur without power in the second moment, it can occur without power in the tenth moment because both moments are equal in lacking power.
3
Grasp the point about the correct objection
The author explains that the only valid objection would be to show a real differentiation between the first state and other states, but since equality is established, the objection fails. He also clarifies that this equality is claimed only in this specific context, not absolutely.

Moreover, this objection is invalid in the way of speculation, for we made equal between the state of existence and other states in both aspects - I mean the aspect of power and the aspect of act. For we said in the aspect of power: when it is not but for the act, and it may be devoid of it at a time despite the existence of its essence, then why may it not be devoid of it at times? For its being devoid of it at one time is evidence that the establishment of the act is not from the requisites of its essence, since the essence is not free from what it necessitates. And if the act is not from the requisites of its essence, then its being devoid of it for many times is permissible. And if its being devoid of it for many times is not permissible, it indicates that it is from the requisites of its essence, so its being devoid of it at one time is inconceivable. For we say: when equality is established by what we have explained in what we are discussing, equality is established in the ruling and in other things, because the differentiation in the ruling appears based on differentiation in meaning, and we have no need to state the meaning necessitating differentiation. And what they object with would only be incumbent upon us if we claimed equality between the first state and other states absolutely in all matters. But if we claim that in a specific matter and establish the evidence of equality, then occupying oneself with establishing differentiation in another matter is deviation from the path of correctness and turning away from the method of speculation. And by this, the ignorance of the Mu'tazila of realities is made clear, and that they base matters on appearances with no evidence nor meaning beneath them.

Mu'tazila on the Intermediate State of the Agent
Mu'tazila claim that the agent is neither capable nor incapable when the act occurs without power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila inferred an intermediate state between power and incapacity from the possibility of an act occurring without power.

Core Idea
When power is absent but the act occurs, the agent is neither capable nor incapable.

In the ongoing discussion about the relationship between power and action, some Mu'tazila drew a striking conclusion: if an act can occur without power, then the agent cannot be described as capable (since power is absent) nor as incapable (since the act happened). They therefore posited an intermediate state. This lesson examines their reasoning as presented in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the logical sequence: the act's occurrence without power leads to a conclusion about the agent's description.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's inference
Find the exact claim: the agent will not be described as incapable nor as capable. This is the key novelty — an intermediate state.
The passage explicitly says 'لن يصير في تلك الحال موصوفًا بالعجز ولن يبقى موصوفًا بالقدرة'.
3
Understand the rationale
Why would the agent not be called incapable? Because incapacity would prevent the act, but the act occurs. Why not capable? Because power is absent. Thus a tertium quid.
This challenges the common assumption that power and incapacity exhaust the options.
4
Recognize the incompleteness
The passage cuts off mid-sentence. Expect that the author (al-Nasafi) will refute or analyze this claim on subsequent pages.
The lesson is based on a fragment; the complete argument includes a response.

Source Text

Commentary
This brief passage explains a consequence that some Mu'tazila drew from the earlier argument about the act occurring without power. The earlier argument states that if the act can occur without power in the second moment (because the agent lacked power at that time but the act still happened), then by the same logic it can occur without power in the tenth moment, since both moments are equal in the absence of power. From this, some Mu'tazila inferred that in the second moment, the agent is not described as 'incapable' (عاجز) nor as 'capable' (قادر). He is in an intermediate state. Consequently, the act proceeds from one who is neither capable nor incapable. The passage ends abruptly at the bottom of the page, leaving the sentence incomplete. The author likely continues by refuting or analyzing this claim on the following page.

The Philosopher's Argument on the States of Action and Power
Al-Nasafi narrates a philosopher's argument on the states of action and power, aiming to establish an intermediate state between capacity and incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a philosophical argument that attempts to derive an intermediate state between capacity and incapacity based on the states of action and existence.

Core Idea
The philosopher argues that during an act, the agent is neither capable nor incapable.

In this passage, al-Nasafi relates a philosopher's intricate argument about the relationship between the act and the agent's power. The philosopher aims to show that the state of actually performing an act is distinct from both the state of being capable (before the act) and the state of being incapable. This argument challenges the common classification of agents into merely capable or incapable, proposing a third intermediate state of actual action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow of the argument.
The philosopher uses technical terms like حالة (state) and إمكان (possibility).
2
Identify the key distinctions
Notice the philosopher's basic claims: the act has two states (non-existence and existence), and the human has three states (existence of the act, possibility, impossibility).
These are the building blocks of the argument.
3
Follow the reasoning to the conclusion
Trace how the philosopher maps states of the act to states of the human, and then to capacity and incapacity, ending with the intermediate state.
The conclusion that the agent is neither capable nor incapable is surprising and is meant to be refuted later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر هذا بعض من ينسب إلى الفلسفة (وهي العلم) بحقائق الأمور والوقوف على دقائق العلوم، ونسب مخالفيه في ذلك إلى العدول عن سبيل المعارف، وطول فيه وهول، وزعم أن الفعل له حالتان حالة عدم وحالة وجود ولا واسطة بينهما، والإنسان له أحوال ثلاثة وهي حالة وجود الفعل منه وحالة إمكانه وحالة امتناعه، وإحدى حالتي زمن الفعل تستغرق حالتين من الأحوال الثلاثة الأخر عدم الفعل، فإن حالة عدم الفعل تستغرق حالة امتناع الفعل من الإنسان وحالة إمكانه منه إذ الفعل ممن الفعل عنه ممتنع معدوم وكذا ممن الفعل منه ممكن، لأن الممكن أن يكون هو الممكن أن لا يكون، والفعل الموجود غير ممكن أن لا يكون، فالممكن إذا ما يكون معدومًا بعد، فإذا كانت إحدى حالتي الفعل وهي حالة العدم تستنفد هاتين الحالتين - أعني إمكان الفعل وامتناعه فقد تبين إذا أن الحالة الثالثة من الأحوال مقابلة للحالة الباقية من الحالتين - أعني أن الوجود مقابل الوجود – ثم حالة الامتناع حالة العجز، وحالة الإمكان حالة القدرة، فتبقى حالة وجود الفعل لا بمقابلة حالة القدرة لفوت صفة الإمكان عن الموجود، وحالة القدرة حالة الإمكان لا حالة الوجود، ولا بمقابلة حالة الامتناع التي هي حالة العجز لأن من يمتنع عنه الفعل يستحيل وجوده منه، وقد وجد في هذه الحالة فلم يكن عاجزًا، وجاء من هذا أن بين حالة القدرة وحالة العجز حالة واسطة وهي حالة وجود الفعل فلم يكن من منه الفعل عاجزًا ولا قادرًا.
Some of those attributed to philosophy (which is the knowledge of the realities of things and the grasping of the subtleties of the sciences) mentioned this, and he attributed his opponents in that to turning away from the path of cognitions, and he prolonged and made much of it. He claimed that the act has two states: a state of non-existence and a state of existence, with no intermediary between them. And the human has three states: the state of the existence of the act from him, the state of its possibility, and the state of its impossibility. And one of the two states of the act (which is the state of non-existence of the act) absorbs two of the three states: the state of impossibility of the act from the human and the state of its possibility from him. For the act from the one from whom it is impossible is non-existent, and likewise from the one from whom it is possible, because the possible is that which may be and may not be, while the existing act is not possible not to be. So the possible, when it becomes existent, is no longer non-existent. Thus, if one of the two states of the act—that is, the state of non-existence—exhausts these two states (namely, possibility and impossibility), then it becomes clear that the third state among the states corresponds to the remaining state of the two—that is, existence corresponds to existence. Then the state of impossibility is the state of incapacity, and the state of possibility is the state of capacity. So the state of existence of the act remains, not corresponding to the state of capacity because the attribute of possibility is lost from the existent; and the state of capacity is the state of possibility, not the state of existence; nor does it correspond to the state of impossibility which is the state of incapacity, because from whom the act is impossible, its existence from him is inconceivable, and yet it has come to exist in this state, so he is not incapable. And from this it follows that between the state of capacity and the state of incapacity there is an intermediate state, which is the state of existence of the act, so the one from whom the act proceeds is neither incapable nor capable.
Source pages p. 820

Commentary
This passage is a narration of a philosophical argument about the states of action and power. The philosopher is attributed to 'philosophy,' defined here as knowledge of realities and subtleties. He begins by claiming that opponents have deviated from the path of knowledge. Then he presents his argument: The act has two states—non-existence and existence—with no intermediary. The human has three states: the state of existence of the act, the state of possibility, and the state of impossibility. The state of non-existence of the act encompasses both possibility and impossibility: if an act is impossible, it is non-existent; if possible, it may be non-existent. The existing act is no longer possible not to be. Thus, the state of non-existence corresponds to the two states of possibility and impossibility. The remaining state, existence, corresponds to itself. Then he maps: impossibility is the state of incapacity, possibility is the state of capacity. The state of existence of the act remains: it does not correspond to capacity because the attribute of possibility is lost in the existent (since an existing act is no longer possible), and does not correspond to impossibility because impossibility would preclude existence altogether. Since the act does exist, the agent cannot be incapable. Therefore, there is an intermediate state between capacity and incapacity: the state of existence of the act, where the agent is neither capable nor incapable. This is the conclusion of the philosopher.

قدرة

إمكان

Misconception: This argument represents al-Nasafi's own theological position.
Correction: Al-Nasafi is narrating a philosopher's argument to refute it later; it is not his own view.

Checkpoint

B) Incapable ('ajiz)
C) Neither capable nor incapable
D) Both capable and incapable

Key Takeaway
The philosopher contends that the act's existence creates a unique state where the agent is neither capable (since possibility is gone) nor incapable (since the act is actual).

Uncovering Obfuscation: Al-Nasafi's Critique of the Philosopher's Use of 'Qudra'
Al-Nasafi uncovers the philosopher's terminological confusion regarding 'qudra'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi identifies the philosopher's error as a terminological conflation of two distinct meanings of 'qudra' (power), and why this distinction is crucial for the debate on causality.

Core Idea
The philosopher's mistake was not logical but terminological: conflating two meanings of 'power' into one.

Al-Nasafi begins his refutation not by attacking the philosopher's logic head-on, but by pinpointing a fundamental confusion: the philosopher has misunderstood how his opponent uses the term 'qudra'. This opening critique sets the stage for a deeper discussion on the nature of power and causality. In this passage, al-Nasafi accuses the philosopher of ignorance of the opponents' doctrines and of the reality of possibility, and then clarifies the two distinct meanings of 'power'.

Guided Path
1

The passage is incomplete; the argument continues beyond this excerpt.

Identify the two meanings of 'qudra'
Al-Nasafi distinguishes between (1) soundness of causes and well-being of limbs, and (2) the unknown meaning that is the reality of power. Understand each one.
The first is called power only because real power customarily occurs with it.

The philosopher took what his opponent uses for two distinct things and treated it as one. Why is this a fatal mistake?
This is the core critique: terminological confusion.

فأقول في الكشف عن هذا التمويه – وبالله التوفيق: [رد النسفي على الكلام السابق] إن هذا الكلام مع هذا التطويل والتهويل صدر عن الجهل بمذاهب الخصوم وجعل ما هو عند خصمه اسم لشيئين متغايرين اسمًا لشيء واحد، وعن الجهل بحقيقة الإمكان، وذلك لأن عند خصمه القدرة اسم لشيئين: أحدهما : سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات، وهي تسمى قدرة لحدوث القدرة فيها في المعتاد عند قصد الفصل وامتناع حدوثها عند فواتها. والثاني: المعنى المجهول للفعل وهو حقيقة القدرة، وهو الذي نتكلم فيه بعد وقبل. وللإنسان أحوال ثلاثة حالة عدم الأسباب وفساد الآلات أو عدمها، وكان الفعل فيها ممتنعاً لا لفساد الآلات بل لانعدام القدرة، إلا أن وجود القدرة مع فامتنع الفعل لامتناع وجود القدرة. وحالة وجود سلامة الأسباب وصحة ممتنع (٥) ، فسادها
I say in uncovering this obfuscation—and with God is success—[al-Nasafi's response to the previous speech]: Indeed, this speech, with all its prolongation and intimidation, issued from ignorance of the doctrines of the opponents and turning what is in the view of his opponent a name for two distinct things into a name for a single thing, and from ignorance of the reality of possibility. This is because, in the view of his opponent, 'power' is a name for two things: one of them is the soundness of causes and the well-being of limbs, and it is called power because of the occurrence of (real) power in it customarily when intending the act, and the impossibility of its occurrence when that is lacking. The second is the unknown meaning for the act, which is the reality of power, and it is what we discuss before and after. And the human has three states: the state of absence of causes and corruption of limbs or their absence, and in it the act is impossible—not due to corruption of limbs but due to the non-existence of power; however, the existence of power with [was?] impossible due to the impossibility of the existence of power. And the state of existence of soundness of causes and well-being of limbs [is?] impossible, its corruption...
Source pages p. 821

Commentary
This passage is the opening of al-Nasafi's refutation of the philosophical argument. He begins by stating his intention to uncover the 'obfuscation' in that argument, seeking divine assistance. He then identifies two errors: ignorance of the opponents' doctrines and ignorance of the reality of possibility. The key error is that the philosopher has taken a term ('power' or qudra) that is used by his opponent for two distinct things and treated it as a single thing. Al-Nasafi explains that for his opponent (presumably the Mutakallimun, especially the Maturidis and Ash'aris), 'power' (qudra) refers to two things: (1) the soundness of causes (salamat al-asbab) and well-being of limbs (sihhat al-alat), which is called power only because real power customarily occurs along with it when one intends an act; and (2) the 'unknown meaning' (al-ma'na al-majhul) which is the reality of power (haqiqat al-qudra) and is the actual subject of discussion. Al-Nasafi then mentions that the human has three states, but the text breaks off mid-explanation. The incomplete sentence appears to discuss the state of absence of causes and corruption of limbs, where the act is impossible due to lack of power, and the state of existence of sound causes, which is cut off.

Misconception: One might think al-Nasafi rejects the philosopher's argument entirely as a logical fallacy.
Correction: Al-Nasafi specifically pins the error on terminological confusion—the philosopher used a term for two distinct things as if it were one—rather than a logical fallacy.

A) He misunderstood the definition of 'act'

D) He ignored the role of God's power

?
The philosopher's argument was built on ignorance of his opponent's doctrines. In your own life, where might you be arguing against a caricature rather than the actual belief of another person or group? What cost does that ignorance have on your relationships and your own intellectual honesty?

When you debate or study theology, do you start with humility and prayer, or with self-reliance? What would it look like for you to truly depend on God for clarity in understanding truth?

Power Must Be Simultaneous with Its Act
Nasafi refutes two variants of the objection and draws the necessary conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why power (qudra) must be simultaneous with its object (maqdur), and why the claim that power can precede its act leads to false doctrines.

Core Idea
Power without an object is impossible.

Nasafi now confronts a subtle objection: if power persists after the act, could it be that the power at the moment of persistence is actually for another, future act? This would seem to preserve the simultaneity of power and its own act while allowing power to continue. But Nasafi shows this leads to contradictions and impossible consequences, reinforcing that power must be conjoined to its act at every moment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Note the two objections (starting with 'If they say') and Nasafi's responses.
2
Identify the two objections
The first objection claims power at persistence is for another act in the third moment. The second claims power at the second moment is for the act that occurs then. Identify how each differs.
The first objection involves power for a future act; the second for a simultaneous act.
3
Understand Nasafi's refutations
For the first: if power is always for a future act, then no act has its own power. For the second: if power in the second is for the act in the second, then power is simultaneous, contradicting their original claim.
Notice how Nasafi uses the opponent's own logic to nullify their position.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
Nasafi concludes that the only outcomes of their claim are the false doctrine of the survival of accidents and the impossible notion of a power without an object.
This shows the necessity of power being simultaneous with its act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قدرة على هذا الفعل الذي اقترن بها وقت البقاء ؟ أم كانت قدرة لا على هذا الفعل بل لفعل غيره يفعل بها في الثاني من وقت وجود هذا الفعل؟
فإن قالوا: هي في حالة البقاء قدرة على فعل آخر غير هذا الذي وجد في حال بقاءه وقدرة هذا الفعل هي فى أول أحوال وجودها ويتعقبها الفعل، ثم هي في الثاني في حال وجودها قدرة فعل آخر يوجد في الثالث من حال وجودها وهو قول بعضهم.
قلنا: هذا مثل الأول فإنها لما لم تكن فى كل حال قدرة لما يقارنها من الفعل بل هي قدرة على ما يتعقبه من الفعل كان كل فعل ثابتًا لا بما له من القدرة، ووجود ما هو قدرة على غيره لا يجدي نفعا ولا يدفع ما ألزمنا.
وإن قالوا: هي في الثاني قدرة على ما ثبت فيه من الفعل المقارن فيها.
قلنا: إذا كانت هي في الثاني قدرة على ما يثبت فيه من الفعل فكانت هي قدرة الفعل الذي اقترن بها، وبطل جميع سعيهم، واضمحل أكثر شبهاتهم، ولم يحصل لهم من القول بتقدمها على زمان وجود الفعل - مع أنها في الحال المتقدمة على وجود الفعل ليست بقدرة لهذا الفعل - إلا القول ببقاء الأعراض وهو باطل، والقول بوجود قدرة لا مقدور لها ولا مقارناً لها ولا متأخرا عنها، وهو محال.
A power upon this act that is conjoined to it at the time of persistence? Or was it a power not upon this act but for another act that is done by it in the second [moment] from the time of the existence of this act? If they say: It is, in the state of persistence, a power for another act other than this one which existed in the state of its persistence, and the power of this act is in the first of the states of its existence and the act follows it, then it is in the second, in the state of its existence, a power for another act that exists in the third of the states of its existence—and that is the saying of some of them. We say: This is like the first. For since it is not, in every state, a power for the act that is conjoined to it, but rather it is a power for what follows it from the act, then every act is established not by its own power, and the existence of what is a power for something else does not benefit nor repel what we have necessitated. And if they say: It is in the second a power for what is established in it of the conjoined act. We say: If it is in the second a power for what is established in it of the act, then it is the power of the act that is conjoined to it, and all their effort is nullified, and most of their doubts vanish, and they obtain from the saying of its precedence over the time of the existence of the act—while it is, in the state preceding the existence of the act, not a power for this act—only the saying of the survival of accidents, which is false, and the saying of the existence of a power that has no object, no concomitant, and no subsequent, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 825

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection about the nature of power (qudra) and its relation to the act (fi‘l). It begins by posing a question: Is the power that is conjoined to an act at the time of persistence a power for that act or for another act? The opponents claim that at the moment of persistence, the power is for another act, while the power for the current act existed in the first moment and the act followed; then in the second moment it becomes a power for another act that occurs in the third moment. Nasafi responds that this is like the first problem: if the power is not always a power for the act that accompanies it, but rather for what follows, then each act is established not by its own power, and having a power for something else is useless and does not repel the necessary conclusion. Then he considers a variant: if they say that in the second moment it is a power for the act that occurs in that moment (i.e., the conjoined act), then it becomes the power of the conjoined act, which nullifies their entire position. Their claim that power precedes the act leads only to the false doctrine of the survival of accidents (baqa' al-a'rad) and to the impossible notion of a power without an object (maqdur), without a concomitant, and without a subsequent, which is impossible (muhal). The argument shows that power must be simultaneous with its object.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power can exist without an object (maqdur).
Correction: This passage shows that a power without an object, concomitant, or subsequent is impossible (muhal). Power must always be for an act.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what follows from the claim that power precedes the act?
A) The survival of accidents and a power without an object
B) The act is created by God directly
C) Power is an eternal attribute
D) The act occurs by chance

Key Takeaway
Power must be simultaneous with its act; a power without an object is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought that your past promises or intentions alone are enough to cover your present actions, without checking if your current power is actually aligned with what you are about to do? How does Nasafi's argument expose the gap between your past intention and present action? Commit to one act today where you ensure your power (resolve) is simultaneous with the deed.
?
The lesson proves that a power without an object is impossible. In your spiritual life, are there beliefs you claim to hold (e.g., 'I believe in God') that have no corresponding action right now? What specific act of worship or obedience can you attach to that belief in this very moment?
?
Nasafi refutes the idea that power can shift between different acts over time. Consider a recurring sin you struggle with—do you assume that because you once resolved to stop, that past resolve is still effective? What would it mean to have your power of repentance be simultaneous with the act of turning away from that sin right now?

The Impossibility of an Act Coexisting with Power
Nasafi poses questions about whether the act can exist with power and argues that if power changes, it becomes incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, in Mu'tazili theology, an act cannot exist simultaneously with the power to perform it, and how Nasafi's dialectical argument forces opponents into contradiction.

Core Idea
Power and act cannot coexist; positing a change in power leads to logical impossibility.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi uses a dialectical interrogation to test the relationship between power (qudra) and act (fi‘l). He asks whether the act can exist at the same moment as the power, and then follows each possible answer to its logical conclusion. This argument reveals deep commitments about the nature of accidents and change. Pay close attention to how he forces his opponents into either conceding the point or admitting an absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the back-and-forth structure: it is a dialogue with an imaginary opponent.
The passage is dense; reading twice helps.
2
Identify the two main branches
Find where Nasafi offers the opponent two choices: either the act is possible with power or it is not. Follow each branch separately.
The first branch (no) leads to concession; the second (yes) leads to deeper questions.
3
Trace the second branch carefully
If the opponent says the act is impossible with power, Nasafi then asks about change over time. Note how he introduces the concepts of 'meaning' (ma‘na) and the nature of accidents.
The key move is that an accident cannot accept a meaning that changes it.
4
Understand the conclusion
See how each sub-answer leads to a problem: either the changing meaning has no effect, or the power before change was really an incapacity. Reflect on why this defeats the opponent's position.
The impossibility of an incapacity transforming into power is the final blow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : إذا كانت القدرة في زمان وجودها موجودة، أيستحيل وجود الفعل معها أم لا؟
فإن قالوا: لا، فقد أقروا بما ذهب إليه خصومهم، وبطل جميع ما يتعلقون به من الشبه.
وإن قالوا: نعم، قيل لهم: أليس أنها في الثاني من زمان وجودها غير ما كان في زمان وجودها؟ فلابد من بلى - قيل: وهل حدث فيها في الثاني معنى أوجب تغيرها عما كانت هي عليه في الأول أم لا؟
فإن قالوا: نعم، فقد ارتكبوا محالاً، لما أن العرض لا يقبل معنى يتغير به وقيل لهم وهل كانت موجبة ثبوت الفعل بدون ذلك المعنى الذي أوجب تغيرها؟
فإن قالوا: نعم، فلم يكن لوجود المعنى المغير أثر وكان وجوده كعدمه
وإن قالوا: لا، قيل : فإذا كانت قبل ثبوت التغير لم تكن قدرة بل كانت عجزاً إذ ما يستحيل ثبوت الفعل به عجز لا قدرة، وإنما صار قدرة عند حدوث التغير في الثاني، وقد قارنها الفعل فصح ما قلنا وبطل سعيكم مع ما فيه من استحالة انقلاب ما هو في حكم العجز قدرة.
Then it is said to them: When the power at the time of its existence exists, is the existence of the act with it impossible or not? If they say: No, then they have conceded what their opponents hold, and all the doubts they cling to are nullified. And if they say: Yes, it is said to them: Is it not that it is, in the second of the time of its existence, other than what it was at the time of its existence? So they must answer yes—it is said: And did a meaning occur in it in the second that necessitated its change from what it was upon in the first, or not? If they say: Yes, then they have committed an impossibility, because the accident does not accept a meaning by which it changes, and it is said to them: Was it causing the existence of the act without that meaning which necessitated its change? If they say: Yes, then the existence of the changing meaning had no effect, and its existence is like its non-existence. And if they say: No, it is said: So if, before the occurrence of the change, it was not a power but was an incapacity, for that through which the existence of the act is impossible is an incapacity, not a power, and it only became a power at the occurrence of the change in the second, and the act was conjoined to it, so what we said is correct and your effort is nullified, along with what is in it of the impossibility of the transformation of what is in the ruling of incapacity into power.
Source pages p. 825

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical interrogation of the opponents about the relationship between power (qudra) and the act (fi‘l). Nasafi asks: when power exists, is the act possible with it or not? If they say it is possible, they concede the point. If they say it is impossible, then he asks whether the power in the second moment is the same as in the first. They must answer no. Then he asks whether a meaning (ma‘na) occurred in the second moment that caused a change. If they say yes, they commit an impossibility because an accident (arad) cannot accept a meaning that changes it; moreover, if the act was caused without that meaning, the meaning had no effect. If they say no change occurred, then the power must have been an incapacity (‘ajz) before the change, since if the act is impossible through it, it is not power. It only becomes power at the moment of change, but then the act accompanies it, so their effort fails. The conclusion is that the transformation of what is essentially incapacity into power is impossible. This argument closes off the opponent's escape routes.

Key Terms
قدرة
فعل
عرض
معنى
تغير
عجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that power can change over time without any underlying cause, or that an accident can be altered by another accident.
Correction: Nasafi argues that accidents (like power) cannot accept a 'meaning' that changes them, because they are simple. Any change would require a cause, and if the cause is external, it leads to problems.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi conclude if the opponents say that no meaning (ma‘na) occurs in the second moment to change the power?
A) The power remains the same and the act becomes possible
B) The power must have been an incapacity before the change
C) The power becomes eternal
D) The act is impossible in both moments

Key Takeaway
The opponent's attempt to separate power and act in time collapses because any change in power either requires an impossible cause or reveals that the 'power' was really an incapacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument reveals that if you claim to have power but never act, that 'power' is actually incapacity. Where in your life do you say 'I can do it' but never do? Will you repent from this self-deception and attribute true power to Allah alone?
?
The Mu'tazilites held a logically flawed view because they refused to submit to revelation. What beliefs do you hold that might seem reasonable but actually contradict the truth revealed in the Qur'an and Sunnah? Are you willing to abandon them even if they feel comfortable?
?
Nasafi's argument shows that change in power is impossible without an external cause. When you intend to do good, do you rely on your own power or do you recognize that the actual action comes from Allah? How can you cultivate that reliance today in a specific action you plan to do?

The Impossibility of Power Being Identical When Act Is Impossible
Nasafi refutes Mu'tazili claim and defeats al-Sirafi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, in Nasafi's view, if a power remains identical across two moments, the impossibility of the act must also persist, and how this refutes the Mu'tazili position.

Core Idea
Identity of power implies identity of impossibility.

Nasafi here tackles a key Mu'tazili objection: that power can remain unchanged while the impossible act becomes possible. He argues that if the power is the very same entity, then whatever was impossible in the first moment must remain impossible in the second. This leads to a powerful reductio ad absurdum about essential relations, and ends with a historic triumph over al-Sirafi.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage through
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the dialectical structure: objection, reply, critique, and historical conclusion.
2
Identify the opponents' claim
The passage begins with 'If they say...' — what is the claim being refuted? Identify the assumption that no change occurred in the power between the two moments.
The opponents likely are Mu'tazila who hold that the act's impossibility can cease without a change in the power.
3
Trace Nasafi's reply
Follow his argument: if the power in the second moment is identical to the first, and the act was impossible in the first, then it remains impossible in the second. Note the phrase 'because it is the very thing' (because it is the very thing).
4
Understand the critique of essential attachment
Nasafi rejects the idea that the relationship between power and act is by essence (dhat). He shows that if it were by essence, then if the act is impossible through it in one state, it would be impossible in all states — which leads to absurdity.
The absurdity: incapacity could then be essentially related to the act and produce it.
5
Reflect on the historical note
The passage ends with mention of al-Sirafi, a Mu'tazili scholar who was defeated by this argument. Consider how this confirms the strength of Nasafi's reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لم تحدث فيها في الثاني معنى أوجب تغيرها عما كانت في الأول.
قلنا: إذا كانت هي في الأول موجودة ويستحيل تعلقها بالفعل وثبوته بها يستحيل ذلك أيضا في الثاني، لأنها عين ما كان يستحيل تعلقه بالفعل ويستحيل ثبوته به والقول بوجوب تعلق شيء بشيء ووجوب ثبوت الثاني بالأول أن ذلك كان لذاته مما يستحيل تعلقه بالثاني وثبوت الثاني بالأول قول محال، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز القول بوجوب تعلق العجز بالفعل ووجوب ثبوت الفعل به في حال وإن ووجوب استحال ذلك في حال من غير حدوث معنى مغير، وهذا عين المحال. وقد أورد هذا الفصل على السيرافي بشيراز فانقطع أفحش انقطاع.
If they say: No meaning occurred in it in the second that necessitated its change from what it was in the first. We say: If it is, in the first, existent and its attachment to the act is impossible, and the act's existence through it is impossible, then that is also impossible in the second, because it is the very thing whose attachment to the act was impossible and its existence through it is impossible. And the saying that the attachment of something to something and the necessity of the second's existence through the first is due to its essence, when it is impossible for it to be attached to the second and for the second to exist through the first, is an impossible saying. And if that were permissible, it would be permissible to say that incapacity necessarily attaches to the act and that the act necessarily exists through it in some state, while its impossibility in another state without the occurrence of a changing meaning [is impossible], and this is the very impossibility. And this chapter was presented to al-Sirafi in Shiraz, and he was cut off in the most disgraceful manner.
Source pages p. 826

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that no change occurs in the power. Nasafi argues that if the power remains identical in the second moment, then the impossibility of the act's existence through it that applied in the first moment must apply in the second, because it is the very same thing (ayn). He then critiques the idea that a thing can have an essential relationship to another such that the second necessarily exists through it, even when that relationship is impossible in one state and possible in another without a change. This would allow the absurdity that incapacity could be essentially related to an act and produce it. The argument shows that consistency demands that if the act is impossible through a thing at one time, it remains impossible unless the thing changes. The passage ends with a historical note that this argument was presented to al-Sirafi, a Mu‘tazili, and he was utterly defeated.

Key Terms
تعلق
ذات
عجز
محال
سيرافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the relationship between power and act can change without any change in the power itself.
Correction: Nasafi argues that if the power is identical, the impossibility of the act must also be identical, because the relationship depends on the essence of the power, not on external factors.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi say about the claim that the attachment of power to an act is due to its essence (dhat)?
A) It is a valid argument
B) It is impossible if the act is impossible through it in one state
C) It is the basis of causality
D) It applies only to God

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's argument shows that if power remains identical across two moments, the impossibility of the act must persist, refuting the Mu'tazili position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever blamed circumstances for your failures while ignoring that your own character—your 'essence'—remained unchanged? What specific change in yourself must you seek to turn impossibility into possibility?
?
Nasafi argues that if your power is identical, impossibility persists. Where in your life do you keep using the same strategies and expecting different results? What does this lesson demand you transform within?
?
Consider the concept of 'essential attachment' in this passage: your actions flow from who you are. If your repentance feels impossible, is it because your core self has not changed? What would it mean for you to truly become a new creation?

intermediate

The Impossibility of Cause Without Effect
Proof that cause cannot be devoid of effect using attribute examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the cause cannot be devoid of its effect, using examples from attributes, and how this principle underlies the Sunni rejection of specifying legal causes.

Core Idea
Cause and effect must coexist; separating them leads to absurdity.

This passage presents a foundational kalam principle: cause and effect are inseparable. The author defends the Sunni position against the Mu'tazila by showing that allowing a cause to exist without its effect leads to logical contradictions, using motion and blackness as examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice the author starts by citing the position of the verifiers (al-muḥaqqiqūn).
2
Identify the two main claims
The passage makes two key claims: 1) the Sunni rejection of specifying legal causes, 2) the simultaneous occurrence of effects with their causes. Find where each is stated.
The second claim is directly linked to the proof that follows.
3
Analyze the proof by analogy
The author uses examples from attributes (motion and blackness) to demonstrate that cause cannot be devoid of effect. Understand how allowing a cause without effect leads to an absurdity: motion in a non-moving body.
The same reasoning applies to all attributes and their subjects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا أبى المحققون من أئمتنا في الفقه المتمسكون بمذهب السنة والجماعة القول بتخصيص العلل الشرعية ونسبوه إلى المعتزلة، لما فيه من القول بخلو العلة عن المعلول، وكذا قالوا باقتران المعلولات بعللها ولم يجوزوا تقدم العلل عليها. ثم ما يحقق استحالة القول بجواز خلو العلة عن المعلول أزمنة كثيرة أو زمانًا واحدًا أن قيام الحركة بالجسم علة الصيرورته متحركا فكذا السواد مع الأسود وكذا كل صفة مع ما قامت به، وكان في القول بما يقوله المعتزلة جواز خلو قيام الحركة بالجسم عن صيرورته متحركا فتكون الحركة قائمة بما ليس بمتحرك والسواد قائمًا بما ليس بأسود، وهذا بإجماع العقلاء باطل خارج عن المعقول، فكذا هذا.
For this reason, the verifiers among our imams in jurisprudence who adhere to the way of the Sunna and the Community rejected the [permitted] specification of legal causes (al-‘ilal al-shar‘iyya) and attributed it to the Mu‘tazila, because it involves the saying that the cause can be devoid of the effect. Likewise they affirmed the simultaneous occurrence of effects with their causes and did not allow the priority of causes over them. Then what verifies the impossibility of allowing the cause to be devoid of the effect for many times or for a single time is that the subsistence of motion in a body is the cause of its being moving; likewise blackness with black, and likewise every attribute with that in which it subsists. And in saying what the Mu‘tazila say — allowing the subsistence of motion in a body to be devoid of its being moving — then motion would subsist in what is not moving, and blackness would subsist in what is not black. This is, by consensus of the rational people, false and outside the intelligible. So is this.
Source pages p. 828

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the cause (al-‘illa) cannot be devoid of the effect (al-ma‘lūl). The author begins by citing the position of the true scholars (al-muḥaqqiqūn) of Ahl al-Sunna who reject the concept of specifying legal causes (takhṣīṣ al-‘ilal al-shar‘iyya) because it implies separation of cause and effect. Instead, they affirm that effects occur simultaneously with their causes and do not allow causes to precede them. Then the author provides a rational demonstration: the subsistence of motion in a body is the cause of the body being moving. If we allow the cause to be devoid of the effect for any length of time, then motion could subsist in a body without the body being moving, which is absurd. The same applies to blackness and being black, and to every attribute and its subject. Such an idea is unanimously rejected as irrational. The author concludes that the same absurdity applies to the relationship between power (qudra) and act (fi‘l): power is the cause and the act is the effect; they cannot be separated in time.

Key Terms
المحققون (al-muḥaqqiqūn)
العلل الشرعية (al-‘ilal al-shar‘iyya)
خلو العلة عن المعلول (khalā' al-‘illa ‘an al-ma‘lūl)
اقتران المعلولات بعللها (iqtirān al-ma‘lūlāt bi-‘ilalihā)
الحركة (al-ḥaraka)
السواد (al-sawād)
إجماع العقلاء (ijmā‘ al-‘uqalā’)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that legal causes can be specified because they are not real causes, but the author argues the same logical principle applies.
Correction: The logical principle of cause-effect simultaneity applies to all causes, including legal causes. Specification would imply the cause exists without its effect, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to prove that the cause cannot be devoid of the effect?
A) Fire and burning
B) Motion and the body being moving
C) Power and the capable agent
D) The sun and the day

Key Takeaway
In kalam, cause and effect are inseparable; allowing the cause to exist without its effect leads to logical absurdity, as shown by examples from attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an action you believe you have 'caused' yourself, separate from God's power. This lesson argues that cause and effect cannot be separated. Does your belief that your action caused something imply that you are the cause? If God is the ultimate cause, how do you reconcile your feeling of causality with this principle? Will you commit to seeing every effect as directly from God?
?
You may have prayed for something and not seen an immediate effect. Does this lesson imply that the cause (your prayer) must have an effect? How does this shape your expectation of prayer? Are you willing to believe that the effect is simultaneous even if you don't perceive it, and to trust that God's response is immediate in a way beyond your senses?

Power as Cause for the Act and Refutation of Bahshamiyya
Proof that power is a cause for the act and introduction of Bahshamiyya objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that power (al-qudra) functions as a cause (illa) for the act (fi'l), and how this refutes the Bahshamiyya view that power is merely a means (sabab).

Core Idea
Power is the cause that makes an act an act, not just a means.

This lesson presents the Maturidi proof that divine power runs as a cause for the act, using the example of trembling vs voluntary movement. It then introduces the Bahshamiyya objection that power is merely a means, setting up the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the argument.
Pay attention to the comparison between trembling and voluntary movement.
2
Understand the proof from movement
Identify why voluntary movement is considered an act while trembling is not. The key is the attachment of power.
The author claims the two movements are identical except for power.
3
Grasp the causal relation
Recognize that power is the cause (illa) that makes the movement an act. Without power, no act exists.
4
Note the Bahshamiyya position
The Bahshamiyya reject causality and instead view power as a means (sabab) to the act.
They distinguish between cause and means.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the author insists on causality rather than means. The passage sets up a refutation that continues beyond this excerpt.
This connects to broader debates on divine attributes and acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل ما ادعينا من جريان القدرة مع الفعل مجرى العلل المعلولات أن لا مع فعل لمن لا قدرة له ولا ثبوت له إلا بذاتها، وما افترقت حركة المرتعش وحركة المختار في كون الثانية فعلاً لمن قام به دون الأولى إلا لثبوت الثانية بقدرته، وخروج الأولى عن قدرة من قامت هي به إذ فيما وراء تعلق القدرة بأحدهما دون الأخرى منهما مساواة من جمع الوجوه. والبهشمية يأبون القول بأن القدرة تجري مجرى العلل للأفعال، ويزعمون أنها تجري مجرى الأسباب للمسببات ويقولون في الفرق بين السبب والعلة: إن...
And the proof of what we have claimed — that power runs with act in the manner of causes to effects — is that there is no act for one who has no power, and no existence for it except through its essence. And the movement of the trembling person and the movement of the voluntary agent differ only in that the second is an act for the one in whom it subsists while the first is not, except because the second is established by his power, and the first is outside the power of the one in whom it subsists. For beyond the attachment of power to one of them without the other, they are equal from all aspects. The Bahshamiyya refuse the statement that power runs in the manner of causes for acts, and claim that it runs in the manner of means (asbāb) for results (musabbabāt), and they say concerning the difference between the means and the cause: that...
Source pages p. 828

Commentary
This passage provides the author's proof that power (al-qudra) is a cause (illa) for the act (fi'l), and then introduces the opposing view of the Bahshamiyya (followers of Abu Hashim). The proof runs as follows: there is no act for someone without power, and the act has no existence except through the essence of power. The difference between an involuntary movement (e.g., a trembling person's movement) and a voluntary movement (al-mukhtār) is that the latter is considered an act of the person because it is established by his power, while the former is not. Apart from the attachment of power, the two movements are identical in all other respects. Therefore, power is the distinguishing factor that makes the movement an act, and it functions as a cause. The Bahshamiyya, however, reject this causality relation and instead claim that power is a means (sabab) to the result (musabbab). The text cuts off as they begin to explain their distinction between cause and means, but the author's position is clear: power is a cause, and the act is its effect, simultaneous and necessary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think power merely accompanies the act without being its cause, or that the act could exist without power.
Correction: The author demonstrates that without power, no act exists, and the difference between an involuntary and voluntary movement is precisely the attachment of power, establishing it as the cause.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is voluntary movement considered an act while trembling movement is not?
A) Because voluntary movement is more complex
B) Because voluntary movement is established by the agent's power
C) Because trembling movement has no external cause
D) Because voluntary movement is always good

Key Takeaway
Power is an essential cause for the act; without it, no act exists, and the difference between involuntary and voluntary movement proves this causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Bahshamiyya treat power as a mere means, but the author insists it directly causes the act. Do you see your abilities as tools God gave you that you can use however you wish, or as causes that inevitably produce consequences you must answer for? How will this change your approach to a habit you have struggled to change?

Why a Cause Cannot Be Separated from Its Effect
Proof by absurdity that cause and effect are inseparable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why separating a cause from its effect leads to logical absurdity, as argued by the Maturidi verifiers.

Core Idea
A cause without its effect is like motion without something moving.

Before we dive into the passage, consider a simple question: Can there be a cause that doesn't produce its effect? The Mu'tazila said yes for legal causes. Al-Nasafi and the Maturidi verifiers said no—and they offer a proof that shows why such a separation is impossible. This lesson walks through that proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let the argument flow naturally.
2
Identify the key claim
Notice that the author rejects the idea of 'takhsis al-'illah' (specification of legal causes) because it implies a cause can exist without its effect.
This claim is attributed to the Mu'tazila.
3
Understand the proof by absurdity
The author gives an example: motion subsisting in a body is the cause of that body being moving. If the cause could exist without the effect, then we could have motion in something not moving. That is absurd.
The same logic applies to blackness and any other attribute.
4
Reflect on the consensus argument
Notice that the author appeals to the consensus of rational people. This shows that the absurdity is not just theological but also logical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا أبى المحققون من أئمتنا في الفقه المتمسكون بمذهب السنة والجماعة القول بتخصيص العلل الشرعية ونسبوه إلى المعتزلة، لما فيه من القول بخلو العلة عن المعلول، وكذا قالوا باقتران المعلولات بعللها ولم يجوزوا تقدم العلل عليها. ثم ما يحقق استحالة القول بجواز خلو العلة عن المعلول أزمنة كثيرة أو زمانًا واحدًا أن قيام الحركة بالجسم علة الصيرورته متحركا فكذا السواد مع الأسود وكذا كل صفة مع ما قامت به، وكان في القول بما يقوله المعتزلة جواز خلو قيام الحركة بالجسم عن صيرورته متحركا فتكون الحركة قائمة بما ليس بمتحرك والسواد قائمًا بما ليس بأسود، وهذا بإجماع العقلاء باطل خارج عن المعقول، فكذا هذا.
For this reason, the verifiers among our imams in jurisprudence who adhere to the school of the Sunna and the community rejected the specification of legal causes and attributed it to the Mu'tazila, because it involves claiming that a cause can be empty of its effect. Likewise, they affirmed the concomitance of effects with their causes and did not allow the causes to precede them. Then, what proves the impossibility of permitting a cause to be empty of its effect for many times or even a single time is that the subsistence of motion in a body is the cause of its becoming moving; similarly, blackness with the black, and likewise every attribute with that in which it inheres. And if one were to permit, as the Mu'tazila say, the separation of the subsistence of motion in a body from its becoming moving, then motion would subsist in that which is not moving, and blackness would subsist in that which is not black. This, by the consensus of rational people, is false and beyond reason. So likewise is this.
Source pages p. 828

Commentary
This passage is a proof by absurdity. The author begins by stating that the verifiers among Hanafi jurists rejected the idea that legal causes can be specified (i.e., their efficacy can be suspended) and attributed that view to the Mu'tazila. The reason is that it implies a cause can exist without its effect. Instead, they insisted that effects are always concomitant with their causes and that causes do not precede them. Then the author provides a logical proof: take the example of motion subsisting in a body—this subsistence is the cause of the body being moving. Similarly, blackness is the cause of something being black. If we allow a cause to exist without its effect, then motion could subsist in a body that is not moving, and blackness could subsist in something not black. This is absurd by consensus. Therefore, the original position—that causes can be separated from effects—is false.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
تخصيص
قيام
حركة
سواد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Legal causes can be specified (takhsis al-'illah) so that they exist without their effect under certain conditions.
Correction: The author argues that specification implies a cause existing without its effect, which leads to logical absurdity (e.g., motion without a moving body).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what logical absurdity follows from separating a cause from its effect?
A) A cause would precede its effect.
B) Motion could subsist in something that is not moving.
C) The Mu'tazila would be correct.
D) Legal causes would become invalid.

Key Takeaway
A cause and its effect are inseparable; claiming otherwise leads to contradictions that every rational person recognizes as false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you act as if you can have the 'cause' without its 'effect'? Consider: you want the comfort of faith (cause) without the transformation of obedience (effect). What is one concrete step you can take today to reconnect them?
?
This lesson argues that a cause without its effect is logically impossible. Yet many of us claim to believe in God but remain unchanged. How does this lesson expose a contradiction between your claimed beliefs and your actual life? What will you do about it?
?
Think of a specific sin you struggle with. If you truly accepted that the cause (the sin) inevitably brings its effect (spiritual harm), would you still indulge in it? What does your continued indulgence reveal about what you really believe?

Power and Action as Cause and Effect
Proof that power and action are cause and effect via voluntary vs. involuntary movement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author proves that power (قدرة) and action are related as cause and effect, using the example of voluntary vs. involuntary movement.

Core Idea
Power is the cause that makes an act an action.

The author now presents a second proof that power and action are connected as cause and effect. He argues that without power, no action can exist, and then illustrates this with a clear contrast: the trembling person's movement versus the voluntary agent's movement. This concrete example helps us see why power is essential for an action to be attributable to the agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully and then the English translation. Let the author's own words set the stage for the argument.
Notice the phrase 'فيما وراء تعلق القدرة بأحدهما دون الأخرى' which highlights the key difference.
2
Identify the author's claim
The author claims that power runs with action as a cause runs with its effect. Find the evidence he offers: that no action exists without power, and its existence depends on power.
3
Analyze the example of movement
Compare the trembling person's movement (involuntary) with the voluntary agent's movement. Understand why only the voluntary one is called an action: because it is caused by the person's power.
The phrase 'مساواة من جمع الوجوه' means that apart from the connection of power, the two movements are alike in all other respects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل ما ادعينا من جريان القدرة مع الفعل مجرى العلل المعلولات أن لا مع فعل لمن لا قدرة له ولا ثبوت له إلا بذاتها، وما افترقت حركة المرتعش وحركة المختار في كون الثانية فعلاً لمن قام به دون الأولى إلا لثبوت الثانية بقدرته، وخروج الأولى عن قدرة من قامت هي به إذ فيما وراء تعلق القدرة بأحدهما دون الأخرى منهما مساواة من جمع الوجوه.
And the proof of what we claim—that power runs with action in the course of causes and effects—is that there is no action for one who has no power, and no existence for it except by it itself. And the movement of a trembling person and the movement of a voluntary agent differ only in that the second is an action for the one in whom it subsists while the first is not, because the second is established by his power, while the first is outside the power of the one in whom it subsists. For beyond the connection of power to one of them and not to the other, there is equality in all respects.
Source pages p. 828

Commentary
This passage provides a second proof. The author claims that power (قدرة) and action are related as cause and effect. The evidence: an action cannot exist without power, and its existence depends on power. Then he gives a concrete example: compare the involuntary movement of a person with tremors to the voluntary movement of a person who acts by choice. Both are movements, but only the voluntary one is considered an action (فعل) of the person, because it is caused by his power. The involuntary movement is not within his power. Therefore, the presence or absence of power determines whether something is an action or not, confirming the causal relationship.

Key Terms
قدرة
فعل
حركة المرتعش
حركة المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that power is merely a condition for action, not a cause.
Correction: The author insists that power is a cause that necessitates the action, as shown by the fact that without power, no action can exist.

Checkpoint
Why is the movement of a trembling person not considered an action according to the passage?
A) Because it is involuntary.
B) Because it is not caused by his power.
C) Because it is not intentional.
D) Because it is not a movement.

Key Takeaway
Power is the causal determinant that distinguishes a voluntary action from an involuntary movement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an action you performed today that you consider a sin. Was it truly within your power to avoid it, or did it happen involuntarily like the trembling of a person with tremors? How does the answer change your responsibility before Allah, and what does that mean for your repentance?
?
Consider the difference between a gesture you made out of anger and a gesture you made out of kindness. Both are movements, but only one was likely voluntary. Are you treating them the same in your self-accountability? How can you better align your voluntary actions with what pleases Allah?
?
If power is what makes an action truly yours, then what does it mean for your deeds when you act without conscious intention—when you are on autopilot? How can you cultivate more presence and intentionality in your daily actions to ensure they are truly your deeds, accountable before Allah?
