Refutation of the Bahshami Distinction Between Cause and Reason
The author refutes the Bahshami distinction between cause and reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Bahshamiyya distinction between cause and reason and why it fails according to Maturidi theologians.

Core Idea
The Bahshamiyya's distinction between cause and reason does not solve the problem of separation.

The Bahshamiyya, followers of Abu Hashim, proposed a subtle distinction between 'cause' and 'reason' to avoid attributing direct causal power to God's attributes. They claimed that power (qudra) is a 'reason' for action, not a 'cause'. In this passage, the author dismisses this distinction as incoherent and shows that even by their own admission, reasons necessitate their effects, so the problem remains.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the literal translation to understand the precise claims.
Notice the technical terms 'cause' (علة) and 'reason' (سبب).
2
Identify the Bahshami claim
Find where the Bahshamiyya define 'cause' and 'reason'. What do they say about power?
They say power is a reason (سبب), not a cause (علة).
3
Understand the author's critique
The author argues that even if we accept the distinction, reasons necessitate their effects by essence, so the problem of separation (how an attribute can exist without its effect) remains.
The author calls the distinction 'nonsense' (هذيان).
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this debate matters for the Muslim understanding of divine attributes and causality. How does this refutation support the Ash'ari/Maturidi position?

والبهشمية يأبون القول بأن القدرة تجري مجرى العلل للأفعال، ويزعمون أنها تجري مجرى الأسباب للمسببات ويقولون في الفرق بين السبب والعلة: إن العلة ما توجب حالاً، والسبب ما توجب ذاتا، وكذا سمعت بعضهم ويعنون بذلك أن الحركة علة لثبوت حال للجسم يفارق بها ذاتاً ليست بمتحركة، فكانت الحال مقتضاة للحركة، ومقتضية لكون الجسم متحركا على ما بينا هذا الهذيان في مسألة الصفات وكشفنا عن وجوه بطلانه. والقدرة توجب ذاتا وهو الفعل، والفعل في نفسه ليس بحال بل الحال من مقتضيات الفعل، وهذا كله مع بطلانه لا ينفعهم لأنهم أقرّوا أن السبب يوجب المسبب وإيجابه إياه باعتبار ذاته، فلا يجوز خلو عن ذلك الإيجاب، وفيه ما أوردنا على سلفه. ثم إن أبا هاشم كما أحدث هذا المذهب الفاسد، قيل له: أرأيت لو كان هذا القادر الخالي عن الفعل إيجابًا وتركّاً مدة عمره لو كان مأمورًا بفعل منهيًا عن فعل آخر ماذا يكون حاله؟ فقال: يكون عاصيًا مستحقاً للذم والعقاب لا على فعل لأنه لم يفعل ما أمر به مع قدرته عليه وتوفر آلاته وارتفاع الموانع عنه. فقيل له: كيف صار مستحقاً للعقاب بأن لم يفعل ما أمر به، والمأمور بفعل إذا لم يفعله فقد تركه، وترك المأمور به حرام فاستحق العقاب على الفعل وهو ترك المأمور به إذ تركه فعل منهي عنه قبيح فقد ارتكبه التارك، فهذا إذا خلا عن الفعل المأمور به وعن تركه لم يصر مطيعا بفعله ولا عاصيًا بارتكاب تركه المنهي عنه فكيف صار مستحقاً للعقاب بل للخلود لا على فعل؟ وهل صار مطيعا مستحقاً للمدح والثواب بأن لم يفعل ما نهى عنه وهو الترك؟ وهل هذا منك إلا القول بأن الله تعالى يخلد أحدًا في النار لا على فعل حصل منه، والأمة أجمعت على خلاف هذا، وسلفك يكفرون أهل السنة بقولهم باستحقاق العقاب على فعل خلقه الله
And the Bahshamiyya reject the view that power runs in the course of causes for actions, claiming that it runs in the course of reasons for the things caused. They say regarding the difference between a cause and a reason: The cause is that which necessitates a state, while the reason is that which necessitates an entity. Likewise, I heard some of them—and by that they mean that motion is a cause for the subsistence of a state in the body by which it differs from an entity that is not moving. Thus, the state is required by motion and requires the body to be moving, as we have explained this nonsense in the issue of attributes and uncovered the aspects of its falsehood. And power necessitates an entity, which is the action, and the action itself is not a state, but the state is among the requirements of the action. All of this, despite its falsehood, does not benefit them because they admitted that the reason necessitates the thing caused, and its necessitation of it by virtue of its essence, so it is not permissible for it to be devoid of that necessitation. And in it is what we have objected to its predecessor. Then, just as Abu Hashim originated this corrupt doctrine, he was asked: 'Do you not see that if this capable person, devoid of both performing and leaving an action for his entire lifetime, were commanded to do something and forbidden from doing something else, what would his situation be?' He said: 'He would be a disobedient person deserving blame and punishment, not for an action, because he did not do what he was commanded while being able to do it, with his faculties complete and obstacles removed.' It was said to him: 'How can he become deserving of punishment for not doing what he was commanded? The one commanded to do something, if he does not do it, has abandoned it. And abandoning what is commanded is forbidden, so he deserves punishment for the action, which is abandoning what is commanded, since his abandonment is a forbidden ugly act that the abandoner has committed. So if he is devoid of both the commanded action and its abandonment, he does not become obedient by doing it nor disobedient by committing its forbidden abandonment. So how can he deserve punishment—and even eternal punishment—not for an action? Does he become deserving of praise and reward for not doing what he was forbidden, i.e., for abandonment? Is this not from you the claim that Allah will eternally punish someone in the Fire not for an action he committed? And the community has unanimously agreed contrary to this, while your predecessors declare the People of the Sunna to be disbelievers for their statement that punishment is deserved for an action created by Allah...'
Source pages p. 828

Commentary
This passage refutes two related positions. First, the Bahshamiyya (followers of Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i) distinguish between cause (علة) and reason (سبب): they say a cause necessitates a state (like being moving), while a reason necessitates an entity (like an action). They claim power is a reason, not a cause. The author dismisses this as nonsense and points out that they themselves admit that a reason necessitates its effect by its essence, so it cannot be devoid of the effect. Thus, their distinction does not save them from the problem of separation. Second, the author recounts an objection to Abu Hashim himself. Abu Hashim was asked about a person who is capable but does not act at all for his whole life, while being commanded to do one thing and forbidden from another. Abu Hashim said such a person would be sinful and deserve punishment, not for an action, but for simply not doing what he was commanded. The objection is that if the person neither does the commanded act nor abandons it (since abandonment is the act of not doing, but he did not commit that act either—he simply did nothing), then he cannot be considered sinful. The objector argues that the only way to be sinful for not obeying a command is by committing the forbidden act of abandoning the commanded act. But if he does nothing, he does not commit abandonment either. Therefore, Abu Hashim's view leads to the conclusion that Allah could punish someone eternally without any action, which is contrary to the consensus of the community. Moreover, Abu Hashim's own predecessors (the Mu'tazila) declare the Sunnis disbelievers for saying that punishment is for an action created by Allah (i.e., they think Sunnis attribute evil actions to Allah), but here Abu Hashim is advocating punishment without any action, which is even more extreme.

Key Terms

سبب
حال
ذات
قدرة
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that calling power a 'reason' instead of a 'cause' avoids the problem of separation between attribute and effect.
Correction: The author shows that reasons still necessitate their effects by essence, so the problem remains.

Checkpoint
According to the Bahshamiyya, what is the difference between a cause and a reason?
A) A cause necessitates a state, a reason necessitates an entity.
B) A cause necessitates an entity, a reason necessitates a state.
C) A cause is temporal, a reason is eternal.
D) A cause is essential, a reason is accidental.

Key Takeaway
The Bahshamiyya's distinction between cause and reason does not solve the problem of separation; reasons necessitate their effects by essence just as causes do.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Bahshamiyya tried to escape the problem of separation by redefining terms. Do you ever use clever reasoning to avoid the full implications of Allah's absolute power in your life? What distinction are you hiding behind right now?
?
This lesson shows that every 'reason' you trust—your health, your wealth, your abilities—is directly necessitated by Allah's will. If you truly believed that nothing exists independently of His power, how would your fear and hope change today?
?
The author calls their distinction 'nonsense'. Are there beliefs or habits you hold that, upon honest reflection, are just intellectual cover for avoiding submission? What would it cost you to abandon them?

Causality and Created Things

7 min
Objection to Abu Hashim: Punishment Without Action
The author recounts and refutes Abu Hashim's view on punishment without action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to Abu Hashim's claim that a person who does nothing can be sinful and deserve punishment without any action.

Core Idea
If a person does nothing, he cannot be considered disobedient because disobedience requires an action.

Abu Hashim proposed a scenario where a capable person does nothing—neither performs a commanded act nor abandons it. He claimed such a person would be sinful and deserve punishment for simply not doing the commanded act. The objector argues that this is incoherent because 'abandoning' is itself an action, and if the person does nothing at all, he has not committed abandonment either. Thus, Abu Hashim's view leads to punishment without any action, which contradicts Islamic consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the scenario presented to Abu Hashim
Note the scenario: a person is capable but does nothing—neither does the commanded act nor leaves it—for his entire life, while being commanded and forbidden.
This is a hypothetical to test the principles of moral responsibility.
2
Understand Abu Hashim's answer
Abu Hashim says the person is disobedient and deserves punishment, not for an action, but for not doing what he was commanded.
He claims the disobedience is due to the mere absence of the commanded act.
3
Follow the objection
The objector argues that if the person does nothing, he has neither performed the commanded act nor abandoned it (since abandonment is an act). Therefore, he cannot be classified as obedient or disobedient.
The key point: abandonment (ترك) is itself an action and distinct from mere non-performance.
4
See the implication
The objector concludes that Abu Hashim's view implies that Allah could punish someone eternally without any action, which contradicts the consensus of the community and even the Mu'tazilites' own condemnation of the Sunnis.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a person can be sinful for merely not doing what is commanded, even if they do not actively abandon it.
Correction: The objection shows that sin requires an action; the mere absence of a commanded act is not itself an action, so it cannot incur punishment.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why can the person who does nothing be considered neither obedient nor disobedient?
A) Because he has no power.
B) Because he neither performed the commanded act nor committed the forbidden act of abandoning it.
C) Because his action is involuntary.
D) Because he is not commanded.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's view that a person who does nothing can be punished leads to the absurd conclusion of punishment without any action, which is rejected by Islamic consensus.

?
When you fail to do something you know Allah has commanded, do you consider yourself guilty of disobedience? Does this passage change how you view that guilt?
?
Have you ever excused your inaction by saying 'I didn't do anything wrong'? Based on this lesson, is that a valid excuse?
?
If you truly believe that punishment is only for actions, how does that affect your daily choices to actively obey or disobey? Are you more mindful of your actions now?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
Proof that a Single Power Cannot Produce Two Opposite Acts
The author enumerates proofs that a single power is specific to one act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single power is specific to one act and how the author proves this through logical necessity and empirical observation.

Core Idea
Each power is uniquely tied to its act; it cannot produce its opposite.

This passage forms the heart of the author’s argument against the Mu tazilite claim that a single power can produce two opposite acts. Through a series of logical and empirical proofs, he shows that the very concept of a power necessitates it being for one specific act. As you read, notice how he moves from abstract reasoning to concrete examples from everyday life.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening logical argument
Start with the first sentence: 'With protection from error, the heart does not rest at existence.' This lays the foundation: power cannot cause both protection and forsaking.
This is a very dense statement; the commentary will unpack it.
2
Examine the two explicit arguments
The author gives two distinct logical arguments: (1) If a disbeliever had the power for both faith and disbelief, the distinction between them would be arbitrary. (2) Combining two opposites at the moment of power is impossible, and power does not persist over time.
3
Review the empirical examples
The author provides several everyday examples: a sick person descending a ladder but not ascending, a child crawling down but not up, a healthy person descending a well but not climbing out, etc. Each shows that a single power is limited to one type of movement.
These examples are meant to be intuitive and universally accessible.
4
Connect to the overall argument
After the examples, the author concludes that a single power cannot be for both opposites. Reflect on how this supports the Sunni position against the Mu tazilites.
The last sentence of this passage says they omit other arguments, so this is not exhaustive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالعصمة زيغ لم يطمئن القلب عند الوجود، فثبت أن قوة كل نوع غير قوة النوع الآخر، ولأن أحدًا لا يطلق القول بأن الكافر معصوم وأن المؤمن مخذول، ولو كان مع كل واحد منهما ما يصلح للأمرين جميعًا لم يكن أحدهما بالشهادة له بالخذلان والآخر بالشهادة له بالتوفيق والعصمة أولى من القلب أو يوصل كل واحد منهما بأنه موفق مخذول وذلك باطل، ولأن الجمع بين الضدين حال وجود القدرة غير ممكن، وهي لا تبقى ليفعل بها ضدًا في وقت والضد الآخر في وقت آخر، فلا يتصور أن تكون قدرة لهما بل كانت قدرة لما وقع بها وكذلك الزمن والعليل والطفل يقدرون على الزحف من الجبل مع العجز عن الارتقاء فيه وكذا العليل ينحدر من السلم على جهد واستمساك ولا يقدر على الارتقاء فيه وكذلك الصحيح ينزل البئر بالجبل ولا يصح منه الارتقاء عنه، وكذا الحي على تفريق أجزاء نفسه ولا يقدر على جمعها، ويقدر على السبب الذي يحدث عقيبه الانكسار من الزجاج وغيره من الأجسام، ولا يقدر على السبب الذي يحدث عقيبه الانحيار، وسوى هذا لهم حجج أخر أعرضنا عن ذكرها.

ثم إن المذهب الأول يندفع عنه هذا التشنيع الذي تتمسك به المعتزلة وهو نسبة قولنا: إن الاستطاعة الفعل إلى القول بتكليف ما لا يطاق، وأما على مع المذهب الثاني وهو مذهب من يقول : إن القدرة لا تصلح للضدين. فقد اختلفت عباراتهم في دفع هذا السؤال
With protection from error, the heart does not rest at existence. So it is established that the power of each type is different from the power of another type. And because no one says that the disbeliever is protected from error and that the believer is forsaken. If each of them possessed that which is suitable for both matters, then one would not be more deserving of being testified as forsaken and the other as being testified as guided and protected, than the other, or each one would be described as both guided and forsaken, and that is false. And because combining two opposites while power exists is impossible, and it (power) does not persist so that one opposite could be done with it at one time and the other opposite at another time. So it is inconceivable that it be a power for both; rather, it would be a power for that with which it occurs. Likewise, the chronically ill, the sick, and the child can crawl down from a mountain while being unable to ascend it. Likewise, the sick person descends from a ladder with effort and clinging and cannot ascend it. Likewise, the healthy person descends a well with a rope and cannot ascend from it. Likewise, the living can separate the parts of his own self but cannot bring them together. And he can cause that which results in the breaking of glass and other bodies, but he cannot cause that which results in its collapse. Besides this, they have other arguments which we have omitted.

Then, the first doctrine is refuted from this accusation which the Mu'tazila hold onto, which is attributing our statement that ability is the act to the statement of burdening with what cannot be borne. As for the second doctrine, which is the doctrine of those who say that power is not suitable for both opposites, their statements have differed in repelling this question.
Source pages p. 839

Commentary
This passage is a proof that a single power (qudra) cannot be attached to two opposite acts. The author first states the conclusion: the power of each type is distinct. Then he gives multiple arguments: (1) No one describes a disbeliever as protected (ma'sum) and a believer as forsaken (makhdhul). If one power could produce both guidance and forsaking, then the distinction would be arbitrary. (2) Combining two opposites at the moment of power's existence is impossible, and power does not persist over time to be used for one opposite then another. (3) Empirical examples: a chronically ill person can slide down a mountain but not climb; a sick person can descend a ladder but not ascend; a healthy person can descend a well but not climb out; a living being can separate its limbs but not reunite them; one can cause breaking but not the opposite (fixing). These show that each power is specific to one type of act. The author then notes that this proof refutes the Mu'tazilite charge that affirming power for only one act leads to the doctrine of burdening with the impossible (taklif ma la yutaq). Then he introduces the second doctrine (the Sunni view that power is not for opposites) and says that its proponents have given different responses to this question, setting up the next passage.

Key Terms
قدرة
ضدان
استطاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power can be used for multiple acts over time because it persists.
Correction: The author argues that power does not persist; it is present only at the moment of action and is specific to that act.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT an example given to show that a single power cannot perform two opposite acts?
A) A sick person can descend a ladder but not ascend it.
B) A child can crawl down a mountain but not climb up.
C) A healthy person can both descend and ascend a well.
D) A living being can separate its limbs but not reunite them.

Key Takeaway
A single power is intrinsically specific to one act and cannot be used for its opposite, as shown by logical necessity and empirical evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You can cause harm but often cannot prevent it, just as the sick can descend but not ascend. In your spiritual life, where have you relied on your own power to climb but found only the ability to fall? Will you today ask the One who grants ascent for the power only He can give?
?
The proof shows that power does not persist. Think of a past good deed you performed. Do you secretly assume that you can repeat it whenever you want, based on your own ability? How does this lesson challenge that assumption and redirect your dependence to Allah?
?
The passage argues that it is impossible to combine opposites—you cannot be both guided and forsaken. Is there an area in your life where you are trying to serve two masters, expecting one power to produce both obedience and worldly pleasure? What opposite must you renounce today to wholeheartedly seek guidance?

4 min
Refuting the Mu'tazilite Charge: Power and Burdening with the Impossible
The author explains how the proof refutes the Mu'tazilite accusation and notes differing responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Sunni position that power is specific to one act refutes the Mu'tazilite charge of taklif ma la yutaq, and why the second doctrine's proponents differed in their responses.

Core Idea
The specificity of power disproves the accusation of burdening with the impossible.

In the previous passage, the author proved that a single power cannot produce two opposites. Now he addresses a common Mu tazilite objection: that this view leads to the doctrine of burdening with what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq). He briefly explains why the first (Sunni) doctrine avoids this charge, and notes that proponents of the second doctrine gave varied answers. This passage sets up a more detailed discussion that follows.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite accusation
The Mu tazila claimed that if power is only for one act, then God would be commanding an impossible act (like belief for a disbeliever who lacks the power for it). This is the charge of taklif ma la yutaq.
This charge is based on the assumption that ability (istita a) is the act itself.
2
See how the first doctrine refutes it
The author states that the first doctrine (Sunni view) is refuted by this passage’s own argument. The specificity of power means that the act is always possible for the one who has the power, so no impossible burdening occurs.
The key is that power is simultaneous with the act, so if the act occurs, power was present.
3
Note the difference among second doctrine proponents
The author mentions that those who hold the second doctrine (power is not for opposites) gave different responses to this question. This hints at diversity within theological schools.
The second doctrine is likely the Sunni view that power is specific, but some of its adherents expressed it differently.
4
Prepare for the next discussion
The passage ends by indicating that the responses of the second group will be explained. This lesson is a bridge to the subsequent analysis.
You may want to compare the two doctrines in your mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالعصمة زيغ لم يطمئن القلب عند الوجود، فثبت أن قوة كل نوع غير قوة النوع الآخر، ولأن أحدًا لا يطلق القول بأن الكافر معصوم وأن المؤمن مخذول، ولو كان مع كل واحد منهما ما يصلح للأمرين جميعًا لم يكن أحدهما بالشهادة له بالخذلان والآخر بالشهادة له بالتوفيق والعصمة أولى من القلب أو يوصل كل واحد منهما بأنه موفق مخذول وذلك باطل، ولأن الجمع بين الضدين حال وجود القدرة غير ممكن، وهي لا تبقى ليفعل بها ضدًا في وقت والضد الآخر في وقت آخر، فلا يتصور أن تكون قدرة لهما بل كانت قدرة لما وقع بها وكذلك الزمن والعليل والطفل يقدرون على الزحف من الجبل مع العجز عن الارتقاء فيه وكذا العليل ينحدر من السلم على جهد واستمساك ولا يقدر على الارتقاء فيه وكذلك الصحيح ينزل البئر بالجبل ولا يصح منه الارتقاء عنه، وكذا الحي على تفريق أجزاء نفسه ولا يقدر على جمعها، ويقدر على السبب الذي يحدث عقيبه الانكسار من الزجاج وغيره من الأجسام، ولا يقدر على السبب الذي يحدث عقيبه الانحيار، وسوى هذا لهم حجج أخر أعرضنا عن ذكرها.

ثم إن المذهب الأول يندفع عنه هذا التشنيع الذي تتمسك به المعتزلة وهو نسبة قولنا: إن الاستطاعة الفعل إلى القول بتكليف ما لا يطاق، وأما على مع المذهب الثاني وهو مذهب من يقول : إن القدرة لا تصلح للضدين. فقد اختلفت عباراتهم في دفع هذا السؤال
With protection from error, the heart does not rest at existence. So it is established that the power of each type is different from the power of another type. And because no one says that the disbeliever is protected from error and that the believer is forsaken. If each of them possessed that which is suitable for both matters, then one would not be more deserving of being testified as forsaken and the other as being testified as guided and protected, than the other, or each one would be described as both guided and forsaken, and that is false. And because combining two opposites while power exists is impossible, and it (power) does not persist so that one opposite could be done with it at one time and the other opposite at another time. So it is inconceivable that it be a power for both; rather, it would be a power for that with which it occurs. Likewise, the chronically ill, the sick, and the child can crawl down from a mountain while being unable to ascend it. Likewise, the sick person descends from a ladder with effort and clinging and cannot ascend it. Likewise, the healthy person descends a well with a rope and cannot ascend from it. Likewise, the living can separate the parts of his own self but cannot bring them together. And he can cause that which results in the breaking of glass and other bodies, but he cannot cause that which results in its collapse. Besides this, they have other arguments which we have omitted.

Then, the first doctrine is refuted from this accusation which the Mu'tazila hold onto, which is attributing our statement that ability is the act to the statement of burdening with what cannot be borne. As for the second doctrine, which is the doctrine of those who say that power is not suitable for both opposites, their statements have differed in repelling this question.
Source pages p. 839

Commentary
This passage is a proof that a single power (qudra) cannot be attached to two opposite acts. The author first states the conclusion: the power of each type is distinct. Then he gives multiple arguments: (1) No one describes a disbeliever as protected (ma'sum) and a believer as forsaken (makhdhul). If one power could produce both guidance and forsaking, then the distinction would be arbitrary. (2) Combining two opposites at the moment of power's existence is impossible, and power does not persist over time to be used for one opposite then another. (3) Empirical examples: a chronically ill person can slide down a mountain but not climb; a sick person can descend a ladder but not ascend; a healthy person can descend a well but not climb out; a living being can separate its limbs but not reunite them; one can cause breaking but not the opposite (fixing). These show that each power is specific to one type of act. The author then notes that this proof refutes the Mu'tazilite charge that affirming power for only one act leads to the doctrine of burdening with the impossible (taklif ma la yutaq). Then he introduces the second doctrine (the Sunni view that power is not for opposites) and says that its proponents have given different responses to this question, setting up the next passage.

معتزلة

C) That power can produce opposites.
D) That God's power is limited.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni proof that power is specific to one act successfully refutes the Mu tazilite charge of burdening with the impossible, though different scholars have expressed the response in varied ways.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that a commandment of Allah was beyond your ability, leading you to doubt His justice? How does the truth that His power is specific to each act—and that He does not burden a soul beyond its capacity—change your perspective on that struggle? Will you now seek His help more earnestly, trusting that He only commands what He gives you the power to fulfill?
?
When you fail to obey, do you tend to blame your circumstances or yourself? This lesson shows that our power is limited and specific; how does that shape your responsibility? Are you willing to accept that your inability is real but that you are still accountable—and to turn that realization into desperate dua rather than excuse?
?
The Mu'tazila accused the Sunnis of making God unjust, yet they were mistaken. Consider a moment when you accused Allah—in your heart—of being unfair. Knowing that His wisdom far exceeds your understanding, will you repent from that assumption and surrender to His decree, without demanding that He conform to your logic?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
10 min
Ash'ari and Isfarayini on Taklif Ma La Yutaq
Two Sunni theologians on burdening with the impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two major Sunni positions on whether God can command the impossible (taklif ma la yutaq) and the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
God's command over the impossible is either permissible by sovereignty or impossible by the nature of command itself.

This lesson presents two contrasting Sunni responses to a key theological question: can God command something that is impossible for a person to do? Al-Ash'ari defends its permissibility based on God's absolute sovereignty, while Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini argues it is impossible because the very purpose of command is undermined by incapacity. Reading their arguments side by side clarifies the deeper principles of divine justice and human responsibility in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the passage first presents al-Ash'ari's view (beginning with 'فذكر الأشعري') and then shifts to Isfarayini's view (beginning with 'وقال أبو إسحاق الأسفرائيني').
The passage is continuous, but the two views are clearly demarcated.
2
Analyze Ash'ari's argument
Identify the key premises of Ash'ari's position: God acts in His kingdom, He is not subject to human needs for benefit or harm, and obedience/disobedience are mere signs of divine decree. Understand why he concludes that taklif ma la yutaq is ja'iz (permissible).
Ash'ari acknowledges the Law only commands possible things, but he sees this as a free choice, not a necessity.
3
Analyze Isfarayini's argument
Examine Isfarayini's reasoning: He rejects ugliness (qubh) as a basis and instead focuses on the meaning of command—entitlement to punishment for violation. Since the impossible act cannot be violated, the command loses its purpose, making it impossible (mustahil).
Isfarayini's view does not limit God's power, but rather the coherence of the concept of command.
4
Compare the two views
Contrast the starting points: Ash'ari emphasizes divine sovereignty, while Isfarayini emphasizes the internal logic of command. Notice how both remain within Sunni orthodoxy but differ on a key detail.
This comparison deepens understanding of the diversity within classical kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فذكر الأشعري في كتابه المسمى بالنوادر أن تكليف ما لا يطاق جائز، وأن الله تعالى لو أمر عبده بالجمع بين الضدين لم يكن سفها ولا مستحيلاً، وهذا على أصله مستقيم فإن من أصله أن الله تعالى لو عذب الخلق في النيران خالدًا مخلدًا من غير جناية وجدت منهم كان ذلك حكمة وصوابًا؛ لأنه يتصرف في ملكه وكون تكليف ما لا يطاق في الشاهد إنما كان قبيحا لأن المكلف لا يتصرف في ملك نفسه، ولأن المكلف في الشاهد يأمر لجلب نفع وينهى لدفع ضرر، ولا يحصل ذلك بتكليف ما ليس في الوسع بخلاف الصانع جل جلاله فإنه يجل عن جلب نفع أو دفع ضرر، ولا استحقاق من العبد لا للجنة ولا للنار بفعله وإنما الطاعة والمعصية أمارتان يستدل بهما، أن الله تعالى يعذب هذا في النار ويدخل ذلك الجنة، فعلى هذا لو كلف العبد ما لا يطاق ويجعل ذلك أمارة أنه معاقب كان ذلك جائزاً، وإذا كان كذلك كان تكليف العبد ما ليست له قدرة عليه لاشتغاله بضده جائزاً غير أن الشرع ورد بتكليف هذا النوع، ولم يرد بتكليف ذلك النوع مع استوائهما في الجواز.

وقال أبو إسحاق الأسفرائيني: قال أهل الحق يستحيل تكليف ما لا يطاق لاستحالة وجود المعنى الذي يقتضيه التكليف مع العجز لا للقبح والسفه، وهذا لأن الذي يقتضيه التكليف استحقاق نوع من العقوبة على ضرب من المخالفة، وهذا
Al-Ash'ari mentioned in his book called al-Nawadir that burdening with what cannot be borne is permissible, and that if God commanded His servant to combine two opposites, it would not be foolishness nor impossible. And this is consistent with his principle, for it is part of his principle that if God were to torment creation in the fires eternally and forever without any sin found from them, that would be wisdom and correct; because He acts in His kingdom. And the fact that burdening with what cannot be borne is ugly in the witnessed world is only because the one commanded cannot act in his own soul, and because the commander in the witnessed world commands to gain benefit and forbids to repel harm, and that is not achieved by burdening with what is beyond capacity, unlike the Maker, exalted be His majesty, for He is far above gaining benefit or repelling harm. And there is no entitlement for the servant, neither to Paradise nor to Hell, by his action. Rather, obedience and disobedience are signs by which it is inferred that God will torment this one in Hell and admit that one to Paradise. According to this, if He burdened the servant with what he cannot bear and made that a sign that he is punished, that would be permissible. And if that is so, then burdening the servant with what he has no power over due to being occupied with its opposite is permissible, except that the Law came with burdening this type (i.e., the possible) and did not come with burdening that type (i.e., the impossible), while they are equal in permissibility.

And Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini said: The people of truth (ahl al-haqq) hold that it is impossible to burden with what cannot be borne because the meaning that the command necessitates cannot exist with incapacity, not because of ugliness or foolishness. And that is because what the command necessitates is the entitlement to a type of punishment for a type of violation, and this...
Source pages p. 840

Commentary
This passage presents two Sunni responses to the question of taklif ma la yutaq (burdening with the impossible). Al-Ash'ari argues that it is permissible (ja'iz) based on divine sovereignty: God owns His creation and can command anything; the ugliness in human commands is due to the human's lack of ownership and need for benefit, but God is free from such needs. He also notes that obedience and disobedience are merely signs of God's decree, so if God makes an impossible command a sign of punishment, it is valid. However, he acknowledges that the Law (shar') only came with possible commands, not out of necessity but by divine choice. Then Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini gives a different view: that taklif ma la yutaq is impossible (mustahil) not because of ugliness but because the very meaning of command (the entitlement to punishment for violation) cannot exist when the person is incapable. If the commanded act is impossible, then no violation can occur, so the command loses its purpose. Thus, according to Isfarayini, the impossibility is due to the absence of the required relation, not due to any restriction on God's power.

Key Terms
تكليف ما لا يطاق
أشعري
أسفرائيني
أهل الحق
جائز
مستحيل
قبح
سفه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ash'ari's view implies that God acts arbitrarily or without wisdom.
Correction: Ash'ari holds that God's actions are not subject to external criteria of good and evil; they are wise simply because He does them. His sovereignty means He can command anything, and any command is just and wise by virtue of being His.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, why is taklif ma la yutaq impossible?
A) Because it is ugly and foolish.
B) Because the command's meaning (entitlement to punishment) cannot exist with incapacity.
C) Because God cannot command the impossible.
D) Because it would contradict the Law.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility or impossibility of commanding the impossible hinges on whether one prioritizes God's absolute sovereignty (Ash'ari) or the internal logic of command (Isfarayini).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command of Allah that feels impossible for you, do you secretly question His justice? How does Ash'ari's teaching that God's commands are signs of His decree – not based on your ability – reshape your trust in His wisdom?
?
Isfarayini insists that a command to the impossible is meaningless because it cannot result in punishment. Consider an area of your life where you have given up on a religious duty, telling yourself 'I can't.' Are you using inability as an excuse, or are you truly incapable? What would change if you treated that command as possible and strove with the power Allah gives?
?
Both scholars agree that God does not need your obedience. If obedience is merely a sign of God's decree, not something that benefits Him, then why do you obey? Is your devotion motivated by love for the One who commands, or only by fear of punishment? How might this lesson purge your intention of selfishness?

The Objection and the Causal Principle
Objection and initial response stating causal simultaneity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that power must precede the act and the Maturidi response that cause and effect are simultaneous.

Core Idea
Cause and effect are simultaneous, not temporally separate.

Theologians object that if power exists when the act exists, it becomes redundant. The Maturidi answer is that power is like a cause, and a cause exists together with its effect. This lesson introduces that principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first sentence of the Arabic passage which states the objection: power is needed to act, so when the act occurs, power is no longer needed.
This objection is based on a temporal view of causation.
2
Identify the response
Read the response: 'قُلنا: قَد مَرَّ أن القُدرَة تَجري مَجرى العِلَل، والعِلَّة مع المَعلول يُوجَدان.' Notice that it asserts coexistence.
3
Reflect on causality
Think about ordinary causes: fire and heat, for example. Are fire and heat simultaneous? Yes, they exist together.
This is a key distinction from Aristotelian efficient causes which are temporally prior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولهم: إن تعلق القدرة بالوجود محال، لأن القدرة يحتاج إليها ليفعل فإذا وجد الفعل استغنى عنها . قلنا: قد مر أن القدرة تجري مجرى العلل والعلة مع المعلول يوجدان كقيام السواد واتصاف المحل بأنه أسود ، ولا يقال إنه لما صار أسود استغنى عن قيام السواد به، وإنما يحتاج إلى قيام السواد به ليصير أسود، بل قيل : يحصلان جميعًا معا، لاستحالة حصول المعلول بعلة معدومة، وكذا العلم بالشيء مع صيرورته معلوماً، وكذا الحركة والموت والحياة.
Their statement: That the attachment of power to existence is impossible, because power is needed to act, so when the act exists, it becomes independent of it. We said: It has already passed that power follows the course of causes, and the cause with the effect both exist together, like the existence of blackness and the attribute of the substrate that it is black. And it is not said that when it became black, it became independent of the existence of blackness in it; rather, it needs the existence of blackness in it to become black. Rather, it is said that both occur together, because of the impossibility of the effect coming from a non-existent cause. Similarly, knowledge of a thing together with its becoming known, and likewise motion, death, and life.
Source pages p. 845

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation regarding whether divine power (qudra) can be simultaneous with the act. The objectors claim that power must precede the act because power is needed to perform the act; once the act exists, power is no longer needed, implying that power cannot coexist with the act. The answer denies this by drawing an analogy: power runs in the manner of causes ('ilal). A cause and its effect exist together simultaneously, just as blackness (suwad) and the substrate being described as black exist together. No one says that once the substrate becomes black, it no longer needs the blackness; rather, it needs blackness to be black. Both occur together (yahsulani jami'an ma'an), because it is impossible for an effect to come from a non-existent cause. The point is that the cause is not temporally prior; it is contemporaneous. The same applies to knowledge and the known, motion and the moving object, death and the dead, life and the living. Thus, power and act are simultaneous. This refutes the objection that power cannot be concurrent with the act.

Key Terms
تعلق القدرة
العلة والمعلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power must be temporally prior to the act because it is needed to cause the act.
Correction: In Maturidi theology, cause and effect are simultaneous; power coexists with its act.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim of the objectors in this passage?
A) Power and act are simultaneous.
B) Power must precede the act because it is needed to act.
C) Power is independent of the act.
D) The act causes power.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi position is that power and act are simultaneous as cause and effect, not temporally separate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you credit yourself as the sole source of that action, forgetful that Allah's power was the concurrent cause sustaining every moment of it? How can you train your heart to feel that Allah's power is present with your action without a gap?
?
Reflect on a sin you committed: Did you think that at that moment Allah's power was withdrawn from you? But the lesson shows that Allah's power was still the cause of your very movement, yet He permitted the sin. How does this truth challenge your excuses and push you to acknowledge your own blameworthiness and His mercy?
?
If Allah's power and your action are simultaneous, then you never act independently of His power—not even for a split second. Will you commit to pausing before your next action—good or bad—and whispering internally, 'By Allah's power, I do this'? What difference do you expect this to make in your day?

Analogies of Simultaneity
Analogies demonstrating simultaneous causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how analogies of blackness, knowledge, motion, death, and life illustrate the simultaneous nature of cause and effect.

Core Idea
Just as blackness and the black substrate coexist, so too do power and act.

The previous lesson established that cause and effect are simultaneous. Now, the author provides concrete analogies from everyday experience to make this principle intuitive.

Guided Path
1
Read the blackness analogy
Read the part about blackness: blackness and the substrate being black occur together. Note that it is impossible for the substrate to become black without blackness.
2
Read the other analogies
Read the analogies for knowledge, motion, death, and life. Each shows a pair that exists simultaneously.
These are all examples of inseparable causal pairs.
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider how these analogies support the idea that cause and effect are coexistent. Why can't the effect exist without the cause at the same moment?
The core argument is that an effect cannot come from a non-existent cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولهم: إن تعلق القدرة بالوجود محال، لأن القدرة يحتاج إليها ليفعل فإذا وجد الفعل استغنى عنها . قلنا: قد مر أن القدرة تجري مجرى العلل والعلة مع المعلول يوجدان كقيام السواد واتصاف المحل بأنه أسود ، ولا يقال إنه لما صار أسود استغنى عن قيام السواد به، وإنما يحتاج إلى قيام السواد به ليصير أسود، بل قيل : يحصلان جميعًا معا، لاستحالة حصول المعلول بعلة معدومة، وكذا العلم بالشيء مع صيرورته معلوماً، وكذا الحركة والموت والحياة.
Their statement: That the attachment of power to existence is impossible, because power is needed to act, so when the act exists, it becomes independent of it. We said: It has already passed that power follows the course of causes, and the cause with the effect both exist together, like the existence of blackness and the attribute of the substrate that it is black. And it is not said that when it became black, it became independent of the existence of blackness in it; rather, it needs the existence of blackness in it to become black. Rather, it is said that both occur together, because of the impossibility of the effect coming from a non-existent cause. Similarly, knowledge of a thing together with its becoming known, and likewise motion, death, and life.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation regarding whether divine power (qudra) can be simultaneous with the act. The objectors claim that power must precede the act because power is needed to perform the act; once the act exists, power is no longer needed, implying that power cannot coexist with the act. The answer denies this by drawing an analogy: power runs in the manner of causes ('ilal). A cause and its effect exist together simultaneously, just as blackness (suwad) and the substrate being described as black exist together. No one says that once the substrate becomes black, it no longer needs the blackness; rather, it needs blackness to be black. The same applies to knowledge and the known, motion and the moving object, death and the dead, life and the living. Thus, power and act are simultaneous. This refutes the objection that power cannot be concurrent with the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would change today if you truly believed He is present in the very moment you act?
?
The passage says cause and effect cannot be separated. Are there times when you think your past sin is 'over' and no longer affects you? How does the simultaneity of cause and effect challenge you to take responsibility for what you are becoming right now?
?
If life and the living being are simultaneous, then your very existence is sustained by God at every instant. What small habit could you adopt today to remind yourself of this continuous, inseparable gift of life?

Refuting the Impossibility of Simultaneous Power and Act
Nasafi refutes the impossibility of simultaneous power and act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why simultaneity of divine power and act does not undermine causality, and how causal priority in rank (al-rutba) resolves the objection.

Core Idea
Simultaneity of cause and effect is common; priority is in rank, not time.

This passage addresses a central objection in kalam about divine power and its act: if power and act exist simultaneously, how can one be the cause of the other? Nasafi responds with logical distinctions and everyday examples that show simultaneity does not negate causal priority. He introduces the concept of precedence in rank (al-rutba) to explain how cause and effect can coexist without temporal gap.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the two-part structure: the objection and the response with examples.
Pay attention to the logical progression.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Notice how the objection frames the problem: does power relate to a past existent or a future one? Nasafi addresses both.
The first possibility is dismissed because accidents don't persist; the second is accepted with simultaneity.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the list of simultaneous phenomena (blackness with being black, motion with moving, etc.) and understand how they demonstrate causal simultaneity.
These examples show that cause and effect often coexist.
4
Understand precedence in rank
Focus on the philosophers' statement: cause precedes effect in rank, not time. This is key to resolving the objection.
If cause preceded in time, it would be nonexistent when effect occurs, which is impossible.
5
Refute mutual conditionality
See how the opponent's scenario of mutual conditionality leads to impossibility, while simultaneous existence avoids it.
The conditioned cannot precede the condition, so mutual conditionality is circular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم: إن تعلق القدرة يستحيل بأي موجود؟ بموجود وقع عنه الفراغ؟ أم بموجود لم يقع عنه الفراغ بعد؟ فإن قال بالأول، فهو مسلم غير أن الأعراض ليس لها حالة الفراغ إذ هي الحالة الثانية في حالة الوجود، وهي لا تبقى فتنعدم في تلك الحالة، وإن قال بالثاني فهو غير مسلّم أن القدرة لا تتعلق به ويوجدان معا على ما مر، وهذا هو الجواب عن قولهم: أن القدرة لو كانت توجد مع الفعل لم يكن إضافة الفعل إلى القدرة أولى من إضافة القدرة إلى الفعل فإن السواد باتصاف المحل بكونه أسود، وكذا الحركة مع المتحرك والموت مع الميت والحياة مع الحي والعصا اتصافها وخروج من اليد مع الإلقاء، وطلوع الشمس مع وجود النهار كل ذلك يوجدان معاً ولا تخفى العلة والمعلول، ويعرف ببديهة العقل حصول أحدهما بالآخر، واستحالة إضافة العلة إلى الحكم، ولهذا قالت الفلاسفة: العلة متقدمة على المعلول لا تقدماً زمنيًا بل تقدمًا من حيث الرتبة، وحقيقة الكلام ما مر أن حصول العلة في هذه الفصول لو تقدمت لانعدمت وقت حصول المعلول، وحصوله بعلة معدومة غير متصور فلابد من حصولهما معاً، فكذا فيما نحن فيه. وبهذا يبطل ما زعموا أن الفعل لو لم يوجد حتى تحصل القدرة ولن تحصل هي حتى يوجد الفعل لا يتصور وجودهما كما ضرب من المثال، فإن الاتصاف بالسواد مع وجود السواد يوجدان معا ولا يتصور وجود أحدهما بدون ذلك يوجدان، وإنما ذلك فيما يتعلق كل واحد منهما بصاحبه تعلق ومع المشروط بالشرط، فيكون وجود هذا شرطًا لوجود ذلك ووجود ذلك شرطًا لوجود هذا، ووجود المشروط يكون بعد وجود الشرط فيصير شرط وجود كل واحد منهما تقدم صاحبه عليه وهذا محال. فأما فيما يوجدان معا فعليه أكثر الأمور.
Then we say to them: Is the attachment of power (taʿalluq al-qudra) impossible with any existing thing? With an existing thing from which one has become free? Or with an existing thing from which one has not yet become free? If they say the first, it is granted, except that accidents have no state of being free from them because they are in a second state in the state of existence, and they do not subsist, so they become non-existent in that state. If they say the second, it is not granted that power does not attach to it; rather they exist together (yūjadāni maʿan) as has preceded. This is the answer to their statement that if power existed with the act, then attributing the act to power would not be more appropriate than attributing power to the act. For blackness is by the locus being described as black, and likewise motion with the moving, death with the dead, life with the living, and the stick being described and its leaving the hand with the throwing, and the rising of the sun with the existence of daytime—all these exist together (yūjadāni maʿan) and the cause and effect are not hidden. It is known by the intuition of the intellect that one is obtained by the other, and it is impossible to attribute the cause to the effect. For this reason the philosophers said: The cause precedes the effect not by temporal precedence but by precedence in rank (al-rutba). The reality of the statement is as has passed: that the occurrence of the cause in these matters, if it preceded, would become non-existent at the time of the occurrence of the effect, and its occurrence by a non-existent cause is inconceivable, so they must exist together. Thus it is in our case. And by this is invalidated what they claimed: that if the act does not exist until power occurs and power does not occur until the act exists, their existence is inconceivable, as in the example they gave. For being described with blackness with the existence of blackness—they exist together, and the existence of one without that [other] is inconceivable. Rather that [would be] in the case where each of them is related to the other as the conditioned is related to the condition, so that the existence of this is a condition for the existence of that and the existence of that is a condition for the existence of this, and the existence of the conditioned is after the existence of the condition, so then the condition of existence of each of them would be the precedence of its companion over it, and this is impossible. As for what exists together, most things are upon that.
Source pages p. 846

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that if power and act exist simultaneously, then attributing the act to power is arbitrary. Nasafi treats the objection as a question: does power relate to an existent that is past or future? If past, accidents do not persist; if future, it is not granted that power does not relate to it; they coexist. Then he gives examples: blackness with the state of being black, motion with the moving, death with the dead, life with the living, the stick and its leaving the hand with the throwing, sunrise with daytime—all simultaneous and the causal relation is clear. Philosophers say cause precedes effect in rank (al-rutba), not time. If the cause preceded in time, it would be non-existent when the effect occurs, which is impossible. This refutes the claim that if act does not exist until power exists and power does not exist until act exists, then neither can exist. That would involve mutual conditionality, which is impossible. But in many cases, things exist simultaneously.

Key Terms
تعلق القدرة
علة
معلول
شرط
مشروط
رتبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if cause and effect are simultaneous, there is no causal priority.
Correction: The passage explains that causality is preserved by priority in rank (al-rutba), not time.

Checkpoint
What do the philosophers say about the relationship between cause and effect?
A) Cause always precedes effect in time
B) Effect precedes cause
C) Cause precedes effect in rank not time
D) They are simultaneous in every way

Key Takeaway
Simultaneity of cause and effect does not negate causality; priority in rank (al-rutba) preserves the causal relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see two events occurring together in your life (e.g., your effort and success), do you instinctively assume that one must come before the other temporally? How might this lesson about rank priority change the way you thank Allah for blessings that appear simultaneous with your actions? Can you identify a specific situation where you felt pride in being the 'cause' of something, forgetting that the true cause is Allah, and that your action was merely simultaneous in rank?
?
The philosophers say cause precedes effect in rank, not time. What does this mean for your understanding of your own free will? If your choice and Allah's creation of your choice exist together, do you still feel morally responsible? How does this challenge your assumption that responsibility requires temporal separation?
?
Nasafi gives examples where cause and effect are obviously simultaneous (sunrise and daytime). In your spiritual life, can you think of a moment when you experienced the 'sunrise' of guidance and the 'daytime' of faith as one event? How does recognizing their simultaneity affect your gratitude and your sense of closeness to Allah?

The Mu'tazila Tripartite Division of Action Creation
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila tripartite division and logical elimination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila threefold classification of how human actions are created and the reasoning by which they affirm human independent creation.

Core Idea
Actions must be created by humans alone to avoid attributing evil to God.

The Mu'tazila, in defending human free will and divine justice, developed a precise logical division of how a human action can come into existence. By systematically examining three possibilities and eliminating the first two, they concluded that humans create their own actions independently. This reasoning, presented here by Nasafi, is a cornerstone of Mu'tazila theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once for overall meaning, then consult the translation. Notice the method of induction (istiqra') and division (taqsim).
The poem by al-Nashi is not included in the excerpt, but its purpose is to versify this reasoning.
2
Identify the three divisions
Find the three possibilities: (1) God alone creates, (2) God and human create together, (3) human alone creates. Note the key terms: استقلال, استبداد.
3
Understand the rejection of the first two
The first division is rejected because praise and blame would revert to God. The second is rejected because blame still goes to the human but God would also be implicated. Follow the logic of 'la budda' (it is necessary).
The argument hinges on the principle that blame for evil cannot attach to God.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Only the third division remains: the servant creates independently. This is the Mu'tazila position on human agency.
This conclusion informs their doctrine of divine justice and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقرروا هذا الكلام وأبرزوه على طريقة الاستقراء واشتغلوا بالتقسيم وقالوا فعل العبد لا يخلو إما أن يكون كله من الله تعالى تولى إيجاده بلا صنع من العبد وإما أن يكون العبد أوجده أيضا مع الله تعالى فوجد بإيجاد الله تعالى وإيجاد العبد، وقد شارك العبد الله تعالى في إيجاده، وإما أن كان العبد هو الذي تولى الإيجاد على سبيل الانفراد والاستقلال والاستبداد. والقسم الأول فاسد لعود المدح والذم على هذه الأفعال إلى العبد دون الله ولو كان الله تعالى هو الموجد له لرجع إليه الذم والمدح والقسم الثاني أيضا باطل لعود كل الذم على ذلك إلى العبد دون الله تعالى، ولو كان الأمر على ذلك لم يكن العبد بأولى به من الله تعالى فتعين القسم الثالث. ونظم الناشي هذا المعنى فقال :
And they formulated this statement and presented it in a method of induction and engaged in division, saying: The action of the servant is either entirely from Allah Almighty, He undertakes its existence without any acquisition from the servant, or the servant also brings it into existence along with Allah Almighty, so it comes into existence through the existence of Allah Almighty and the existence of the servant, and the servant has participated with Allah Almighty in its existence, or the servant is the one who undertakes the existence on the basis of independence, autonomy, and exclusivity. The first division is invalid because praise and blame for these actions revert to the servant, not to Allah. If Allah Almighty were the one who brings it into existence, then blame and praise would revert to Him. The second division is also invalid because all blame for that reverts to the servant, not to Allah Almighty. And if the matter were so, the servant would not be more entitled to it than Allah Almighty. So the third division is established. Al-Nashi versified this meaning and said:
Source pages p. 855

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's tripartite division of how a human action can be created. They propose three possibilities: (1) God creates the action entirely without any human acquisition, (2) both God and the human create it together, (3) the human creates it independently. The first is rejected because praise and blame for actions are directed to humans, not God, so if God were the sole creator, blame for evil actions would fall on God. The second is rejected because if both share in creation, then the human is still blamed for the evil action, but God would also be blameworthy, which is impossible. Thus only the third option remains: humans create their own actions independently. The poet al-Nashi then put this reasoning into verse, though the verse itself is not included in this excerpt.

Key Terms
تقسيم
إيجاد
استقلال
استبداد
الناشي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila deny any role for God in human actions altogether.
Correction: The passage shows they only deny that God directly creates human actions; they affirm that God creates the human and their power to act, but the action itself is produced by the human.

Checkpoint
Why do the Mu'tazila reject the first division (God alone creates actions)?
A) Because it would make humans powerless
B) Because praise and blame for actions would revert to God
C) Because it contradicts the Qur'an
D) Because it implies polytheism

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use a tripartite division and elimination to conclude that humans independently create their own actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever find yourself thinking, 'God made me do it' or 'It was my destiny'? How does the Mu'tazila's elimination of the first two divisions expose your tendency to shift blame away from yourself? What would it cost you to own that sin fully as your own, without any excuse?
?
If you truly believed that every action you do is created entirely by you alone, would it change how you approach repentance? Would you be more or less likely to ask God for help in changing your behavior? Why?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that blame cannot fall on God, so humans must be independent creators of their actions. But do you live as if your actions are independent, or do you secretly rely on God's grace to cover for your failures? When was the last time you thanked God not for creating your good deed, but for enabling you to do it?

The Mu'tazilite Argument from Witnessed Impossibility
Mu'tazilite argument using analogy to deny two agents for one act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that one act cannot be under two powers because the same logical rules apply in the witnessed and unseen worlds.

Core Idea
What is impossible in the physical world cannot become possible in the unseen.

The Mu'tazilites use a strong analogy to argue against the idea that both God and humans can be agents of the same act. They insist that logical principles are the same in the witnessed and unseen worlds. This passage presents their reasoning and warns that departing from it violates reason and intuition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a premise, a principle, an analogy, and a conclusion.
The argument is concise and relies on the reader's acceptance of the premise.
2
Identify the analogy
The Mu'tazilites compare the impossibility of one act under two powers to the impossibility of combining black and white. Reflect on why this is a strong analogy in their view.
They claim both cases are self-evidently impossible in the witnessed world.
3
Understand the uniformity principle
The Mu'tazilites assert that what is possible or impossible does not change between the witnessed and unseen worlds. This principle is crucial for their argument.
The Ash'arites later challenge this principle by distinguishing divine from human power.
4
Recognize the charge
The passage concludes that anyone who claims otherwise departs from reason, invalidates proofs, and violates intuitive knowledge. See the severity of the accusation.
This shows how central this issue is to Mu'tazilite thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: وكذا دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين في الشاهد، ولا يختلف الشاهد والغائب في الجائز والممتنع كما في الجمع بين السواد والبياض، فصار مدعي إمكان دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين في الغائب كونه ممتنعا في الشاهد كمدعي إمكان الجمع بين السواد والبياض والحركة والسكون في الغائب، مع كون ذلك مستحيلاً في الشاهد، وهذا خروج عن قضية المعقول، وإبطال الدلائل ونقض البداهة.
They said: Similarly, the inclusion of a single object of power under the power of two agents in the witnessed world [is impossible]. And there is no difference between the witnessed and the unseen regarding what is possible and what is impossible, as in the combination of black and white. So the one who claims the possibility of a single object of power under the power of two agents in the unseen, while it is impossible in the witnessed, is like the one who claims the possibility of combining black and white, or motion and rest, in the unseen, while that is impossible in the witnessed. This is a departure from the ruling of reason, an invalidation of proofs, and a violation of intuitive knowledge.
Source pages p. 856

Commentary
This passage presents a Mu'tazilite argument based on analogy between the witnessed (physical) world and the unseen (metaphysical) world. In the witnessed world, it is impossible for one object to be under the power of two independent agents simultaneously (e.g., one object cannot be moved by two people without each having full control). The Mu'tazilites assert that logical principles apply uniformly to both worlds. Therefore, claiming that a single human act can be created by both God and the human in the unseen is as absurd as claiming that black and white can coexist in the unseen while being impossible in the witnessed. Such a claim is a departure from reason and violates intuitive knowledge.

Key Terms
مقدور واحد
قدرة قادرين
الشاهد
الغائب
الجمع بين السواد والبياض
الحركة والسكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite analogy proves that one act cannot be under two powers in the unseen as well.
Correction: The analogy assumes that divine power operates like human power, which is not necessarily true. The Ash'arites argue that God's power is qualitatively different and can be simultaneous with human power.

Checkpoint
What analogy do the Mu'tazilites use to argue against one act under two powers?
A) The combination of heat and cold.
B) The combination of black and white.
C) The combination of life and death.
D) The combination of sound and silence.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites argue that what is impossible in the witnessed world cannot become possible in the unseen, using the analogy of combining black and white to assert the impossibility of one act under two powers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazilites argue that the unseen must follow the same rules as the witnessed. Have you ever limited God's ability to act in your life because you assumed He must operate within the constraints you observe in the world? How will you actively challenge that assumption today?
?
If you truly believed that God's power is not bound by physical rules, would you pray differently? Would you hope for the impossible? What specific situation in your life right now reveals that you actually trust the Mu'tazilite logic more than the Ash'arite affirmation of God's absolute power?

6 min
Refutation of One Act Under Two Powers
Nasafi refutes the argument that one act cannot be under two powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's refutation of the claim that a single act can be under both God's and the servant's power, and why this leads to theological absurdity.

Core Idea
One act under two powers leads to shirk or impossibility.

This passage presents Imam Nasafi's decisive refutation of a key Mu'tazili argument. He first exposes the logical trilemma about blame, then demolishes the analogy from the witnessed world, and finally demonstrates that the claim inevitably leads to associating partners with God or to logical contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the trilemma
Understand the three options for blame and why only one is coherent. This sets up the problem of dual agency.
Notice the poetic form of the trilemma.
2
Examine the opponents' argument
They analogize from the witnessed world, claiming the unseen must follow the same rules of possibility.
3
Follow Nasafi's refutation
He shows the analogy fails because it leads to absurdities like combining opposites, which violates reason and intuition.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the consequences
The claim leads to shirk (partners with God), jabr (compulsion), or ta'jiz (incapacitation of God) – all impossible.
These are the three logical outcomes; each is unacceptable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا تعدو أفعالنا التي نذم بها إحدى ثلاث خلال حين نحصيها إما تفرد مولانا بصنعته فيسقط اللوم عنا حين نأتيها أو كان يشركنا فاللوم يلحقه إن كان يلحقنا من لاتم فيها أو لم يكن للإله في خليقته صنع فما الذنب إلا ذنب جانيها
قالوا: وكذا دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين في الشاهد، ولا يختلف الشاهد والغائب في الجائز والممتنع كما في الجمع بين السواد والبياض، فصار مدعي إمكان دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين في الغائب كونه ممتنعا في الشاهد كمدعي إمكان الجمع بين السواد والبياض والحركة والسكون في الغائب، مع كون ذلك مستحيلاً في الشاهد، وهذا خروج عن قضية المعقول، وإبطال الدلائل ونقض البداهة.
وكذا القول به يؤدي إلى جعل العباد شركاء الله تعالى في تحصيل الأفعال، لأنها تحصل بقدرته تعالى وبقدرتهم، والقول بإثبات الشريك الله تعالى في تخليق العالم كفر صراح، وخروج عن التوحيد إلى القول بالتثنية، وكذا القول به يؤدي إلى أن يكون العباد مضطرين في أفعالهم أو يؤدي إلى تعجيز الله تعالى، وكلا الأمرين بين الإحالة ظاهر الفساد، وما أدى إلى ما هو المحال كان محالاً ممتنعا في نفسه.
Our actions that we blame do not exceed three kinds when we enumerate them: either our Master is unique in His making, so blame falls from us when we do them; or He shares with us, so blame attaches to Him if blame attaches to us from the one who has no share in them; or there is no making by God in His creation, so the sin is only the sin of the one who commits it. They said: Likewise, the inclusion of a single object of power under the power of two powerful agents in the witnessed world – and the witnessed and the unseen do not differ in the possible and the impossible, just as with the combination of blackness and whiteness – so the one who claims the possibility of a single object of power under the power of two powerful agents in the unseen, given that it is impossible in the witnessed world, becomes like the one who claims the possibility of combining blackness and whiteness, or motion and rest, in the unseen, despite such being impossible in the witnessed world. This is a departure from the dictate of reason, an invalidation of proofs, and a violation of intuition. Likewise, holding this view leads to making servants partners of God Almighty in producing actions, because they are produced by His power and by their power. And the statement confirming a partner to God Almighty in creating the world is outright disbelief, a departure from monotheism to dualism. Also, holding this view leads either to servants being compelled in their actions or to rendering God Almighty incapable – both matters are obviously absurd and manifestly corrupt, and whatever leads to what is absurd is itself absurd and impossible in itself.
Source pages p. 856

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that a single act cannot fall under two powers simultaneously. It begins with a poetic couplet presenting a trilemma: if God alone creates acts, blame is removed from us; if He shares in them, blame attaches to Him; if He has no part, then sin is solely the sinner's. The opponents then argue by analogy with the witnessed world: in this world, one act cannot be under two agents, and the unseen realm follows the same rules of possibility and impossibility. To claim otherwise is as absurd as claiming blackness and whiteness can coexist. Nasafi then points out that accepting the possibility of one act under two powers leads to shirk (associating partners with God) because it gives servants a share in producing actions alongside God, which is outright disbelief. Alternatively, it entails jabr (compulsion) if God's power overrides, or ta'jiz (incapacitation) of God if the servant's will prevails. Both conclusions are impossible, so the premise is false.

Key Terms
مقدور
قدرة
شاهد
غائب
شرك
توحيد
اضطرار
تعجيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God and the servant can cooperate in creating an act without contradiction.
Correction: Nasafi shows this leads either to shirk (if both contribute) or to jabr/ta'jiz, which are impossible.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi argue is a consequence of claiming that one act is under the power of both God and the servant?
A) It proves human free will.
B) It leads to making servants partners with God in creation, which is disbelief.
C) It is the position of the Jabriyya.
D) It is acceptable in the unseen but not in the witnessed world.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi demonstrates that the claim of one act under two powers leads to either shirk or logical absurdity, thus refuting the Mu'tazili position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever subtly feel that Allah is partly to blame because He created the act and gave you the ability? The trilemma shows that either blame falls entirely on us or on Him—there is no middle ground. How does this shatter any excuse you might make for your sins? Will you now take full responsibility, knowing that your sin is entirely your own choice, while simultaneously acknowledging that Allah creates it?
?
The passage warns that claiming one act under two powers leads either to shirk (making yourself a partner with God) or to jabr (feeling compelled, thus excusing your sin). Which of these two dangers do you lean toward in your heart? Do you ever act as if your desires or circumstances force you to sin? How does the principle that the witnessed and unseen share the same logical rules compel you to reject both extremes and embrace true accountability?
?
If you truly internalize that every action is created by Allah alone—including your obedience and disobedience—how would that change your attitude in worship and repentance? Would you become more humble, knowing you are not the ultimate agent, or more hopeful, knowing Allah is the sole creator of all good? Answer honestly: does this truth make you want to praise Him more or to question His justice? Let the lesson's reasoning settle your heart.
